MUSTDIE
KILLS FLIES
The Red Page
About Maggots
Arthur Symons collects two dozen of his
essays upon books ;ind the makers of books
under the title Figures of Several Centuries
(Constable, per Sydney Book Club). To
the average reader of literature many of
these brilliant studies will be somewhat
familiar; none of them . are really
recent. It is a painful thought that
in a world already overcrowded with
people who write about life, there exist
people who write about other people who
write, and books that are written about other
books. These human beings, once removed, are
kindly called literary critics; their function,
really, is to act as spruikers in front of the
picture-show of literature —and among them
may be dimly discerned the shrinking figure
of the Red Page. We are the parasites of
literature, getting a modest living out of our
unwilling host. And, like all parasites, we
hold fast the conviction that it does litera-
ture good to have parasites: we keep litera-
ture from brooding on the fact that it is
literature. Arthur Symons is a charming
species of literary flea, lie writes lucidly
and with knowledge of liis various hosts:
Saint Augustine and his Confessions, Charles
Lamb, Villon, Casanova, Swinburne, Ros-
setti, Hardy, Ibsen, Baudelaire, Pater, Pat-
more, and the rest of them —mostly dead.
That is the main characteristic of the literary
parasite: an author must be sufficiently dead
before the literary blowfly will browse on
him. And that is why this plague of blow-
flies is superfluous. They go on breeding
critical maggots that do not matter. If any'
young thing yearns to know about
Casanova or Emily Bronte, the only soul-
satisfying way is to borrow Norman Lind-
say’s copy of the former estimable author or
purchase a cheap edition of the latter’s
Wuthering Heights. Doubtless she will be
grateful to Arthur Symons for reminding
her that there are such authors as Casanova
or Emily; but the only way to see a picture-
show is to disregard the spruiker and go
right in. Arthur Symons’ idea of the pic-
ture-show will certainly not be hers —after
she has seen the film. The reputations of
quite dead authors may be left to take care
of themselves. Of course, there remains a
scientific or merely frivolous curiosity on our
part to know exactly what a blowfly thinks
about the dead sheep it is using as a mater-
nity hospital. But for students of literature
that hardly matters. The reputations, or
smells, of dead authors may be left to take
care of themselves. You can always discover
the corpses neatly buried in bookshops or
public libraries.
But where the parasitic critic could be of
some use is in the discovery of live authors,
new, struggling, embyro authors. And here
this particular red-pageish parasite does
attempt to find its proper function. It
makes its eager guesses at what a new Aus-
tralian author means, and it presents that
personal guess for the consideration of its
readers. If it succeeds in attracting atten-
tion to some new Australian with something
to say, it is content. But it doesn’t expect
you to agree with its peculiar views; all It
attempts to say is: “Here’s some new wrig-
gling thing that is worth watching. To me it
appears such-and-such a kind of thing; I
may be quite wrong. Have a look at him
yourself, and judge. At least, you’ll find his
squirmings interesting.” And sometimes the
wriggling insect itself finds it interesting.
Some of these literary parasites perform
a double function: Arthur Symons writes
delightful poems in his parasitic intervals:
Thus it is not fair to judge Rupert Brooke,
who is solely a poet, by his essay on John
Webster and the Elizabethan Drama (Sidg-
wick and Jackson, per Sydney Book Club).
A poet infesting an Elizabethan dramatist is
interesting chiefly because we are interested
in the recently-living parasite and hardly in-
terested at all in his very dead host. Those
of us who are interested in John Webster
can make up our minds about him by read-
ing his chief plays. To ask Rupert Brooke
to make up our mind about Webster is to
ask somebody else to eat our dinner and tell
us whether it was a good dinner or not. So
this volume is valuable only in so far as it
tells us things about an interesting contem-
porary who has left us singularly little
about himself for us to discuss. Thus his
definition of a good Elizabethan play as a
Elay that would have been good in Eliza-
ethan times affords us a new Insight into
the downrightness of Rupert Brooke’s mind.
And his difficulties in appreciating that
drama are ours: “An Elizabethan play is
written in a language somewhat different
from ours, and pronounced differently, too,
and it was performed in conditions we do
not completely know and cannot at all
realise. It was composed for an audience
accustomed to the platform stage and no
scenery; which we can never be. It was
composed for the stage, and we judge it as
literature; we are only readers It Is right
enough to admire their great literary merits
and their rather accidental power as study-
drama. But, after all, we hare only the text
—and that a not always trustworthy ope —
one factor of several in the play, a residue,
fragments of the whole.. We are like men
who possess sweet-smelling shards of a jar
which once held perfumes, and know how
fragrant it must have been; but the jar Is
broken and the perfumes lost.”
And it is pleasant to find this modern
quite free from the literary idolatry with
which our Shakspeare parasites contemplate
the Elizabethans. Brooke mentions that
critics have always idiotically thought it
their duty to praise tlie chronicle-plays,
partly because Shakspeare, in obedience to
popular demand, wrote some; partly because
they are supposed to exemplify the patriot-
ism of the Elizabethans, and we are sup-
posed to enjoy that patriotism. “It must be
clearly decided that these histories were a
transient, dreary, childish kind.” The same
fate lias overtaken the romantic comedies,
though “Shakspeare threw a pink magic over
them.” “The whole stuff of this kind of
play—disguises, sentimentality, girls in boys’
clothes, southern romance —was very thin.”
Wisely Brooke contrasts this class of play
with the crude, but realistic, domestic trag-
edies of that time, dramatisations of recent
occurrences, which profoundly influenced the
Elizabethan tragedy that was to come.
Brooke has some sane words to say about
the shocked modern critics’ pain at “the
depth of gross and vicious realism to which
the comedy of manners descended.” Frankly
Brooke says: “The Elizabethans liked
obscenity... .If literary criticism crosses
Lethe, and we could hear the comments of
the foul-mouthed ghosts of Shakspeare, Mar-
lowe and Webster on this too common atti-
tude, their outspoken uncleanliness would
prostrate these modern critics.” And it is
interesting to hear Webster’s opinion of his
contemporaries. He puts them in this order
of merit: Chapman; Jonson; Beaumont
and Fletcher; Shakspeare, Dekker and Hey-
wood. And for those who prefer to read
Webster rather than to hear what even so
poetic a maggot as Rupert Brooke thinks of
that corpse, this particular parasite remarks:
“A play of Webster’s is full of the feverish
and ghastly turmoil of a nest of maggots.
Maggots are what the inhabitants of this
universe most suggest and resemble
Human beings are writhing grubs in an
immense night. And the night is without
stars or moon. But it has sometimes a cer-
tain quietude in its darkness; but not very
much.”
Drama
Plays of the Natural . and the Super-
natural, by Theodore Dreiser (Lane, New
York, per Dymock’s Library), is an extra-
ordinary collection. Two of these one-act
plays are episodes in the life of the New
York poor, grimly and realistically treated.
The rest read like scenarios for motion-
pictures. In The Blue Sphere the scene
changes nearly every page. The . leading
character is a monstrosity of a child with
a head almost twice the normal size, whose
only speech is "Urg-übbla-da! Blub !” “A
shadow/’ carrying a blue sphere, attempts
to lead the monstrosity down on to the rail-
way track, and in the distance, at intervals
during the scene, the express train is heard
saying to itself, “0000000-ee! Ooh-ook!” as
it approaches. All the efforts of the shadow
with the glorious blue sphere to entice the
child to its death are thwarted by uncon-
scious human agency, until at last the shadow
tempts the child on to the line, and the
express mercifully ends its hopeless life. The
conductor remarks, “Well, it’s God’s blessing
if a child had to be killed it. was a deformed
one, anyway.” It is a horrible nightmare,
yet there is a fine purpose in its construc-
tion and development.
Laughing Gas is a still more impossible
play. It is just the psychical experiences
of a mao under the influence of nitrous
oxide undergoing a minor operation. The
chief character of this weird experience is
“The Rhythm of the Universe,” who says
nothing but “Om! Om! 0m!....” The
operation takes a serious turn, and the
patient’s life is in danger. The struggle by
the surgeon to save his life is paralleled by
the struggle that is represented as going on
in the various higher psychical planes. The
patient’s own thoughts take physical shape
and voice. Yet possibly only by these means
could the author get the realistic and con-
vincing effect he produces.
In the Dark is just a sordid murder, with
wraiths and assorted ghosts and' policemen
and dogs, and with the scene changing with
almost every flicker of the film. The Spring
Recital has more imagination in it. An
organist gives a recital- in a church. The
public does not come, except a pair of
lovers and a few stragglers, but the music
of the organ attracts the dead, the fauns,
the nymphs, the hags and all the other
animal and human spirits. Also a dead
loafer, who- jeers at the spirits of Egyptian
priests and monks and Scotchbyterian min-
isters: “Yon thought you knew somepin’
when you were alive, didn’ j&h? You thought
you were smart, huh? You thought you’d
find out somepin’ when yuh died, huh?
Well, yuh got fooled, didn’ jah? You’re like
all the other stiffs that walk about and
think they know a lot. Yuh got left. Har!
Har! Har! I know as much as you fellew,
and I're only been dead a dozen year*.
There ain’t no answer! Oh, ho, ho!”
Thereupon he grins maliciously and exe-
cutes a crude clog dance. But the next
moment, attracted by the beauty of the
musie—the organist thinking that he is
playing only for four people—troops of
fauns and nymphs and hama-dryads dance
in, “persistences of fish and birds and ani-
mals, attracted by the rhythm which is both
color and harmony to them, turn and weave
among the others,” and the ancient and
newly dead join the glad throng. The scene
ends with the weary organist leaving the
darkened clmrcli, thinking of the nearest
pub, with the young lovers embracing, with
the dead loafer saying gloomily, “Now I
gotta find somepin’ else,” and the church cat
resolutely remarking, “Now for one more
try for that mouse !”
Some day this is just the sort of play
that we shall see at the moving-pictures.
Possibly Theodore Dreisser is subconsciously
feeling for a new dramatic form to fit this
new dramatic medium.
Alfred Sutro lias a right to be annoyed by
the war. He had just finished a new play,
Freedom. (Duckworth, per Dymock’s
Library), when the Kaiser decided to stage
a drama of his own. For Freedom deals
exclusively with the sex question, and in
the way the sex question was treated in those
forgotten ante-bellum days. “During the
past two years women have given proof of
such heroism aud devotion.... that it seems
an impertinence to publish a play in which
they are shown concerned merely with one
minor issue, struggling for a freedom that
affects themselves alone.” Sutro says that
that female is out-of-date; but offers his play
as possessing some value as an indication of
the state of affairs from which the war
rescued us.
Here are the usual women demanding
their “rights,” and their men who help them
in their struggle. The principal characters
are the proprietors of a woman’s rights’
journal. Theoretically they believe in the
right of any woman to have a child without
being married; but when one of the men
confesses that his “wife” is not really his
wife, the other men insist on him going
through the rite of marriage. Having drunk
too much, this man blurts out the fact that
the wife of one of his friends is in love with
the husband of another. But the outraged
husband does not treat the matter from the
high philosophic standpoint of his journal.
He merely tries to kill the other man and
almost strangles the offending wife. Miriam,
the wife who had caused all the trouble, ex-
plains quite calmly that she took on_ a lover
because she rebelled against the feeling that
she belonged to her husband. “There’s some-
thing in us that hates law —revolts from it
—the law that we haven’t made!” Also she
was equally annoyed with both men—her
husband for being “so abominably sure” of
her, and the lover who refrained so long
from making love to her “because to him,
too, I was your property.” So, proudly,
triumphantly, she gave herself to the other
man! And then she “resented the way he
looked at me —his little conquering air.” And
finally she discovered that die loved her hus-
band best! But when her husband threatens
to divorce her she promises to be good, be-
cause she does not want to be taken from
her children. Her fault, she cont nds, is too
trivial to have such a punishment. “The
law allows you to hit the mother through
her children.” If only somebody—husband
or lover —had spanked Miriam! But then
there would have been no play.
The moral is that women should have
their freedom, because it is only when they
have got their freedom that they’ll learn
how to use it. But Sutro shows, in
the .characters of two of his women,
that they haven’t by- any means learned
to handle that freedom. Fanny explains
to a suffragette who asks all women
to stand together, that women never will.
“They’ll always be the woman like me. I’m
one of the betwixts and betweens. Just got
my bit of good looks. And they’re going.”
Fanny didn’t expect her man to marry her
at first, because she had had an “adventure”
before she met him, and he knew. But she
hoped that some day he would marry her.
Eve. the wife of the erring husband, i*
another type. She knows all about her hus-
band’s affairs, but: “What can one do?
I’ve been very miserable, of course. But it
happens to so many women.... You see, I
love him... .One loves, because one loves.
I suppose wemen are like that. At least
some women. They just cry a little, and go
on darning the children’s clothes.” “You
take it like that?” she is asked. "It’s the
best way, isn’t it?” she says. “Happier for
the children Whatever they do, or law*
that they make, there’ll always be Borne In
whose life there’ll be only one man. Women
like me.”
An interesting play, but dreadfully
“dated.” A quite dead play, almost as dead
as the suffragette.
J*
Hobson’s Choice, by Harold Bridhouse
(Constable, per Dymock’s Library), is an
interesting Lancashire play, with more meat
and genu me humor in u than we are likely
to see for a long time in Australia. It is
the sort of play that one would expect Arnold
Bennett to write if he would only write up
to and not down to his theatre public. It is
a play _of Lancashire character; the char-
acteristics of the Hobson family, their prac-
ticality, hard-headedness and aggressiveness
are just the characteristics that the average
Manchester man prides himself on possessing.
This delightful story of the domineering
daughter of a domineering old bootmaker has
all the charm and quaintness of Bunty Pulls
the Strings, though the no longer youthful
Maggie marries her man and manages his
business, and her father’s, by means of no
captivating charm, but by her downright
shrewdness and her grim, curt, business-like
determination.
Walt Mason is a cheerful verse-writer
who by the simple expedient of printing his
verse as prose has achieved a large popu-
larity ; Edgar Lee Masters achieved an
equal fame by writing prose as verse.
Mason’s Horse Sense amused John Masefield
and others. Hence he follows it with
Rippling Rimes (Duckworth, per Angus and
Robertson, Sydney, 2s. 6d.). His simple
philosophy is aptly expressed by “The Brief
Visit.”
I won’t be long in this vale of tears; my works
may run for a few more years, but even that is a
risky bet, and the sports are hedging already yet.
At morning a gent, feels gay and nice; and evening
finds him upon the ice, with his folded hands and
his long white gown, and his toes turned up and his
plans turned down. So, viewing this sad uncertainty,
and hearing the wash of the Dead Man’s Sea, I want
to chortle the best I can, and try to cheer up my
fellow-man; to make a fellow forget his care, and
make him laugh when he wants to .swear, is as
much as a poet can hope to do, whose lyre is
twisted and broke in two.
A little of this other Walt goes a long way.
An English critic remarked that after reading
the book through he experienced the vague
discomfort of wanting to sneeze and not
being able to. However, here is “The Handy
Editor”: —
When a man- has got a grievance that is keeping
him awake, some old mouldy, tiresome trouble that
has made his innards ache, then he comes a-cally-
hooting to the printing-office door, for he wants to
share hi# trouble with the humble editore. When
a man has got a hobby that has put him on the
bum, then the people flee a-shrieking when they
chance to see him come; but he knows one weary
mortal who must suffer and endure, so lie comes
to share his theories with the lowly editure.... When
a man has got. a story that with age was stiff and
stark when old Father Non'' told it to the people in
the ark, then he cornea a-bubblinsr over to tiie
Weekly Bugle’s lair, for he wants to share his
gladness with the soulful editaire. Oh, he’s always
freely giving of the things that make us tired, and
he’s often pretty stingy with the things that are
desired; he might bring a ray of sunlight to a life
that’s sad-and drear if he’d give the absent treatment
to the heartsick editeer.

H.G. (Gympie) suggests as a name for
Billjim’s Maoriland mate, “Moazac.”
The grim confession of Jack London: "I
hate my profession.... I assure you that I
do not write because I love the game. I
loathe it. only reason I write is be-
cause I am well- paid for my labor —that’s
what I call it—labor. I get lots of money
for my books and stories. I tell you I
would be glad to dig ditches for twice as
many hours as I devote to writing if only I
could get as much money. To me writing
is an easy way to make a fine living.
Unless I meant it I wouldn’t think of saying
a thing like this, for I am speaking for publi-
cation. I ‘am sincere when I say that my
profession sickens me. Every story that 1
write is for the money that will come to
me. I always write what the editors want,
not what' I’d like to write. I grind out
what the capitalist editors want, and the
editors hay only what the business and edi-
torial departments permit... .1 am weary
of everything. I no longer think of the
world or of the movement (the social revolu-
tion), or of writing as an art. lam a great
dreamer, hut I dream of my ranch, of
my wife. I dream of beautiful horses and
fine soil. I dream of the beautiful things
I own up in Sonoma County. And I write
for no other purpose than to add to the
beauty that now belongs to me. I write a
book for no other reason than to add three
or four hundred acres to my magnificent
estate. I writs a story with no other pur-
pose than to buy a stallion. To me my
cattle are more interesting than my profes-
sion.” Quite time he died, wasn’t it?

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
Morning.
Chirping birds their music trill,
Happy in the freshened earth,
Songs of careless life that fill
All the world ; oh, life has still
Ecstasies of love and mirth!
Heralding the coming morn,
Harmony again is born.
There was tempest in my mind ;
By strong gusts of passion curled,
Phantom shapes bestrode the wind.
Thoughts loomed strangely in the blind,
Dim-lit darkness of a world,
Where the nightmare demons thrust
Arrows poisoned with distrust.
Through its gloom my spirit tossed,
Torn by evil thoughts and doubt,
Grief for things I fancied lost
Ahger for ambitions crossed —
Dreadful shadows round about!
But upon its strong, sweet wings
Gentle calm the morning brings.
Somehow, lingers in this breeze
From the tears of nature born,
Fragrance of old memories.
Far away across the sens
Lo, the silver shafts of morn
Brightly break the clouds apart!
There is morning in my heart.
N.S.W.
HAMER.
Sea Fruit.
To think that slimy thing they dragged was
she!
The fools they were,
Hiding what its face was, with its hair,
Lest I should see!
And how they bit their lips when, curiously,
For all their protests, not to be denied,
I pulled the towzled mess of hair aside
And saw the sea-slugs’ havoc. As for me,
Who knew so well her loveliness, I found
No other thing to do hut laugh aloud,
Marvelling at this madness of the crowd.
“It may be, as you say, that she is
drowned, -*
But not like this my Love will come to me!”
I said and walked in silence from the quay!
How long ago that was I cannot guess;
If days or years
I know not, counting time but by its tears
And loneliness.
I know she woulu have come by now, unless
(Ah, that one fear, it ever will recur!)
Unless the Sea Kings, pleased so well with
her,
And heeding not the signs of her distress,
Hold her their captive underneath the sea,
And as a queen, have set her on a throne
To drab their kingdom’s beauty by her own,
Nor heed her pleadings that she come to
me.
And so I wait her, all the day and night,
Nor ask of God one easing of my plight.
For she will come. It may be late or soon,
But come she will.
Perhaps some night, when all the winds
are still,
Beneath the moon
I'll spy the silver splashing of her shoon,
As, fleeing down the waters in her fear,
She hears the Sea Kings chasing in her
rear.
Then will I rush to her and claim the boon
That niggard Joy withholds from us so
long.
And she and I will Love’s delighting prove,
While all the stars, compliant to our love,
Sing from the skies our hymeneal song.
To think that it was she, the fools they were,
Hiding what once its face was, with its hair!
MAORII.AND.
BERNARD INGLEBY.
Just to Drift.
Drifting down the Harbor,
Stars on high,
Lovers of the surface,
You and I,
Let us never pry and wonder
At the things that lie thereunder.
Underneath the surface
Silver-fair,
Let us never question
What lies there ; .
Lest we lose, like some robbed miser,
All our treasure, growing wiser.
Lo, it has the beauty
Of a flower!
Is it not sufficient
For the hour
Just to drift as mists are drifted,
Depths unplumbed and veils unlifted?
Where’s the flawless jewel,
Stainless breast?
Where’s the Love that answers
Every test?
Where’s the past that’s altogether
Cloudless as this radiant weather?
Drifting down the Harbor
On the tide,
Careless of all knowledge,
Let us glide,
Heedless of what Life discovers,
Save that you and I are lovers.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Thou Shalt Not.
Woman, pausing on the marble stair,
Come down one....come down two.
Death is creaking through the doors of air,
And a red, red knife for you.
Woman, lying on the gleaming floor
Warm the blade. .. .cold your skin.
Love’s a madman when he loves no more,
And a heart is hot with sin.
N.S.W.

Under-Sized Tyres on your
Car act like Under-Sized
Shoes on your feet!
HEN your Car-Manufacturer speci-
fies ‘‘Bls x 105 m/m” Tyres for
your Car, he does not mean that
815 x 96, 97, 98, 99 or 101 m/m will do
just as well.
When he specifies that the Air-Capa-
city in “815 x 105 m/m” of Tyres is neces-
sary to carry the weight of the Car and
its Occupants satisfactorily, he does not
mean 815 x 96 3-5 m/m.
When he says the Tyres should be
“880x120 m/m” he does not expect that
880 x 109 3-5 m/m will do justice to the
Car, or give its Owner the maximum
return for his investment in Comfort or
duration of Service.
YET these are instances of the short
measurements you actually yet
when you buy any millimeter Tyres
made in Europe (not Goodrich Tyres).
The Car Maker specifies the MINI-
MUM size of Tyre which will properly
carry the Load of Car and Occupants,
because he must supply the first set of
Tyres.
If, then, that first set proves Under-
Sized, for the Car, blame it largely on the
fact that no millimeter Tyres are FULL-
SIZED (except Goodrich Tyres).
* * * *
PUT on your Car the actual SIZE of
Tyres which its Maker specifies—
viz., Tyres that measure (in cross-
GOODRICH
section, ns well as diameter) tlie actual
millimeters which the Tyre Size calls for,
and you will find a very different PER-
FOItMANCE resulting.
You will find longer life of the Car —

THURSDAY, MARCH 22, 1917.
A STATE LABOR PARTY IN THE
DOCK.
What in N. S. Wales is called “the State
ejection campaign” is timed to collapse on
Saturday. The casualties will be removed
to the morgue on Sunday. On Monday there
will be many funerals, with loud, bombastic
remarks in the newspapers, and cheers,
whoops, groans and moans over' the quick
and the dead —and then the weary old
wrangle will start at a fresh place while the
public sits back to hope for the best and
wait for the worst. There may possibly be
a few benighted citizens who during the last
week or so have imagined that the Voice of
Ihe People was going to decide great issues
of public policy, and that on the terms of a
meashred popular judgment the new Parlia-
ment would proceed to execute the mandates
of the electors. If there are any such indi-
viduals it is safe to say that they will be less
numerous next time. As at other elections
the people have counted like so many jum-
-1 nicks. A few persons selected by obscure
committees have been produced, hot-air liber-
ated in large volume, and the electors stam-
peded after this man or that with wild yells
of Party. The citizen who wanted to vote
for Labor lias in many cases had before him
the alternative of either not voting at all or
casting his scrap of paper for someone whom
he regards as a wooden-headed incompetent.
The man who has believed it to be his duty
to go with the other crowd has just as often
no choice between voluntary disfranchise-
ment and voting for a spineless dullard. In
the mass the problem has been whether the
elector would swallow something whole, Lib.
or Lab., and in both cases the dose has been
the same —the party joss, horns, tail, hoofs
and all.
Three years ago this paper urged its read-
ers to seize and introduce the axe to the
reckless crowd which had been making hash
of the Labor policy, and turning the Labor
platform into an object of derision. Labor
was mightily indignant at this. To-day there
is no thoughtful Labor man who does not
know that if the advice had been. followed
the movement would have been in better
health than it is now. The intervening years
have been a rush down-hill, culminating in
a smash which left Labor almost unidentifi-
able. The Bulletin, which has always
warned Labor against the demoralising
theory that mere victory at the polls after a
disordered scrap in the electorates repre-
sents the goal of Labor policy, repeats the
advice of three years ago. Any trickster
may win an election. It is allegiance to
principles alone that counts. Labor in N. S.
Wales won to office on the policy it placed
before the electors, genuinely believing in the
honesty of its promises to make that policy
good in the fields of legislation and adminis-
tration. That it failed —and failed worse
and worse as time w r ent on —is but to mildly
express what happened. Once in office the
retention of place became a passion. To
this end it bartered away every vestige of
principle it possessed, and at the last, after
a record of muddle and debauchery without
any parallel in the history of Australia, pre-
sented the public with the degrading spec-
tacle of Labor politicians being jobbed into
public offices and shamelessly fighting over
the plunder which politics in unworthy hands
may be made to yield.
* * * *
It makes a sorry story, and The Bul-
letin is more convinced than ever that until
the Labor movement in the State purges
itself of the weaknesses and cupidity and
downright flat dishonesty that made the
betrayal of a great trust possible, the move-
ment has everything to gain by being pre-
vented from degrading itself still further.
As a critical and suggestive force there is
room for a sane Labor Party to exercise a
salutary influence on the politics of N. S.
Wales. For men ready to pawn the country
and spend' the proceeds in bribing workmen
with easy, handy jobs, many of them of no
public value, there should be no room.
Democratic government is not another word
for fraud. Yet,. though Labor govern-
ment has drifted into a policy of fraud,
the P.L.L. is impenitent. It still stands
for government by dope. It will do nobody
any good to look away from the truth.
The Labor movement is in a bad condition
in this State mentally and morally;
and until it can again get a grip on those
finer conceptions of duty and citizenship
that it relinquished to foregather with the.
swine, the proper place for it is where it
has a chance of redemption.
* * * *
The alternative to Labor is Holman; and
this paper has many times expressed its
opinion of Holman quite frankly. But out
of all the -welter of confusion and chican-
ery there emerge considerations for to-
morrow that are not covered by squabbling
about yesterday. The State has to take
things as they are instead of as they might
be, just as the man' with but a single pair
of boots must wear them even if they be
shoddy instead of leather. And considera-
tions for to-morrow have in the main far
more connection with Administration than
with Legislation. The immediate interests
of the State turn upon whether the public
shall witness a repetition of the adminis-
trative follies of recent years or something
with more reasonable claims to decency.
The P.L.L. in its present corrupted and de-
generate state is hopeless. Holman is at
least free from that .influence. Moreover,
the people with him know their job better.
They have more brains. If not more honest,
they can see further. Holman, having worn
the shoe, knows where it pinches. He knows
that he is not getting value for the borrowed
money; whereas John Storey is holding
out promises with a disregard for settling
day as casual as that of Holman in his
hours of greatest madness. Apart from this
vital consideration that administratively
Holman’s new party appears at least more
reliable than the party of Borrow and be
Damned, the Labor party as it stands in
this State is cursed by an inclination to
transfer the final word in government from
Parliament and people to a coterie of party
■wirepullers. This is so flagrant an attack on
the principles of democratic government that
if deserves the most vigorous assault. The
plan is adopted not from any desire to see
democratic progress, wise legislation and de-
cent administration,-but because it appeals to
the P.L.L. as a method by which Parliament
may be dragooned and the electors throttled
—because official Labor wants above all
other things to get hold of the machinery
of government and use it to bend the public
to the party joss. This is not what Labor
came into politics for. It represents, in-
deed, a negation of everything that Labor
stood for. This paper therefore recom-
mends N. S. Wales to give the Labor party
on Saturday a further chance to get sober.
That is the best service any true friend of
the movement can render to his democratic
ideals.
PICKING UP AN ESCORT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tiie broad expanse of ocean was relieved by
a group of sombre-painted vessels w 7 hose
rusty sides gleamed as they heeled over to
the long, eternal Atfantic roll. Day after
day, night after night, they had ploughed
along in line across the wastes, bearing to
the Motherland’s aid the sons of the outposts
of the Empire.
They have now 7 reached their final rendez-
vous. A semaphore message waves from the
escorting cruiser, and, in response, the leader
of the convoy “clowns ‘Answer’,” draws out
of the line and reduces speed. A boat
plunges across the heaving swell and ranges
alongside. Hundreds of sun-tanned troops
list the ship as they crowd along the rails,
A canvas bag is deftly drawn on board from
the boat —messages from the sea to wives
and mothers and sweethearts waiting re-
signedly athome.
The cruiser has come to the end of her
beat. Signal follows signal in quick succes-
sion—wishes of “Good luck” and “God
speed,” and, finally, a string of bunting
creeps up to the signal-yard. The troop-
ships alter course together and their heads
are pointing at last to the little Homeland.
With “A pleasant voyage” streaming out on
the wind the cruiser slowly swings round on
lief helm. A rousing, thrilling cheer speeds
out from ship to ship as she passes along—
pent-up expressions of thanks for her cease-
less protection. She dips her ensign in
acknowledgment, and, rapidly gathering way,
is lost astern in a cloud of smoke.
“Strange business that we should be left
to our own devices at this juncture,” ob-
served the Colonel, reasonably.
“Look here, sir,” the captain replied, “the
Admiralty knows what it’s about —don’t you
worry!”
So the ships proceeded independently dur-
ing the day. The Ariel, having the best
speed, gradually drew ahead, while the
Miranda and Ophelia jogged along more or
less in company. The trail of the Ariel’s
smoke besmirching the eastern horizon was
all that was visible when night closed down
upon the grey undulating ocean.
All lights being screened, the ships soon
became merged in the darkness.
Below, in the dormitories, the troops
snored as usual. The armed guard strutted
to and fro, stopping now and then to peer
into the darkness. But only the phosphores-
cent trail of a porpoise making for the ship
caused occasional momentary concern.
A muffled eight bells marked midnight.
Nothing had intruded upon the gloomy blur
of the horizon.
Soon after the middle watch had settled
itself down to its job, the captain—prone on
the chart-house settee, lightly dozing with
one eye on the clock—was brought to his
feet by the thrust of a Marconigram between
the door curtain. “Naval message, sir,”
said the operator. “A good way off yet,
though, sir.”
The captain eagerly turned over the pages
and soon decoded the grouped letters: “State
your position, course and speed.” The chart
lay spread open before him. After a careful
laying on of parallel rulers and a pricking-
off with dividers, the result, dotted on a chit,
was promptly converted into an apparently
unintelligible jargon. In a few moments a
crackling could be heard overhead, and the
secret reply had flashed into space.
The captain, having cautioned the officer
of the watch that the speaking-tube whistle
would find him immediately, went below to
his cabin. There he had his cocoa and
toast; then lighting a pipe, he “stretched off
the land” again, feeling at comparative
peace with the world. The troops below
still snored. A few hours passed. At 4 a.m..
as though by instinct, the captain roused
himself, mopped his face with a spongeful of
icy water, and struggled into his unifoi’m
overcoat. Simultaneously the wireless oper-
ator emerged from his kennel of a room and
dashed along to the captain’s cabin. “Must
be up against us now, sir,” he said breath-
lessly, as he handed the captain a message.
“Nearly knocked the top of my head off,
sir,” he added with a smile. “Sparks” was
game ; he’d been “listening in” then for 16
hours without a break.
The captain skipped up the bridge ladder
to the chart-house, and again fingered the
pages of his code. The few letters now
meant: “Burn navigation lights.” He hurled
the book into its recess, pressed down the
electric knobs on the switchboard near the
door, and rushed out into the blackness of
the bridge. He was greeted instantly by a
shout from the officer of the watch: “There
you are, sir, lights abeam —just switched
on, sir !” The glare of the chart-house light
had momentarily affected the captain’s
vision; nevertheless he soon made out the
masthead and port lights of three vessels in
line.
“Must be the destroyers,” said the cap-
tain, peering through his binoculars. “Ha.
ha,” he chortled as the small craft swerved
round and showed their green lights, “Worth
while pottering about all night for this, eh?
Position’s about dead-on, too. Good old
Navy !”
A peremptory Morse signal ordered all
lights out, and in a minute or two three
low-lying black streaks were swishing eerily
alongside in the darkness.
“Are you Miranda?” the lamp flashed
again.
The fact that the ship addressed teas the
Miranda caused one to refleet upon the con-
summate skill of these sea-patrols.
“Where is the Ariel?” was the next query.
“About 30 miles ahead —last seen slightly
on starboard bow,” was signalled from the
Miranda’s bridge. The escorting vessels
thereupon had a rapid interchange on flash-
ing lamps, and, without more ado, the lead-
ing destroyer darted ahead and vanished.
“I reckon I can enjoy a pipe now,” remarked
the captain as lie descended the bridge
ladder.
At the first streak of dawn the alert de-
stroyers commenced their day’s work of
sweeping the Way ahead of their charges;
backwards and forwards, to and fro, they
went incessantly, darting finely across the
bows. Instructions from the shore Military
Commandant to the O.C. Reinforcements
were unconcernedly shot across by sema-
phore as the little ship skimmed and churned
about on its lively manoeuvres.
Gradually the convoy approached the ship-
ping tracks, and vessels of all descriptions—
mail liners, transports, cargo tramps and
colliers —pursued their various ways, zig-
zagging incongruously.
Suddenly, a few miles away on the star-
board bow, there appeared two low-lying
grey objects with squat, tower-shaped humps
protruding from their middles. In a few
seconds a terrific explosion flung skywards
a great cloud of spray and debris from a big
homeward-bound cargo steamer’s foredeck.
Like an arrow released from the bow-string
the Miranda’s destroyer flew off, and was lost
in oily black smoke, foam and lashed up
spray. The Miranda turned and zigzagged
over the ocean.
The troops, teeming with excitement,
crowded along the rails and cheered wildly
as the destroyer’s bow- and stern-guns spat
out flashes. The firing ceased abruptly and
the destroyer dashed off to the damaged
steamer. A few 7 moments later she was back
again, unconcernedly criss-crossing the
Miranda’s bows.
By this time the animated soldiery had
swarmed for’a’d on to the fo’c’s’le.
“Call her up, sir.” said the captain to ihe
watch officer. “Ask the news.”
The signal was soon made. The destroyer’s
answering pennant came fluttering down,
and J(long-tack)M instantly flew up in its
place. The destroyer was swishing along
at 28 knots, and with each sharp swerve
round she leaned over and smothered herself
in sheets of wind-driven spray. A blue-
jacket, swaying like a human clinometer, was
poised on the top of the after gun-shield,
from which venturesome position he calmly
waved his red and yellow semaphore flags.
The destroyer’s reply, characteristically
terse, sent a thrill through a thousand men.
Just this: “Steamer’s crew saved. Bagged
the two submarines.”
* * * *
Three days later the usual cablegram
appeared in the Australian daily papers:—
“The Umteenth Reinforcement arrived at
its destination on its due date.”
CATHEAD.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Choice.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Scene : — -An open place near the Forum.
A man standing on a srtone is stringing in
mid air. The stone is attached to a crane on
a high building by a long tvire rope. A
nurse passes, wheeling a go-cart which con-
tains the future Premier.
Enter two citizens. .
Ist Citizen : Observe the pleasant knave
that lightly swings
From the high pulley of the moving crane.
What recketh he of falls or pavement
bumps
Where high he soars, his lighted cigarette
Between his careless teeth?
2nd Citizen: Much doth he venture;
But that stout rope of wire that holdeth
him
Is hardly stronger than his iron nerve.
I would I had the heart to venture so.
Ist Citizen : Tush! ’Tis a small thing to
the desperate chances
Our politicians take. His life’s in peril;
But they, while votes are cast in ballot-
boxes,
Must stand in hourly peril of their screw.
2nd Citizen : How will you vote?
Ist Citizen : Why there, good friend, you
ask
The riddle I myself would somehow solve.
The gracious Holman with large words
and pleasant
Hums like a summer music in mine ears
Of that he will do if I but support him
And keep his crowd in office. Wonder fills
me
And stirs me almost to the brink of tears.
I marvel at the greatness of his purpose.
2nd Citizen : Ay, he speaks well, but will
he keep his promise?
Ist Citizen : Why now you ask too much.
Hath he not talked
These ten years past with excellent clear
tongue
And failed fulfilment of his smallest
word ?
2nd Citizen : The honorable Storey talk-
eth, too.
Ist Citizen : So do they all. The place is
full of talk.
They drown the shrill mosquito’s voice at
night
And hush the roaring of the trams by day.
2nd Citizen: Storey is new. The new
broom sweepeth clean.
Ist Citizen : It sweepeth clean so it hath
any head;
Yet none sweep chambers with the stick
alone.
2nd Citizen: The P.L.L. is mighty in the
land
And all its force is set against the w r ord.
The honeyed word, that Holman loves to
speak.
Ist Citizen : The P.L.L. is like a ship at
sea
O’erburdened with much cargo and a
crew
That overthrongs the deck, but with no
man
Who knows to read the compass or can
take
The sun at noon. Its sails of good inten-
tion
Are filled with gusty gales of cheap adven-
ture
That blow towards the shoals of Nothing
Done.
2nd Citizen; Will Holman’s talk do more?
Ist Citizen : I cannot tell.
It may do far too much, as heretofore,
And still do nothing.
2nd Citizen: See. Who comes here?
Ist Citizen : This is a prating fellow
Who lately battled ’neath the flag of
Wade
And now would snatch a crumb from
Holman’s plate
Or any other who hath bread to give.
2nd Citizen ; See, he hath joined another.
Stand aside.
They are in high debate as well beseems
The sturdy leaders of a people’s thought.
Let us be listeners if we may but know
The purport of their discourse.
Enter two National Party candidates,
lately members of the Great Liberal Party.
Ist Candidate : I backed him for a fiver.
Tompkins reckoned
The tip a good one.
2nd Candidate: Do you speak to-night?
Ist Candidate: Yes, I must keep them
moving. Win the war —
That is the talk. Change horses in the
strea m
And the high flood rolls o’er you. Liberty!
Freedom from P.L.L. and caucus chains!
2nd Candidate: The same old yarn with
minor variations.
I'm talking too. If only we might have
Another dozen millions now 7 to spend
These knaves would never v T in a doubtful
seat.
Ist Candidate: Don’t make too sure. Those
knaves can borrow too —-
And talk : and those behind can organise.
Besides, they scored at the last Referen-
dum ;
And don’t forget' it.
2nd Candidate : That was months ago.
The crowd is fickle. Its opinion changes.
Ist Candidate : The time is ripe— swing
of the pendulum—
The same old yarn told in the same old
way,
And nothing certain till the figures come.
We may fluke through; so comeanavea-
drink.
The candidates go out.


Dark Russia.
Someone at Washington has said that the
earthquake at Petrograd “represents the
greatest victory for democracy the world has
known.” But even to speak of democracy in
connection with Russia is to strain the mean-
ing of words. The revolutionary instinct has
always been strong among the people; but
between revolt against the gnout and the
pogrom and the exactions of nobles and the
Church on the one side and on he other a
comprehension of democracy, there is a vast
difference. The great mass of the Russian
people are no more concerned about democ-
racy than this paper is about O’Malley’s
hat. They are ignorant, frugal and patient,
concerned only with the elementary affairs
of life, ready to accept any form of govern-
ment as a divine dispensation, provided they
get food and shelter and are left alone. Far
different has been the spirit of those among
whom the democratic instinct has found
expression. These have been peqple tor-
mented by philosophies, struggling for the
assertion of theories of government and
human rights which Russian citizenship has
ha -P v yet scented. The constitutionalists in
have never looked beyond government
through a parliament: and in their present
mental development a democratic -parliament
is to them inconceivable. It is possible
that the revolution may be the birth of a
Russia with free institutions; but even that
must remain doubtful for a long time. Czars
have abdicated before, and it is not the first
time blood has flowed in the streets of
I’etrograd. What is probably true is that
the semi-representative Duma, stung by the
inefficiency of the autocracy, and alarmed at
the sinister power of enemy influences, saw
that the only hope for the country lay in
securing an authoritative part in the nation’s
affairs; wherefore, seizing hold of the revo-
lutionary movement, they gave it force and
direction. But there are very few points of
resemblance between this upheaval and the
revolutions through which France purged
herself of the old regime. And it must not
be forgotten, anyhow, that Bonaparte
succeeded Louis and the second Empire
arrived after the second Republic. Democ-
racies do not grow up in a night. What
the world sees in Russia is what it has seen
somewhere ever since the dawn of time —
I reedom, throttled and bound, writhing in
agony for deliverance. More than that one
cannot say surely; for it is idle to suppose
that any of the current yarns we are hearing
give us more than a rudiL.entary notion of
what has been happening behind the dark
curtain that has been drawn between Russia
and the rest of the world ever since the war
began. Is there the material in Russia to
stem the flood of anarchy now that it has
begun to overflow ? There was not in France.
Is one despotism fighting another? Ilow can
we know when we are permitted to hear
only the fag end of a distant echo?
The Law of “Military Necessity.”
In spite of the loud talk of the Prime
Minister a fortnight ago, Senator Millen,
Vice-President of the Executive, now an-
nounces that there is to be a political cen-
sorship “governed by the law of military
necessity.” The Prime Minister and hi's
colleagues are, of course, to be the judges of
what constitutes “military necessity.” In
the newspapers of the Commonwealth there
are ten thousand examples of the operation of
the law hitherto; for, of course, everything
that ever has been suppressed has been dealt
with avowedly under this law. Unfortunately
the newspapers are not permitted to tear the
mask from the thing and expose it by giving
instances. But fortunately the Prime Min-
ister has himself supplied the public with
one sample. When Mr. Hughes was strug-
gling to keep, the Labor party together, he
told the House the other day that lie forbade
any newspaper reference to the threatened
split. So the solidarity of the Labor party
was a “military necessity.” There are, of
course, equally good grounds for declaring
that the maintenance of the Fusion is a
“military necessity.” If, for instance, John
Forrest, becoming disgusted with the con-
duct of Defence affairs by the brilliant Mr.
Pearce, proceeded to resign, that fact might
be suppressed on the grounds of “military
necessity.” _ Even a suggestion that the
Prime Minister should replace Mr. Pearce
as head of the all-important Defence De-
partment might be vetoed by the very oblig-
ing law of “military necessity.” It is just
as well that the people, on the threshold of
a general election, should know what a
convenient thing “military necessity” is.
The Kaiser found it so in 1914. It was by
the law of “military necessity” that he just-
ified the over-running of Belgium. During
the coming campaign in Australia the people
here are to be subject to the same law.
Exactly why they should curse the German
Kaiser for applying it to Belgium and re-
ward any other IvMser for applying it to
themselves The Bulletin confesses it does
not know.

The Policy of Winning the War by
Talking a Lot.
“Help the Ministry and win the war”
is the plea put forward on behalf of
the Hughes-Cook Fusion. And “they
would not come in and help us win
the war” is the principal charge brought
against the Federal Labor party. Now,
this would be all very well but for the
lamentable omission of Hughes to explain
how he is going to win the war. Until he
produces his war policy how are people to be
blamed if they regard with derision the “Win
the War” label that decorates the Fusion.
Mere talk about winning the war is the most
futile exercise imaginable. To say what the
policy is not only adds to the general con-
fusion. What the public wants to know is
what the policy is. Wherefore. the time is
more than ripe for Hughes to cut away
from all the rhodomontade he has bewildered
the community with and tell Australia in
plain, direct language what he is relying on
to win the war with, and exactly what it is
an elector who wants to give him the assist-
ance of a vote is voting for. Until Hughes
does so The Bulletin is going to contem-
plate this “Win the War” brand worn by
the Government with the same suspicion as
it views a mask and a set of false whiskers.
Canberra—the Great National Mess.
The publication of an interim report by
Wilfred Blacket. the Commissioner ap-
pointed to tell the Federal Government what
had been happening at Canberra, gives those
individuals who are accustomed to “resent”
charges of waste and muddle a fine chance to
make some more remarks on the same lines.
Canberra has been a carnival of ineptitude,
shirk, laziness and loot ever since it was
mentioned. From first to last the public in-
terest has had about as much chance of
getting a fair deal there as an unprotected
leg of mutton has in a hyena’s cage. The
place was chosen as the national capital site
after a disgraceful intrigue, and wire-pull
and smoke-o have prevailed there ever since.
The substance of Blacket’s finding bears
out every word said in this paper about the
scandals perpetrated at the place in the
name of administrative government. He finds
that there was an actual conspiracy among
officials to defeat the supposed policy of
Parliament, and a Minister (Archibald)
gave to this his sanction and patronage.
Blacket in his report says
The reasons why Mr. Griffin [the designer of the
city], between October 18, 1913, and November 15,
191,3, performed no substantial part of his duties
under his contract with the Commonwealth Govern-
ment are as stated in four of the five charges
advanced on his behalf—viz., that the necessary infor-
mation and assistance were withheld from him and
his powers were usurped by certain officers; that he
and his office were ignored, his rights and duties
under his contract denied, and false charges of
default made against him; that Mr. W. 0. Archi-
bald and members of the Departmental Board endea-
vored to set aside his design and to substitute the
Board’s own design; and that there was in the
Department a combination, including Mr. W. O.
Archibald and certain officers, hostile to Mr.
Griffin- and to his design for the capital city.
So Parliament decides to build a city, agrees
to a plan of operations, votes money for the
job—and the proceedings promptly fall into
a state of chaos, the public is fooled and
various individuals are paid for doing the
fooling! Everywhere the Australian citizen
may took he sees himself surrounded by evi-
dence that the direction of national'affairs
is in the hands of messers, and that whether
it be building a city or running a war or a
post office muddle reigns supreme. In this
particular case of Canberra it is true that
Archibald was torn from his place, snarling
about the unjust way he had been treated.
But his vast capacity for wasting money and
making a mess mattered so little that when
Hughes formed his Rump Administration
Archibald was given the place of Minister
for Trade and Customs —a job only second in
importance to that of Defence Minister! Is
the public never going to wake up and insist
upon at least decent administration? Or is
it simply going on approving of politicians
who plainly say that decent administration
doesn’t matter?
German Hopes in China.
China has broken off friendly relations with
Germany; and that is more important than
it may seem on the surface. Germany knows
that the international struggle witli arms is
to be followed by a no less desperate inter-
national struggle with trade; wherefore,
just as it organised for war during many
years of peace, and thus put the Allies at a
tremendous disadvantage, so it has filled all
the years of war organising for peace. It
has ererfted a “Board of Imperial Commis-
sioners for Transition Economics” ; and the
business of this group of experts is to do all
things necessary to seize trade immediately
peace is proclaimed. To that end large
quantities of manufactured goods have
been accumulated and vast quantities of
raw materials arranged for, principally
through America. With the object of
gaining an advantage in populous neu-
tral countries where commerce has hitherto
been little developed, Germans have been
established in agencies. China has been the
object of special attentioh. “There has been
no feature of the war,” Gerard Williams
wrote to The Bulletin from London the
other day, “more striking than the response
of the oversea Englishman to the call of the
Empire.” He called it magnificent. It pro-
bably was. But the watchful German
promptly jumped at the chance it offered. In
China the Imperial Maritime Customs
Department was manned chiefly by young
Englishmen. They rushed to England on
the call to arms —and their places have been
taken mostly by Germans. That is only
one instance of the German’s after-war
strategy. Britain’s trustful complacence
before the war has made things easier. It was
alleged some time ago that as late as 1013
30 “British” consuls in China were Ger-
mans. All these and many more things
point to the fact that Germany had made
deep plans for the exploitation of the vast
trade possibilities of China. Now, with the
rupture, the preparations will be at least
more difficult. But, though that may be a
good thing for Britain, it by no means fol-
lows that it will necessarily be a good thing
for Australia. If other avenues of trade are
not as open as the German had expected, the
chances are he will make the fight for his
old avenues more willing ; wherefore it is all
the more necessary that we should lose no
time in blocking him with the - only thing
that will do it—a sky-scraping tariff.

Party Funds and Public Dishonors.
The London Morning Post puts it this
way:— ■
If we are to have a better country and a better
Government after the war there must be all end
made of two things—the secret party funds and the
sale of public honors. They are both detestable to
honest men; they are both a danger to the State.
The one is used to corrupt men ; the other to corrupt
a party. They insult the King; they degrade
honor; they betray the nation.
Which, coming from a Tory paper, is evi-
dence that J. Bull is waking up. It is
good doctrine for him and as good for Aus-
tralia. We have gone even farther, though.
In this part of the world public funds too
often do the detestable work of corrupting
both men and parties; and there won’t be
much hope of better things till the Labor
Federation and the Liberal League at a
joint conference pass a resolution something
like this: —
Until the use of the national revenues and public
credit for the purpose of catching'votes and creating
jobs for party heelers is stopped Australia will not
have democratic government. The practice now
followed of debauching the electors with loan
money is abominable in the eyes of every decent
Australian and a hideous danger to the nation. It
degrades our political ideals and standards of public
and private life, makes mockery of the Decent Thing
and turns the Fair Deal into an obsolete phrase.
Then if the Argus weighed in with some
appropriate observations about “honors con-
ferred on Australians” being slops from an
unclean bucket, and the 8. M. Herald pub-
lished a full statement of the source of the
funds of political parties in Australia, apart
from the public cashbox, the dawn of a new
day would be at hand.

The first officially acknowledged and organ-
ised “go-slow” strike has been under the
consideration of the N. S. Wales Arbitration
Court. It concerned the lumpers employed
in discharging coal from vessels for the big
Sydney Gas Co. The award under which
the men work required a gang of five to
unload 10 tons of coal per hour; but with
the machinery at their disposal 25 tons can
be discharged. The employees were encour-
aged to work up to this rate by being paid
for the extra quantity as if they had toiled
extra time; if they unloaded 200 tons in
eight hours, • they were paid for eight hours
ordinary and 12 hours overtime, which ran
out at £2 12s. per day. Being unselfish men,
however, they demanded that a sixth hand
should be added to the gang, and that he,
also, should get £2 12s. The Gas Co.
objected, and there was a strike until, the
Industrial Court having made some remarks,
the secretary of the Lumpers induced, the
men to get back to work. But that didn t
settle the trouble. The men had not won
their point, and, to show their dissatisfac-
tion, they have been unloading only 10 tons
per hour. This means that they have to
work 20 hours for the pay they could earn
in eight, but it upholds the disunion princi-
ple of hitting the boss, and, incidentally,
threatens the public with a gas famine,
because the vessels which used to be emptied
in a day are now held up for several days,
and at that rate there is not sufficient ton-
nage to keep supplies up to requirements.
The seriousness of the position having been
represented to the Minister for Labor, he
went to the Industrial Court for power to
appeal against the award so as to compel the
gangs to discharge 20 tons per hour; and he
obtained it. But any award of the Arbitra-
tion Court having such an object looks
pretty hopeless. It isn’t a Judge that these
men need ; it is an educator. Instead of
guiding them, their so-called leaders have
been merely telling them the things they
liked to hear, with the result that they have
been filled up with the rottenest economics
that ever misled men and threatened the
future of a nation.

The Russian upheaval has been followed, by
the same old struggle to show that it is just
the thing the Allies wanted. The truth is
that nobody knows what the Bear .will do
when he has finished dealing with the
bureaucrats who have oppressed him. In
that connection it is significant that on the
day after the news reached Australia —which
was a week after the trouble began—-the
cables mentioned that “Von Hindenb.UßG is
concentrating tremendous resources in the
West” ; and that did not look as though the
Germans, with their intimate knowledge of
wlmt was happening in Russia, were expect-
ing anything more serious in the East.
Russia may presently claw the enemy as he
was never clawed before; but for the pre-
sent, that being at least doubtful, the only
safe and reasonable tiling to do is to assume
that a great additional responsibility is
thrown on the other Allies —which, so lar-
as we are concerned, simply means on Aus-
tralia.

Arthur Renard, of Melbourne, who knows
as much about the relation of soil-contents
to production as anybody in Victoria, has
lifted up his voice to protest against the
export of maize. He is hammering away
at the principle that, while the Victorian
stock industry is in its present feeble con-
dition, it is foolishness to export substances
which should be used locally to conserve the
fertility of a soil naturally deficient in
phosphates, and to increase the beef and
pork supply. Renard doesn’t worry with
figures, wherefore it seems an opportune
moment to mention the fact that a cargo of
maize sent last year to Bristol (Eng.) was
valued at £60,273, and of this sum only
£18,826 was for the maize. The rest was
freight. The biggest return for maize as
an export would be received by sending it
abroad turned into meat. Freight is
freight, and it is better to pay a lot of
freight on a dear article than a lot of
freight on something a good deal cheaper.
In the case of maize-fed carcases, too, much
valuable plant-food has been returned to the
soil before the poleaxing of the beast. Still,
preaching crop-economies to the farmer is a
thankless job. Matters might be more hope-
ful if national authority showed itself
capable of giving a lead, for instance, in
regard to the exportation of flour instead of
wheat, retaining the valuable “offal” in
Australia.

The first secretary of the Boilermakers’
Union in Sydney was James McGowen,
subsequently one of the first Labor repre-
sentatives in the State Assembly, and later
Premier of the first Labor Ministry.
McGowen supported compulsory military
service and was declared “bogus” : and last
week the Boilermakers’ Union voted £25 from
its funds to help the expenses of the P.L.L.
man who is out to biff the old warrior from
his seat at Red fern—after 25 years.
McGowen may be this, that and the other
thing; but here he is being persecuted be-
cause he held a certain opinion on a ques-
tion of public policy submitted to a plebiscite
—and the funds of a union are used to
assist in giving him the boot! Apart alto-
gether from the morality of this, see how
it cuts across the preference-to-unionist doc-
trine. A man must join a union to get work.
Good. But the money he contributes to his
trade-union may be used to defeat a politi-
cal idea which he may believe right. That
means compulsory subscription for the sup-
port of policies which the subscriber objects
to. Does anyone outside a madhouse think
that preference to unionists can stand with
a disease like that eating its vitals?

A picturesque yarn was floated by a Syd-’
uey paper the other day about the anxiety
of certain anonymous individuals to start in
business as exporters of horse-meat. It was
represented that there is money in the pro-
ject, that the necessary capital and stock can
be secured, that horse-flesh is always in de-
mand in-London and Paris; and, generally
speaking, that if ,T. Bull could get a steady
supply of refrigerated horse it would help
him to save and keep in good nick. One of
our main troubles just now is to deliver T.
Bull supplies of wheat, flour, mutton, beef,
cheese and butter, and this paper knows of
no reason why any of those commodities
should be shut out to permit of embalmed
nag getting in at the early doors. If there
were available shipping it might be a fair
thing for Private Enterprise to go into the
horse-meat business; but the project hap-
pens to be heard about at a moment when
millions a month are being spent in securing
a passage for more palatable grub. Especi-
ally in view of the supply of horse available
from the Front, where the wastage is stupen-
dous, the notion that the British citizen is
yearning for more in preference to chops
looks rather too wild an assumption to go
into business on.

WORKMEN'S COMPENSATION.
The N S Wales Workmen’s Compensation Act operates as from July 1 this year. Every employer of labor is legally liable to pay compensation for injuries
sustained’by his employees. In case of fatal injury three years’ wages is the allowance (with a maximum of £5OO and minimum of £300) ; during the term of
disablement of an employee, half-wages allowance, total payment not to exceed £750.

A Warm Summer.
If during the battle of the Somme the Brit-
ish troops had blasted their way to I’eronne
and Bapaume, the result might well have
been that decisive victory which the Allies
are awaiting. For the German line from
the North Sea to Verdun would in
all likelihood have been seriously imperilled,
and the mending of the fracture would have
been a hard job. But that is not what did
happen. The thrust petered out in the mud
and the German had time to establish a new
line. To this he lias adjourned ; but where
the line is, whether it is as strong as the
last one and whether any substantial advan-
tages will have accrued to the Allies by the
change in Hindenbuhg’s address have at
the time of writing not been disclosed. That
the German was lately feeling uncomfortable
Is certain. That the battle of the Somme
represents a defeat for the enemy is clear.
What seems equally beyond dispute is that,
the battle has to commence elsewhere, and
that we can make up our minds that it will
be more furious and prolonged than the ter-
rible struggle waged last summer. Of the
other side we know practically nothing ; but
not much lias occurred since 1914 to justify
us in believing that the flattening out of a
salient on any front can be taken as evidence
of a crumbling defence. That the most criti-
cal stage of the war will soon be reached is
almost beyond dispute—critical in the sense
that the staying power of the armies engaged
will be tested as they have not been tested
before, the effect of the submarine cam-
paign determined, and the place of Russia
in the conflict settled. If Russia could
have been eliminated. Germany’s chances
of making some sort of a win would have
been far greater than with Ivan on the war-
path. Will tin 1 revolution weaken or
strengthen the Russian arms? There can
be no answer to this, for revolution may
mean anything. Still, the removal of a
German Czarina and the tearing of pro-
nounced pro-German sympathisers from the
administrative machine are good reasons for
expecting that all this will later on mean
greater trouble for the Kaiser on the eastern
frontier of his “beloved Prussia.” There-
abouts General Frost is still in command
and General Mud will carry on when the
thaw commences. Soon after these old war-
riors take a holiday the answer to the Rus-
sian mystery will be available- —and, probably,
the end of the war be within range of the
more powerful telescopes.
The last act of the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment was to fix matters so that Australian
soldiers may vote in the Federal elections.
It was a proper thing to do, if only for
sentimental reasons. But whether Billjim
will know what or who he is voting for seems’
doubtful; for his voting paper is only
going to present him with choice between
“Governfnent” and “Opposition.” Can he
have any comprehension of the earthquake
that, during the last few months, has turned
upside down the political significance which
these words previously carried? This paper
'does not think so, and it is fairly certain
that Hughes and Co. have the same notion.
Anyhow, they resisted an amendment to
alter “Opposition” to “Labor” ; and if that
was not done in the hope of the soldier-voter
voting the wrong way, the reason for object-
ing to the change is hard to guess. The
Opposition is Labor, anyhow. Why give the
soldier a vote with one hand and surreptiti-
ously drop dust in his eye with the other?
A typical cry of anguish (and they have
become much more numerous again of late) :
I must, yell a protest or burst.
Can you tell us what has gone wrong with the
Postal Department? During the past 10 months I
have received nine letters, four Bulletins and two
small parcels. At least 10 times that number have,
I am told, been sent to me.
I was wounded nine weeks ago,, and notified the base
office in London of my transference to an English
hospital seven weeks ago. Since then I’ve been
waiting for something to turn up, but up to date all
that has arrived was a tin of cigarettes, much the
worse for wear, that had been roaming around for
the past seven months.
One letter —the last—was received 11 weeks ago.
It enclosed some documents for signat e and stated
that a letter explaining the matter had been posted
a fortnight previously. That letter has not arrived
yet.
Another Australian here has sent two cables, one
five weeks, the other two weeks ago ; he has had no
reply. We know it is not the fault of our friends.
These are not isolated cases; they are in accord-
ance with what has become the general rule.
One of our boys “went West” three months ago
without hearing a word from home all the time he
had been away. Poor beggar ! —he felt that more
than anything else.

“Jocic McWagga” asks for the home grown
article: —
I am tired of hearing the perpetual abuse of Aus-
tralia on account of the muddle made by a few
foreigners in Federal politics. Hughes misrepre-
sented Australian thought in England. But how
could it be otherwise? Hughes is first and foremost
a Welshman; after that a British schoolmaster. What
should he know of the thoughts of Australians?
Had he the artistic skill to enter into the very soul
of his surroundings and get with something of a
dramatist’s instinct the angle from which Austra-
lians looked at things it might be different; but in
that case he wouldn’t be in politics. Any number of
people from overseas become thoroughly Australian
and identify themselves with the thought and life
of the people, but they must serve a long apprentice-
ship in private life in order to do so; and even
then the kinks they brought from older lands occa-
sionally show up. Our Federal Ministers should be
native born. By so insisting we might occasionally
lose a good man capable of leadership, but we should
also lose the foreign adventurers to whom can be
traced almost the whole of our political trouble. The
millennium wouldn’t arrive at once, but the result
might be a great straightening out of crooked things
and a clarity of issues such as we haven't known for
a long time. It isn’t fair that decent Australians
should be branded with the sins of the stranger within
our gates. To import our political shoddy is as bad
as to import any other sort. The foreigners in poli-
tics don’t even pay duty; and they can go to the
poll without wearing the brand of their country of
origin.
The N. S. Wales election disturbance has
raged with unparalleled ferocity in the
Phillip division of Sydney. The retiring
member is Dick Meagher, Speaker of the
Assembly, for many years returned on the
Labor ticket —which constituted perhaps the
greatest political joke in all Australia. Now
lie and I’.L.L. President Doyle are bombing
one another from house to house. The once-
uqanimous electorate has been torn asunder
as by dynamite, and after wrangling at the
top of its voice all day throws itself into
the fray at night with tremendous bate.
Brothers fight broth rs, families have been
broken up. and Mick rends Mick. Ilichard
is having the worst trip he ever had in his
life, and though making the usual claims
about “confidence over the result” has horri-
ble dreams of going to the pack. Most of
the newspaper reporters have been tipping
him a sure loser.
We are saving up quite a lot of things
for the kid —including the repair of the ,
State schools lie gets his hit of education in.
Dr. Abraham Haynes, health-officer at Rich-
mond (Vic.), has just made some heated
remarks to the local municipal council about
the five dispensaries in that suburb. Of
the school at North Richmond, he reports
that “the playground in wet weather is a
swamp and should be asphalted.” The
other four are all “in a deplorable condition,
badly lit and ill-ventilated in summer, with
most of the class-rooms damp from broken
gutters and neglected roofs.” They have
not been painted for 20 years, it seems, and
“the walls of the Central and Burnley schools
have large cracks from the roof to the
ground, and are Unsafe.” But the lads have
this satisfaction : there is no report to the
effect that the residence of the superfluous
State Governor is leaking; and the last
time this writer inspected th premises the
playground attached to the local Parliament
House was quite well-drained.

by (S.A.), and sending Watt over to smell
roiyid, decided that he had better not. The
trouble in S. Aus. is that the Liberals de-
cline to accept these late Labor men aw
Liberals. Peake, ex-Premier and Liberal
leader, remarked in a pointed way that
Labor men, however patriotic, are pledged
to certain Labor principles which Liberals
cannot swallow. Failing a fusion or a
Labor reconciliation the Liberals will pro-
bably get into office again in the State. The
figures are always pretty close in S. Aus.,
and having three parties must mean trouble
for the one party that used to combine two
of them.
Melbourne news item: “Mr. Hughes left
for Sydney on Saturday with Mr.. Cook.”
Resigned to his mate, so to speak.
Frank Anstey, outspoken as usual, told
the House of Reps, the other night, without
a contradictory murmur, that “Parliament
had been returned practically unanimously
to build up a higher tariff, but for the most
part members never had any intention of
doing so. They were false to the pledges
they had made to the public at the general
election.” But what if they were? The
par tv whips will crack and the people will
rush into two flocks; and it won't matter
two straws whether Smith has proved him-
self a traitor and a liar —he will get the
vote if he gets the party nomination. And
in nine cases out of 10 he gets the party
nomination not because be is honest, but be-
cause lie is subtle; not because be can run
straight but because be can dodge.
Martin Edward .Tull. Westralian Public
Service Commissioner, died at Perth last week
after a short sickness. He came .from Sussex.
England, and was 50. After serving articles
with a firm of surveyors in London, lie
arrived in the West in 1886, and was
appointed confidential clerk to J. A. Wright,
then Director of Public Works. In ’92 lie
became chief clerk and in ’95 Under-Sec. to
the Department.

“Corn-meal mush” is what the kiddies of
the John Grier Home in N’Yark are fed on.
It is the same mush that the audience at
Sydney Criterion is fed on ; for the unmen-
tioned" person who dramatised the novel,
“Daddy Long Legs,” produced for the first
time on Saturday, ekes out the thinnest story
ever told with large helpings ~of sentimental-
ism and “sob-stuff.” The circulation of the
novel has, however, proved that there is a
huge femiuine appetite for mush; so the
dramatist provides four courses of the fill-
ing food, garnished with thick slabs of
pathos and innocuous humor, and with three
long intervals between the courses. There
is no dramatic interest in the story ; every-
thing happens just as it is expected to hap-
pen ; the final curtain is foreseen half-way
through the first act. When a middle-aged
bachelor millionaire becomes the guardian of
a remarkable pretty orphan waif, what every
woinan knows is that she will end by marry-
ing him. She could easily have married him
by the end of the third act, but in order to
spin out the comedy ‘to a fourth act the
girl keeps silence when the millionaire pro-
poses. This simple theme is mixed with
much extraneous pathos and the humor of a
number of stereotyped stage characters.
Unadulterated mush would be too cloying.
The play would be impossible without
Kathlene MacDonell. This imported actress
proves herself possessed of a charming per-
sonality, a delightful sense of comedy,
and somewhat unexpected reserves of
emotional power. She dominates the
stage with her youth, charm and vitality,
and she has the instinct of dress. Charles
Waldron, without any special magnetism,
makes the most of the part of the pleasant
millionaire. Louis Kimball is well placed in
the part of an irresponsible youth, used as a
transparent puppet to arouse the million-
aire’s jealousy. J. Plumpton Wilson pre-
sents a delightful cameo of the dear old
butler of the stage: and Maggie Moore
is an equally pleasant interpreter of
the placid old dame who inhabits every
farm in American fiction or drama. Eily
“THE PINK LADY.” AT SYDNEY
MAJESTY’S.
Mrs. Dondidier (Celia Ghiloni) discovers
her husband at the Satyrs’ Ball.
Malyon makes the manageress of the orphan
home the ogre that melodramatic tradition
insists upon ; while Emma Temple has to be
hut her agreeable self to provide the
foil required by all the canons of the stage.
Clarice Hardwick is the usual college
girl of the United States, and Olive Wilton
is her conventional mother. In the first
rather dragging act the kiddies of the orphan
home provide some conventional humor,
while in the second the goings-on in a. girl’s
study afford the only genuine comedy relief.
The rest is corn-meal mush, well served up
by Kathlene MacDonell. The run of the
play will depend upon whether the great
heart of the Australian female is suffused
with the same large oozy sentimentalism
as that of the feminine United States.

Italian opera flow's on at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s. one night’s applause being only a
little less vociferous than another’s. “Caval-
leria” and “Pagliacci” were sung two'nights
last w'eek, and in these tabloid tragedies
Scamuzzi and Balboni repeated themselves
to perfection, or something very near it.
Scamuzzi’s picture of the tall, trim teamster
who disposes of young-man Lois behind the
scenes, is a masterly creation. He lights
his pipe and blows a cloud with the air of
a man w T ho is cocksure of his vengeance.
His stage business is as expressive as his
countenance and his baritone. In the other
opera he is twice blessed, for his rendering
of the familiar Prologue is a fine piece of
declamatory singing, and his subsequent per-
formance of the malignant clown comes a
good second to Balboni’s notable success in
the name-part. The plump, genial tenor
wins all the sympathy of the house for
Pagliacci by his unaffected way of getting
obsessed with jealousy, and his warble at the
close of Act 1 has some sweet notes of
pathos in it. All the men of the co. seem
to have held their own whilst the orchestra
and chorus were improving, whereas the art
