"Fair and Warmer,” transferred from Syd-
ney to Melbourne Royal, is a pretty poor
medium for the introduction of a neAv Eng-
lish comedy company. True enough, there
are many laughs in it—laughs that ring par-
ticularly loudly on a first night, Avhen the
Amphi. is croAvded with girls Avho have come
prepared to roar and shriek. But, for all that,
it is often tiresome. The story has a frankly
American fictitiousness. Tavo childless
young married couples dwell on differ-
ent flats in the same building, under
different domestic conditions. The bored
wife of Billy Bartlett proposes to elope with
a Don Juanic friend because he is bright and
wicked, whereas Billy has no pleasant vices
nor has ever dreamed of being jealous of
Juan’s attentions to Mrs. B. On the other
hand, the artless, brainless spouse of Jack
Wheeler remains intensely devoted to him
because he makes a point of taking one night
a Aveek off to play cards Avith the “boys.” His
only sinfulness consists in telling his dovey-
dovey that he goes to lodge meetings when
he sallies forth to the poker school. It
happens naturally enough that when Billy’s
AA’ife tells him what an insufferable son of
virtue he really is, the other lady learns of
her Jack’s mild deceitfulness, and, being left
for the evening in company with the disillu-
sioned wowser, she proposes that they shall
nurse their respective grievances until Mrs.
Bartlett returns from the opera with Don
Juan, and then get themselves discovered in
a compromising position. In a long-drawij-
out scene the pair of impossible
fly to mixed drinks to keep themselves awake,
with the result that they are discovered by
their respective partners in a compromising
state of squiffiness. Next act the blackest
clouds of suspicion are dispersed in a bedroom
scene, where Billy and his morning headache
are the central facts, and the incidental com-
plications are sadly hackneyed.
* * * #
Only tAvo of the newcomers seem naturally
adapted to their parts. T. A. Braidon has
the difficult job of presenting the wowserish
Billy, with his childish ignorance of intoxi-
cants. as a serio-comic person in various
phases of enlightenment. He does it very
well, though lie cannot suggest the possibility
of liis taking a second cocktail after his awful
experience Avith the first, the drunk scene
being obviously prolonged for the sake of
spinning out the farce. Miss Nightingale
looks more convincing than her acting as a
pert parlormaid who has eyes only for the
men. whereas Miss Royton’s acting in the
drunk scene is rather more effective than
her appearance as a guileless little Avife.
Percy Marmont is a conventional, harmless
deceiver Avho goes mad when lie thinks he is
deceived, and Harry Sweeney is a. trifle too
loud for an insidious seducer. Miss Ather-
ton, who acts with cold correctness, hardly
seems the sort of matron to listen to the
A'oice of the tempter Avhile she has got a con-
fiding husband who buys her the loveliest
diamond rings. She isn’t of the warm,
capricious type. The farce, Avhich is care-
fully and ornately staged, is intended to run
three weeks.
At Melbourne King’s the blokes who start
the show by swapping sad reflections in a
pawnshop continue to “Turn to the Right”
and prosper exceedingly. The nice girls are
still like unto ripe plums or peaches that fall
into the outstretched mouths of their hungry
admirers, and the moral of the piece con-
tinues to be that philanthropic burglary is
the first step towards an upright and honor-
able career.
At Melbourne Majesty’s the nights of “The
Waltz Dream” are numbered. “Canary
Cottage” comes due on Saturday for the
first time in Australia. This musical play
is a product of the same ’Murkan factory
that “So Long, Letty,” came from.
The most important difference between the
present and previous week’s bill at Mel-
bourne Tivoli is in the ballads sung by Mal-
colm McEachern, whose treatment of “When
Bright Eyes Glance” reflects further credit
on the estimable basso. Malcolm’s songs are
a musical oasis in ' a desert of rag-time
raucosity. The latest re-arrivals are the
Flying Weavers, who fly by the skin of their
teeth, so to speak, and would be better
described as exponents of the iron jaAV. The
voice of Bluett is recovering its punch, and
Bell Oliver opens her mouth in hearty praise
of Big Healthy Men.
The Fisk Jubilee Singers. Avho arrived at
Melbourne Auditorium on Saturday, chorus
niggah melodies in a Moody and SAvankey
manner, or solo effusively in songs that are
dead. Mostly a harmonium supports the
clamor, but occasionally a piano provides
the musical scaffolding for some tottery in-
stitution like “Annie Laurie,” or that dear
antiquity, “Silver Threads Among the Gold.”
Now as ever, the Fisks are best in their
chorus Avork, and Avlien they Avarble "De
Loard’s Prayer” and other religious num-
bers, the absence of a plate is severely felt.
Marshall Palmer is the most cultivated of
the soloists, and reveals his mangled feelings
in the brief and passionate history of a frac-
tured heart. The soprano, Miss Morrison,
hasn’t enough natural graces to make her
vocal deficiencies charming; and the bari-
tone, Harold Wilson, is a draAvling genTman
AA'ho turns every item into a series of musi-
cal yaAvns. There are four colored ladies
in the combination, and the greatest of them
is Bertha Miller. Bertha is a Sambo plagi-
arism of Maggie Moore, wherefore it is
superfluous to remark that she is immense.

Vic. Chief Secretary Donald McLeod lias
once more made up liis mind about the
appointment of policewomen. Some months
ago iliac invited applications from persons
willing to serve as Jane Hops, and nearly a
hundred were sent in. McLeod looked the
papers over and then announced that he
Avouldn’t appoint anybody. Now, having
thought the matter over again, he has de-
cided to appoint “ttvo or three.” The public
hasn’t yet been informed Avhat their
duties will be; but where tAVO or three
policewomen are gathered together to justify
their official existence, it’s likely that there
Avill be something doing there that has never
been properly done before.
AT POVERTY POINT.
"C. Oekney”: Marie Tempest, soon to be
seen on Australian boards, was, like Ethel
Irving, a comic-opera artiste before she
found her feet as a comedienne pure and
simple. Straight from the London R.A.M.,
her fresh soprano was first heard on the
theatrical stage in “Dorothy.” After the
record run of Collier’s opera Marie had a
long experience of musical-comedy under
George Edwardes at Daly’s in “The Greek
Slave,” “The Geisha,” “San Toy,” etc. One
of her first hits in a legitimate acting part
was made as Nell GAvynne in an Anthony
Hope play, “English Nell.” Since then,
resting her singing voice, her talents as a
comedy actress of the first water have long
been recognised in England and America.
She should be welcome.
Marie Tempest is a perfect gourmand for
publicity. She frankly confesses that in the
matter of advertisement, enough is only as
good as a snack to her.
“Sardonyx”: “Jwosp” (B. 15/3/T7) is
wrong about that old-time scrap in “Madame
Angot.” I saAv the shindy, and it was Jean-
nie Winstoun, not Alice Lambert, who came
to blows with Clara Thompson. The ill-
feeling betAveen the two had made them
several times threaten hostilities ; this time
an over-vigorous slap from Clara brought an
equally solid return from Jearinie. For
about half a minute exchanges were lively,
and the audience avus roaring at what it
thought was clever realism, when the sudden
fall of the curtain made clear what Avas
happening. Blood aaus drawn and hair was
•pulled, but there Avsn't any “cluttering of
the stage Avith rags, locks and hairpins.” The
stage-manager squashed the exhibition too
promptly for that.
“Monkbarns”: Dyson (B. S/8/T7) is all
too brief in touching on the demolition of
old Ballarat Theatre Royal. Former play-
houses there were the Montezuma and
Charlie Napier. The Royal became Snow’s
drapery store. Foundations were laid 60
years ago for a theatre in Smith-street, Col-
lingwood, but more sensibly they were used
as the nucleus of the Foy and Gibson rag
shop. The disguised remains of defunct
playhouses are everywhere. The ramshackle
Victoria, in Bourke-street, near Hosie’s
restaurant, became the Canterbury Music
Hall. Queen’s Theatre, opened in ’45, re-
mains intact in the shell. It turned into
Clarke’s coach works, and is noAv Fall-
shaw’s billiard-table factory. There was a
little Theatre des Varieties at Salmi Morse’s
Collins-street Criterion Hotel, which had the
famous bridal chamber for lucky diggers.
Coppin’s Olympic, opened in ’55, became the
Turkish baths. After a fire it Avas renewed
as Cohen’s furniture factory. Noav there is
nothing left but the Avails, and scarcely a
fragment of the old bricks in them. . Cop-
pin’s little wooden Pantheon Theatre at Cre-
morne Gardens has long vanished. Likewise
his Haymarket, in Bourke-street, where
your grandfather saw Mr. and Mrs. Charles
Kean, Dillon, Anderson and Madame
Celeste. The Prince of Wales’s Theatre Avas
in Lonsdale-street, opposite Melbourne Hos-
pital. Burnt theatres: Royal, Bijou and
Bourke-street Varieties, afterwards the
Opera House.

Memories of 29 years ago were revived by
a cablegram last week announcing that Mrs.
Fenwick had been appointed supervisor of
a military hospital mansion in England.
Mrs. Fenwick used to be Sylvia Grey, ow
of the principal dancers of the first Gaiety
Go. that came to Australia. Sylvia was a
trifle quicker on her feet than her brilliant
rival soloist, Letty Lind, an elder sister of
Fan Dango, who was also a charming comedi-
enne. They were pioneers in the art of long-
skirt dancing in thjs country, and the fact
that their gauzy under-dvaperies were art-
fully divided in a “bloomer”-like fashion
came as a revelation to the women journal-
ists who interviewed them. Sylvia Grey, in
one of her dances, used to duet with Fred
Storey, the lean and agile young man of the
accomplished co.
“Gin Fiz”: An Australian who is
landing in ’Frisco about this time is Jean
Robertson, a young Adelaide girl who
Marlowdramaed until the Firm —or vvas it
the youthful Julius? —gave her a chance
with the last Knight co. Jean should have
a better chance than most gumsuckers. She
did a fairly decent bit of movie work before
leaving here. Another recent exit was that:
of picture-man J. I). Williams and his wife.
“Stargazer”: I have been too long associ-
ated with studios to be greatly startled by
revelations of the human form divine : hut I
must confess that the use to which the
creators of great films are putting the un-
draped dame pulls me up short. In terrific
50-reel plays, costing a billion dollars and
designed to inculcate great moral lessons, I
find that almost invariably one of the most
conspicuous mediums of inculcation is
woman in the raw or so nearly so that the
scarcity of the garment deceives the eye and
you think she’s got nothing on even if she
has a bit. Eve in her salad dressing is sup-
posed to convey an important lesson to man-
kind ; and picture actresses are taking leaves
out of her book, though a great many are
not wearing them. Poor Chidley’s mistake
Avas in being born a man. Had he been born
a woman he could have pursued any great
moral purpose he cared to in prodigious film
dramas, in verdure clad, like Eve, except for
the absence of the verdure.
(Continued on page 26.)
If the British could only pay compliments
as nicely as the French, a decent Australian
soldier would not now be walking lame as
the result of a terrific leg-pull. When a
driver got a typed letter on A.I.F. note-
paper conveying the news that he had been
awarded the V.C. and promoted to sergeant,
he took it at face-value, quietly spread the
good tidings, and bought himself a strip of
V.C. ribbon. There was nothing in the inci-
dent to arouse a trusting man’s suspicions,
as, apparently, the usual procedure is to
allow the mechanical typewriter to clatter
first news of the award to V.C.-winners.
But instead of being paraded at Bucking-
ham Palace. Driver Billjim, who was in
London at the time, found himself presented
at ■ a police-court on a charge of illegally
wearing the ribbon. He' was acquitted; but
that doesn’t end the matter. Our boys are
shy birds, and the next .Australian hero will
have to be leg-roped and have an affidavit
read to him before he will submit to any
medalling.
Shackleton, lecturing in Melbourne, let the
cat out of the bag in regard to the Polar
exploration business. After confessing that
his last expedition was a failure, he
announced
It was not science that caused me to attempt that
journey from Weddell Sea to Ross Sea across the
Antarctic, but the sentiment and idea of the thing,
and a love of adventure.
This should be remembered next time
Shackleton or some such adventure-seeker
proposes another sentimental journey to
Polar regions and appeals to the Govern-
ment or the public for funds. Some valuable
lives and large sums of cash have been
Avasted on Polar jaunts, but the old gag
about the scientific importance of the adven-
ture has always been exploited. Shackleton
is the first public performer of his class
honest enough to admit that the spirit that
sends men to the Arctic and Antarctic is not
very different from that which causes other
blokes to try to bang a piano or bIoAV a
trombone for 68 days.

Put up decrepit fights once more.
And senile heroes roar and blent,
AVith bundles on their gouty feet.
“By gad!” said one, and dropped a tooth,
“Once more to our forsaken art,
Too long besmirched by callow youth,
Fresh glory will our work impart;
To-day ’tis youth, and not old age,
That bides superfluous on the stage.
“This war will not have been in vain
It' soon, though indirectly, it
Brings genius to light again.
A fid, on my word, I’m feeling fit.
i'll do my lngomar, 1 swear,
With one to wheel me in my chair!”

“Wart”: Circular Head (Tas.), which
gets a boat for a few hours weekly, has a
Waterside Workers’ Union. When the mem-
bers are not watersiding they are mostly
farmers, timber-getters and spud-diggers.
The union is a loyal branch of the big
Federation of Waterside Workers which
Billy Hughes .created, and as such was
ordered by headquarters to have a picnic
and be gay. A special meeting of the union
was held, and it was unanimously resolved
to hold the picnic on a AVednesday, the day
the steamer Marrawah leaves for Mel-
bourne. It was also resolved to demand ss.
an hour for any work done on that day, and
tine any member f 1 who worked for less.
However, that idea was dropped,- because
someone found out that, according to the
rules, the boat could not be loaded by the
union under any circumstances. Nor could the
crew be allowed to touch the cargo offering;
if they did it would be “black” ou arrival
in Melbourne. There were 180 cases of
butter and six tons of cheese which had
been carted by motor-lorry from the country
districts, to say nothing of a quantity of
blackwood timber. The boat left at 11 in
the mornings—empty. Then the motor-lorry
was again brought into play, and the butter
and cheese rushed to Smithson, to be for-
warded by a ketch. And the members-of
the union spent the picnic day at their
ordinary work of spud-digging and timber-
getting !
* * * *
While he lived John Norton wasn’t much
of a success as a reformer; but he did a
big thing when he died. For more years
than most people can remember there had
been talk of a Testator’s Family Mainte-
nance law in N. S. Wales; but as there
didn’t seem to be any votes in it no party
ever made a hobby of it. Then John died,
after practically cutting his wife out of his
will; and as Mrs. John wasn’t a lady to turn
the other cheek in a spirit of unchristian re-
signation, the usual old Testator’s Family
Maintenance Bill that happened to be then
before the House found itself kicked and
cuffed along as it never had been kicked and
cuffed before. Since then, partly because the
law was (through the same influence) made
retrospective, a Court has been kept so busy
with disputed .wills that the usual Divorce
business has had to be hung up. As a fair
sample of the cases dealt with, take this
one. A son had crept into his father’s
favor, and, by a late will, scooped practi-
cally all the estate, out of which he made
the widow a niggardly allowance. Another
son came in for a small share. The Court
not only granted a decent income from the
estate to the widow and a fair allowance to
a daughter, but decided that the portion of
the son who had scored under the will
should provide seven-eighths of the allow-
ance. This knocked all the glory out of the
luck of the favored son, and placed him on
a level hardly as good as that of his brother
and on a worse one than that of the widow.

A flying man drops a bokay for Billjim : —•
I had the luck last week to see the Light Horse in
action, being just overhead when they rushed .
This flying game has always struck me as being the
best fun, but that charge beat anything I’ve ever
seen, and, as you know, I’ve had the luck to see a
fair bit. Any man who fell in that gallop must have
had one compensating flash of glorious life before
going out. Just mad men on mad horses! They
swept everything before them, and I found myself
hanging out and hooraying as if I were one of a race
crowd. M swears that I howled “Australia
wins! Australia wins!” in a voice like a steam-
whistle, and, by Jove, Australia did win. It was a
wonderful thing to see, but think of being in it!
I saw some of the fellows later, and they were as
modest as men who’ve done a good job usually are.
Having nobody else to give the credit to, they gave-
it to the horses.

“A.W.Iv.”: Harry, our ' company cook,
whom we buried at Gallipoli, had the queer-
est resurrection story I’ve ever heard. He
was a “leatherneck” on H.M.S. Camper-
down when that ship was accidentally
rammed and sunk during Mediterranean
manoeuvres. Though on duty ’tween decks
when the plunge came he was vomited to the
surface with the rush of escaping air and
drifted into Dreamland clutching a floating
grating with a 5-h.p. clutch. Unnoticed by
the naval rescue parties, he was picked up
some hours later by French fishermen, and
eventually reached his naval depot in Eng-
land, fondly hoping for hearty greetings. He
was soon disillusioned by the “brass hat”
in charge, who pushed him into the clink,
where he sat and swore for 24 hours. He
was “in” on a charge of impersonating him-
self ! He roared with laughter when he
came to the next chapter—how Mrs. Harry
received the news of his death. Then there
was the joke of having his name qhipped off
a seaside memorial to the Camperdown vic-
tims. Never did a man have such fun
coming to life again! But Death laughed
last, as he always does, when our joker fell
face down with a Turkish bullet in him.
An old contributor to The Bulletin, an
officer of the Flying Corps in Mesopotamia,
now a prisoner of war i.i Turkey-in-Asia,
describes Christmas and New Year’s Day :
In the yard at the rear of our jerry-built terrace
of four houses, where 40 of us live, we had a
rough proscenium, where during the summer months
we staged Sundry Shows, written by various of us,
and ranging from drama and revue to rag-time and
ancient Greek. They were “produced” by a naval
officer of a theatrical family who was once stage-
manager at London Garrick. Of course “properties”
are scarce and oil for illumination expensive; but
we do not want for wigs, which are made from
goats’ hair by a soldier servant with some experience
as a coiffeur. “The Pessimists’ Dream” was about
our best farce. The actors were supposed to be
cheery young fellows of from 00 to 80. They are
British prisoners in 1950, and on arrival of parcels
of false teeth and ear-trumpets grow quite excited.
This excitement is increased by the appearance of
•a new prisoner, a Boy Scout, of the 1000th Division,
who discovers his grandfather among the prisoners,
and is welcomed by all hands, since it gives them the
excuse to tell all their time-worn stories over
again. Finally word comes that the war has
ended; but everybody refuses to leave without a
year’s notice.
Christmas was as festive as circumstances would
permit. After much debate “Jack the Giant Killer”
was agreed upon as the panto. We produced it on
December 23, and beards were taken off by those
concerned to do it justice. Now that winter is
KNEW HIM.
SHE: "Well, I predicted it.”
IT: "What?”
SHE: "Whatever happened.”
with us and our open-air theatre is covered with
snow, “Jack” had to come indoors. We fixed up a
stage in our largest room, which measures nearly
20 x 12. We dispensed with ballets, but we had
Principal Boy, Princess (our blondest blonde), K,ing,
Widow, and Jack’s dog. There were six acts full of
topical jokes and gags, and our scene cloths were
works of art —our “painters” did them with char-
coal on bed-sheets. The Pirates’ Ship and the Cave
gave the brigands an opportunity to show what they
could do in the way of consuming rakki, the local
tipple, which is the nearest we could get to rum.
It may have been the rakki; it certainly wasn’t the
lobster, but poor old Jack had to make his own
splash when he was thrown overboard. Still, like
the other shows, it was a huge success.
On Christmas Day we exchanged visits with the
people next door. They weren’t exactly strangers;
in fact, we exchange visits every day. But we
made these occasions of great importance, and
decorated the premises with home-made paper lan-
terns and the tables with elaborate menus. If
we couldn’t always produce the corresponding dishes
it wasn’t our fault. There was mistletoe, too; but
thereabouts there seemed to be something missing.
Festivities concluded on New Year's Eve with a
fancy-dress ball; and with the aid of a small organ,
which an explorer had discovered, we did very
well. Our only other instrument is a mandolin.
Perhaps it was the mandolin; or maybe it was the
rakki. Certainly by the time 1917 arrived some of
the ladies looked almost real. But they never
went to the Methodist Ladies’ College.
And then we settled down to devise new schemes
to break the monotony of captivity. All of us
came to grief in the early days of the Mesopotamia
campaign, and most of us have enjoyed the hospi-
tality of Apion Kara Hissar for the worst part of
two years.

Englishman in Egypt, and he was so cock-

“Tam O’Shanter” : There is no fun in elec-
tions now. When I was young all the
officers at the country polling-booths were of
the imported class, and most of them wore
tall hats and had a habit of pulling things
out of an abyss in the tail of coats that hung
below their knees. They
knew nobody, and no-
body cared, for that
made it all the easier
for the hardworking fel-
low who wanted to be
Tim Finnigan, deceased.
pro tem. Every tomb-
stone with a man’s
name on it was an
election asset. I remem-
ber a contest for Yass
Plains. Polling-day
was over, and I
watched the numbers
being posted till all the
returns except one were
in. As my man was
then over 80 votes be-
hind, and there were
fewer than 60 eligible
voters in the place
where the belated re-
turn was to come from.
I went home and kicked
the dog. But I apolo-
gised to the poor brute
when I heard that my
man had been returned
by a majority of over
20. The graveyard had
given him a majority for
that centre during the
last hour or so. But
altered “c ut s” an d
clean-shaven faces for
the next few weeks
showed clearly that a
close contest had been
anticipated. It was a
great lesson in civics.

“The Immature Male”: During a recent
argument in the lines one swaddy called the
other—a late arrival —a “Nat Gould.” After-
wards I asked him why. “Well,” drawled
the warrior, “Nat wrote ‘Landed at Last,’
didn’t he?”
“Astron”: One of the biggest blazers ever
known in comets will be visible with the
naked eye this year; but it will be small,
because of its tremendous distance. It will
only come within 164 million miles of our
inquisitive noses and we must look for it in
July. This traveller was discovered by
Wolf, of Heidelberg, on April 27 last, so it
has been hurrying towards us for nearly 11
months. Last July it passed behind the
sun, and did not emerge till lately. When
we lose it we need not expect it again for
60,000 years. So make the most of it,
gentlemen ! You will be snugly tucked away
before it plays its return season.

THE SILENT KEYBOARD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Melbourne musician has invented a silent key-
board for the use of piano students. With it the
hands may be perfectly educated, though the notes
struck are so faint that they cannot be heard outside
the room.
’Twill now be worth one’s while to live ;
New hopes and aspirations buoy us 1
I will a hundred kopeks give
To mark appreciation joyous,
And help to raise a sculptured stone,
A pension, and eternal free board
For him who by hi* grace alone
Has filled the want that's longest known
With his amazing silent keyboard.
That some must write while others play
Has been the rule in every lodgings,
And wails of tortured airs would stray
To plague the bards, despite their dodgings.
Now give the violin that’s dumb,
A ’cello that’s the merest lisper,
The still bassoon, the noiseless drum,
The cornet that will never hum
Above the faintest, furthest whisper 1
Do this, 0 rare philanthropist,
Hob of their sting the parties choral,
Let only silent songs exist,
And on your brow be all the laurel 1
’Twill but remain for us to see
Your keyboard fathered by the nation;
Then give it out to players free,
And make its use compulsory
By unrelenting legislation!

“Peter” : The Italian Opera Co. now again
in Melbourne is an enthusiastic customer of
a Bourke-street small-goods emporium, but
never has enough English to satisfy its appe-
tite. The other day a member of the co.
wandered into the shop and tried hard to
indicate his favorite nourishment. His frag-
ment of English failing him, and his gestures
still leaving his hunger unappeased, he sud-
denly had the bright idea of grunting while
planting one large Italian hoof on the
counter and pointing at it in an eloquent
manner. The shopkeeper immediately
understood. He wanted pigs’ feet.

’“Bill Bowyang” : 1 know something about
bayonet fighting now, and declare that half
of the stuff that used to be drummed into the
recruit in military camps in Australia was
rot. Two weeks after I landed at Anzac I
pulled up in front of a burly Turk —one of
the Constantinople Fire Brigade, and they
were big brutes. There was no time for
right or left parries which we had been
taught to use ’way back in Liverpool. To
have adopted such tactics would have given
Abdul the opportunity he was looking for.
1 thought of a couple of boxing feints I had
learnt in Snowy Sturgeon’s rooms in Bris-
bane years back. As a result 1 surprised
the Turk and punctured him in the ribs.
A cobber following behind
finished the job. The chap
with a knowledge of boxing
easily makes the best bay-
onet fighter. He is provided
against almost every eventu-
ality—even against that
cunning ruse practised by
the Iluns upon the Anzacs
at Pozieres. They advanced
with hands uplifted, shout-
ing “Kamerad,” and then, as
Billjim lowered his bayonet-
point, threw their arms in
seeming gratitude around the
Anzae’s neck. As the Ger-
man arms fell upon Bill-
jim’s shoulders they tight-
ened, and another Hun
would snatch up the rifle
and bayonet that poor old
Bill had .dropped and stab
him through the back. Now,
even though Billjim is locked
in the German embrace, if
he is a boxer he has half a
dozen methods of adminis-
tering to the Hun the treat-
ment designed for himself,
and that without trying to
escape from the grip. The
boxing brain has adopted
tricks from the hempen
square whereby even an un-
.armed man may conquer an
armed opponent and possess
himself of his enemy’s
weapon. I have seen it done
in half a dozen different
ways, by small men against big, by weak men
against strong. And fr >m our point of view
the beauty of it is that the German never is
a boxer.

“Trooper Bluegum” : Come over to Egypt
for camels on a string. Through the glasses,
just after Romani, I figured that 1 saw a
team of, as I thought, 1000.. Archer, of
Grassmere (Q.), plumped for 1200, while
Stuckey, of Mount Gambier (S.A.), opined
that there were 1400. Mclvell, of Fiji,
looked longest and estimated 1600. I
hurriedly adjourned then for a long
beer, but came back and had an-
other look. The string was still winding in
towards El Ferdan. My final estimate was
2000. Anyhow, I’ll swear to 1500. liici-
dentally. you should keep iu mind that the
Imperial Camel Corps which biffed Abdul so
heartily at Katia, Maglidaba and Rafa was
more than half. Australian men, and Eng-
land does not know that yet. Come to think
of it, there are lots of things that England
does not know yet.
“Gunner”: A sentry standing easy at
Liverpool camp gate did not salute his
superior officer. Then this: —
The Officer: You are the sentry ?
Sentry: Yes, sir.
Officer: Do you know your duties?
Sentry: Yes, sir.
Officer: Do you know you should salute your
superior officer?
Sentry: No, sir.
Officer: How long have you been in camp?
Sentry: Two or three days, sir.
Officer: What company do you belong to?
Sentry: The Gas Company, sir.

From the Office Curate: —
There’s no need for a Sydney-sider to go to
Queensland for a good sectarian shindy. He can
find one raging right at his front door, where Arch-
gaiters Wright is doing his little bit to win the war
and promote national unity by keeping the home
fires .of sectarianism burning. It began at a Bible
Society’s meeting in Sydney Town Hall, where Dr.
Wright delivered from the platform a lengthy eulogy
of W. M. Hughes. The Archgaiters has lately dis-
covered in Hughes, the partner of Joe Cook, many
virtues to which the archiepiscopal eye was formerly
blind in the president of the Waterside Workers’
Federation. By way of supplying some sort of
relevancy between his speech and the occasion on
which it was delivered, he shed a few tears over the
alleged fact that most of the opposition to Hughes
came “from a great communion in our midst which
denies the Bible to its people.” It was well-worn
Orange fiction, but it did its work, and started the
flames. Next, in case the conflagration should burn itself
out without doing enough harm, the Archgaiters
dropped oil on the embers by preaching an election-
eering sermon in his cathedral under the guise of
a Lenten meditation. According to Sydney Tele-
graph, which gleefully gave his inflammatory
remarks three cross-heads on its centre page
(19/3/’l7), he told his drowsy congregation:
“Labor is supported apparently by the whole strength
of the Roman Catholic Church, and Rome always
has its price. .. .1 object to the domination of Rome,
and I distrust it.... Most serious would it be if it
dominated the Government. .. .Such considerations
make me decide to vote unhesitatingly for the
National party, and I hope that no ope will fail to
register a vote, and I hope, also, a vote for the
National party, if the considerations I have referred
to are worth anything.” A good section of Dr.
Wright’s own clergy and people haven't forgiven
him for the calamitous “vestments” squabble which
he stirred up just after his importation seven years
ago, and they aren’t likely to. take very seriously his
attempt to influence their votes; but the sectarian
devil will riot with fresh vigor after thus receiv-
ing his blessing. What effect it will have on the
Church is another matter. There are tens of thou-
sands of Australian people who were looking upon it
rather favorably as an institution whose mission it
was to preach Christ. If it can without protest be
prostituted so that it preaches Christ and Billy
Hughes, or Christ and Bigjohn Forrest, or Christ and
the 1.W.W., it seems to be quite hopeless to expect
any future for it.
Sydney Equity Court lias as last gut rid
of a case that has been worrying it for
years. A Chinese, who came from Canton,
died and left £3353, which he bequeathed
vaguely to "the hospital at Canton, China.
First the children tried to upset the will
on several grounds and failed; then the
hearing of the Public Trustee’s request for
instructions had to be held over while some-
one went and counted the hospitals in Can-
ton. Last week the Court gave its decision
that the money must go to - the Canton
Hospital, the Bund, Canton, along with its
regrets that, owing to the testator having
died prior to the date to which the measure
was made retrospective, the Testators’
Family Maintenance Act could not be held
to apply. So, if the Commonwealth Govern-
ment doesn’t intervene, the three thousand-
odd will go out of Australia at a time when
cash is short. There won’t be any more
joss-sticks burnt before dad’s photograph
in that household.

“Bill Bowyang” (London) : Over this way
the A.S.C. is now enlisting women as drivers.
But I’m told that they are not aroused in
the morning by the familiar old barrack-
room shout of “Come on, there! Show a
leg!” • ' »
“Quidam”: If, as many people expect,
hard times are to come to Australia, and the
easy, loan-fed conditions of so many years
are to be exchanged for a period in which
pretty well everyone will take a hole up in
his belt, they may at least have the effect of
putting some ginger into a lot of the young
men of the present day. A very large per-
centage of them appear to exist in a condi-
tion of suspended animation. Twenty years
ago a camp of young fellows working in the
bush was a cheerful place, and slight hard-
ships were not worried about. Nowadays
only middle-aged or married men appear to
get any pleasure out of roughing it. Big,
strong, single young fellows seem to get
pleasure only in being miserable. They can’t
stand “batching,” can’t stand the loneliness,
can’t stand the life and can’t stand anything
except drawing their pay, and that is never
enough. If a young chap with only himself
to keep loses half a day through rain, he
groans about it as if somebody had
drunk his beer, though lie is getting 10s. or
11s. a day. The price of everything is a
robbery, the rate of wage a scandal. Every-
thing should be legally cheapened—except
wages, which should be put up. At the same
time, if it is suggested to one who is appar-
ently irrevocably ruined because he has lost
half a day during the week that, if he likes,
he can put it in on Saturday, it is shown
that it is morally and physically impossible
for him to work on Saturday afternoon.
Compared with their fathers, the plucky and
energetic cockies and diggers and bush-
workers who faced hardships like men and
carved out homes and brought up large
families on less income than the poorest
rouseabout now has, they are a discontented,
growling, gloomy and cantankerous lot, a
disgrace to a country that has plenty of room
WHILE THE MISSUS FRIES THE
BACON.
and plenty of rewards for men with grit
enough to get away from the towns and
battle with life. If hard times compel the
young man of to-day to forget what a dread-
ful burden life is, and inspire him to bustle
around like his dad did (not forgetting
“mum” either), they will be by no means an
unmitigated disadvantage. If not, the young
groucher will have to be dynamited out of
the road, or Australia will not prosper as it
should.
CLOTHES AND THE MAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A nip is in the air,
The evening breeze blows chilly;
The costumes of the fair
Grow sober and less frilly ;
My tailor pipes a stave—
Seductive are his flirtings—
Ere Summer’s in its grave,
Orders “esteemed” he’ll crave
For Autumn-Winter suitings.
Declining is my biz,
Bills on my head are pouring.
What odds if coal has riz,
That gas is skyward soaring?
What though my purse grows lean
Through these eternal lootings'?
Cash, to the last, last bean,
Must be laid out, I ween,
On Autumn-Winter suitings.
The season’s at “the Fall.”
The seedy-suited Adam
Eve’s dress-accounts appal—-
Sealskins demandeth madam !
Hard-up he curses war’s
Upheavals and uprootings,
And as “the Court’s” grim doors
lie nears, his tailor roars
His Autumn-Winter suitings.
VICTORIA.
EARDLEY TURNER.

ITEMS
The Ngatimamoe, Waitaha and Ngaitaliu
tribes of Maoriland lost their big chief the
other day, when Taame Haereroa Parata,
M.L.C., passed out at his home in the South
Island (M.L.). lie was the direct descend-
ant of Tamatea, the famous old Maori navi-
gator who skippered the canoe Takitimu
from Hawaiki. “Tommy” represented the
Southern Maoris in the M.L. Representa-
tives for 27 years. In 1912, when the Upper
House coo-eed, the Maoris of the South
selected the old chief’s son Rakatauhake to
fill his chair in the House of Reps. Our
departed brother was 81 when the black
canoe came for him. *
On the unity of Australian origin, as con-
trasted with the mixed breeding of the
U.S.A., Professor Scott, in his new “History
of Australia,” points out that from the very
beginning of responsible government there
has not bbeh a Premier in any of the six
States, oi* a Commonwealth Prime Minister,
with a mune of foreign derivation ; and the
same thing applies to every one of the 50
elected members of the Conventions of 20
years ago that framed the Commonwealth
Constitution.

“Bob” Smith, perhaps Sydney’s best-
known solicitor, died last week. For many
years past he had been a member of the firm
of Norton, Smith and Co. James Norton,
the founder, was admitted to practice in
England, and arrived in Sydney on the ship
Maria, carrying female exiles, in September.
ISIS. When he died in ’62 his son carried
on the business in company with the late
William Earner. On the dissolution of that
partnership James Norton carried on the
business alone, until he was joined by W. C.
Westgarth, O. S. Sanders and Robert Smith.
The firm is now the oldest in the six-and-
eightpenny business in Sydney, Allen and
Allen coming next.
“Fleecer” on a much-discussed Hun :
I’m tired of watching people shudder at mention
'of the name of Edwards, formerly Eichengruen, of
the Continental Tyres, who is glibly set down as a
German spy. From a first-hand knowledge of the
circumstances, let me point out 'that it was the
NO LAUGHING MATTER.
S HE: “I read in a magazine that indiges-
tion could be cured by laughter.”
HE: "Probably, but it's so difficult to see
the humorous side of it.”
Cook-Deakin Administration that gave Edwards a
commission, and the said commission was doubtless
granted in the full knowledge of what little use it
was likely to prove. The grandiloquent “Australian
Volunteer Automobile Corps” was formed by cer-
tain members of the motor and allied trades, pos-
sibly in the hope that some cars and equip-
ment would later be sold to the Defence Department.
The Melbourne “division” of the “Corps” consisted
of 15 traders, all of whom, with two exceptions,
were lieutenants, The other two were majors. You
couldn’t be a private in that “Corps” no matter how
hard you tried. Edwards was the leading man in the
lyre trade, and couldn’t afford to remain outside
the “Corps” from a business point of view—nor
could the,other members keep him out, as a matter
of fair play. The members were really glorified chauf-
feurs, whose military duties consisted*; of driving, a
budding general or field-admiral or something from
point to point. All the military secrets which any
“lieutenant” had the chance of acquiring can to-day
be learned quite as easily by any ordinary car-owner.
So much was the “Auto Corps” valued by, the mili-
tary authorities, that it was disbanded within a
month of the outbreak of war. Incidentally, the
name Edwards, was adopted by this man’s father,
who probably got tired of hearing people mangle his
real label. .

In .a- couufqrtable. artistically-furnished cottage in
a quiet street* at Neutral Bay reside two daughters
of the late Henry Barkcs, each battling successfully
with the world and its ways. One lias a typing

F. 10. Horan, solicitor, who had spent
many years at Inglewood (Vie.), died a few
days ago at Albert Park, Melbourne. Horn
in Dublin 72 years ago, be cairn* to Victoria
with bis mother and three sisters when be
was eight. A few years later be was sent
to St. Patrick's College; among bis school-
fellows there were John Madden and brother
Frank, now Speaker of the Leg. Assembly.
Horan was admitted to the Vie. Bar in
1878. his admission being moved by his old
school-clmm Madden. Next he was a clerk
in the Crown Law Offices at Melbourne; bnt
after a while he tried his luck at Inglewood
as clerk of court. The man who encour-
aged him to go there was W. 11. Coffey, who
afterwards became a Judge in N. S. Wales.
Four years afterwards, Coffey sold his prac-
tice to Horan and left Inglewood. The new
man stuck to his job on the old goldfield till
a few months ago, when, his health failing,
he sold out.
Concerning our departed brother: —
Tommy Parata, M.L.C., who died in Maoriland
the other day, had socked it into every' Government
good and hard over Maori rights ever since 1885,
Taame had been a toiler in his day by sea and land.
He was born with the perfume of whale oil in his
nostrils; his father was an old-time New Bedford
whaling skipper named Trapp, who bossed a 350-ton
barque in southern seas and spent a' profitable season
in 1536-37 round Foveaux Strait way. He married
a Ruapuke Island woman. Soon after Taame
appeared on the scene he was sent off to Johnny
Jones’s whaling station at Waikouaiti, the hard-case
place where a shocked missionary was Informed that
“Sunday never comes into this yere blanky bay.”
In the ’seventies Taame ran a whaling station there
himself, after doing a turn in the pilot-boat at
Otago Heads and a spell of bullock-punching in the
Backblocks. The old man leaves a fat little farm
nearby the old blubber-slicing camp, a top-notch
collection of ancestral greenstone head-smashers, and
a handsome family well distributed over the ferny
islands.
Canon P. A. Micklem, of Brisbane, chosen
to superintend the collection-plate at tony
St. James’s, Sydney, is a dapper, smooth-
faced cleric a little over sft. high in his
boots, but a good head and shoulders aboA'e
the average Anglican parson in scholarship
and ability. Born in the Cold Country 37
years ago, Micklem Avas schooled at HarroAv,
and won an open scholarship at Oxford,
where he studied other things besides the
bleat, leaving with the highest honors
(“double first”) which Oxford can give.
Cuddesdon College then trained liim for the
Anglican priesthood. After a spell as a
master at Harrow, and another as a curate,
Micklem settled down at St. Augustine’s
College, Canterbury (Eng.), helping to train
missionary priests under Bishop Knight—
an Australian, bom at Bega (N.S.W.).
Seven years ago, when Archbishop Donald-
son and the other Anglican gaiters in
Queensland went searching for the best man
Anglicanism could produce to boss its Col-
lege at Nundah (Q.), in the hope of raising
the low standard of parsonical education in
this country, they imported Micklem for the
billet. As all his able-bodied students are
now wearing khaki, and as Anglican bishops
iu Queensland have agreed not to ordain
men of military age till the job of putting
the Kaiser in his place is finished, Micklem
has had nothing to do at the college lately.
He has filled in time by writing a volume of
the big Westminster biblical commentary
which he was engaged by the London pub-
lishers ‘to prepare. Sydney Anglicanism,
which runs mostly in a narrow Orange
groove, will look sideways at him, for he
will be too brainy and broad-minded to re-
peat its insectarian party formulas; but he
is a sure tip for a mitre somewhere in Aus-
tralia within the next feAV years.

In the early coal-mining days of the Illa-
Avarra district trade hinged largely upon the
eccentric movements of a boat named the
William the Fourth (the first ocean steamer
built in N. S. Wales, a.d. 1831). Conse-
quently the men Avere often in doubt at
nightfall as to whether there Avould be work
on the folloAving day. There were no steam
Avhistles to call them; but one of the pits
employed .as under-manager a deep-lunged
Geordie who, whenever he possessed reliable
information, made it
a practice, in the still-
ness of the night, to
clamber to the top of
the stack heap, like a
rooster surmounting a
dunghill, and crow:
“WaAA'k tomowah!
W a av k tomowah !
Wawk tomowah!”
After Ayliich he would
preen his wing feathers,
and strut back to his
fireside. Old hands de-
clare that when the
wind was favorable,
“the gaffer” could be
heard in the Market
Square. Wollongong,
five miles away. But
you can’t always be-
lieve the old hands
anymore than you can
the new.

“Tot” Hall, whom nobody had ever called
Anthony James Alexander since his christ-
ening, died at Broken Hill the other day;
and thereby the big, convivial mining town
lost its best after-dinner speaker. He was
kept in practice till Bright’s disease sud-
denly cplled for him; the night before his
fatal seizure he had done brilliantly at a
send-off to his old friend Avery, who was
quitting the Silverton Tramway’s local ser-
vice to follow the late C. F. MacDonald as
secretary of the co. in Melbourne. Because
he was a good sport and arrived in Broken
Hill with the blessings of his brother’s part-
ner, Charles Kingston, the unions mostly
turned to Hall for advice —and that dated
from the time when Sleatli, Ferguson and
half a dozen others were taken to Deniliquin
25 years ago and tried there for conspiracy.
Very few of the lawyers engaged in that big
trial are left. Jack Want, who “led” for the
prisoners, and Coffey (afterwards Judge),
who talked to the jury like a father on the
other side, went long ago ; only his Honor
Backhouse, who was given a Supreme Court
commission for the job, has grown no older.
Killed in action in Egypt, Corporal Frank
Robert Morse, Camel Corps, second son of
the late Captain F. A. Morse, D. 5.0., of
Bellinger River (N.S.W.), aged 21 years.
Ho left Australia with the Ttli Light Horse,
and at The Landing was in the third boat,
and had to climb over the dead body of the
first lad to get in.
Artist Will Dyson, after buzzing about
Salisbury Plain and other English places
where material was to be picked up, now
finds himself “somewhere in France,” and a
full lieutenant. Lieut. Bill has been in the
thick of things, and finds the magnitude of
them artistically overwhelming. A long
hunt for Australian friends brought him in
touch with Australian Ted Colles —just then
in a particularly nasty hole which Fritz was
plastering day and night with blazing shells.
“O.K.’s” send-off to a person of no,
account: —
An Annus of last week announced “the death of
Sir George Clietwvnd, a well-known racehorse owner
of 30 yea rs ago. He married,” it added,
“the widow of the fourth Marquis of Hastings,
known as ‘The Pocket Aenus,’ at a time
when she was engaged to be married to Mr.
Henry (now Lord) Chaplin.” The fact of the old
matter is that the reckless young Marquis of Hast-
ings eloped with Lady Florence Paget when she was
under contract to marry -Henry Chaplin, and young
George Chetwynd married the little woman when she
was widow of the mined marquis. Henry Chaplin
was then much too wise to want her. Sir George
-Chetwynd, as I remember him in his honeymoon days,
was a good-looking young greenhorn, -whilst his tiny
bride was already a faded bit of goods. Her
notoriety had evidently been her attraction for the
boy' who had become “the marchioness’s husband."
Chetwynd was certainly no catch. lie owned race-
horses and figured scandalously. For a time he ran
after Mrs. Langtry, and caused a passing sensation
by assaulting one of bis rivals in a public place.
Subsequently he sued the Earl of Durham for £20,000
damages in a racing libel case, and came out of it
with a farthing solace for his wounded feelings. His
wife took to piety and survives the scapegrace, I
fancy. Their only' son married a Yankee heiress, who
divorced him.
BILLJIM, THE VIRTUOUS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Investigating the conditions of London streets,
Scotland Yard reports highly, favorably of the Aus-
tralians, whose glamor attracts respectable girls
without any evil consequences.— Cable.
When Billjim takes his walks abroad,
With noble- pride he stalks abroad,
Though girls about him clamor;
Ilis manner is so haughty,
He chokes off Flossies naughty;
About him is a glamor.
Though all are quite selectable girls,
He much prefers respectable girls.
With head held perpendicular
Goes Billjim, most particular,
Through Waterloo’s environs.
No fear of his enslavement
To ladies of the pavement—
He doesn’t like those syrens,
Those easily collectable girls;
He asks for nice respectable girls.
Though Flossie tries to cuddle him
And with strong liquors muddle him,
He chucks her for that other—
The modest English maiden
With all the virtues laden,
And always with her mother.
They’re meek, demure, dissectable girls';
But, then, they are respectable girls.
Although these English misses are
Not backward where the kissgg are,
I think that you will find him,
Despite the fuss and petting,
Not in the least forgetting
The Kid he left behind him.
At home he has delectable girls
Who knock out those respectable girls!
N.SAV.
A. H. A.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Mutual Life and Citizens’ office is
responsible for the brightest idea in connec-
tion with war finance yet born in Australia.
In a brief circular to be issued to every
policy-holder John J. Garvan, the managing-
director, says:—■
The Mutual Life and Citizens’ has, as .you probably
know, shared substantially in financing the Com-
monwealth Expeditionary Forces, having subscribed
£1,250,000 to the Commonwealth War Loans. Yet
1 feel that we, as citizens of the Empire, cannot
claim to have clone enough to maintain and consoli-
date that freedom under which our country has pros-
pered, and for which the calls are specially urgent
at this juncture.
There are some 100,000 policy-holders in this office
sharing in the profits, and a vbry small contribution
by individuals amounts to a very large sum in the
aggregate. I do not suggest any cash subscription,
but if you would be prepared to devote a year’s
reversionary bonus to aiding the forces at the Front,
the result would be very appreciable.
Field-Marshal Sir Douglas Haig only a few days
ago said, “Our two most serious preoccupations at
the present* time are railways and artillery,” and
again he said, “We must have more artillery.”
If our policy-holders would agree to apply their
share of this year’s profits to the equipment of one
or more brigades of artillery, we would be able to
present our forces in the field with a most valuable
contribution. Some policy-holders like to
apply only a portion of, and others even more than,
a year’s bonus by using some of the bonus already
attaching to their policies.
It is important that the matter be dealt with
quickly, and if you are prepared to co-operate will
you please sign and return attached form to me.
Should the response of policy-holders be whole-
hearted I will be prepared to take action on their
behalf. On receipt of sufficient replies from policy-
holders the company will be asked to pay the amount
into the fund created for the purpose. This fund
will be known as the “Mutual Life and Citizens’
Artillery Fund,” and a committee of the directors
will report to you as to its disposal for the purposes
named and to the best advantage.
Thus the policy-holder is asked, not to pay
anything out of pocket, but to relinquish
for one year the profits which in ordinary
circumstances would go to make an addition
to his insurance. But the gift cannot be
made on a majority vote or anything of
that kind. Whether sufficient for the pur-
pose is available depends therefore upon the
individual policy-holder. If Brown or Smith
does not send back the authority to use the
profits in respect of his own policy for the
suggested purpose, those particular profits
cannot be thus employed. There are no
doubt many policy-holders who will be pre-
pared to relinquish more than one year’s
bonus additions. Some will think it a fair
thing to yield up to the service of the coun-
try the profits made since January 1. 1915
—the beginning of the year after the war
started. If there is unanimous approval,
the office will be able to hand over about a
quarter of a million. Putting it on the
very lowest plane, it will probably pay the
policy-holder very well to do it. A scheme
of this kind, if it is carried to a successful
issue, will unquestionably benefit the office;
and that will mean bigger business and
larger bonuses in future.
The City Mutual Life Assurance
Society (Sydney) :

*This includes “premiums purchasing paid-up poli-
cies allocated from cash bonuses not drawn.”
+This includes “cash bonuses”—£l7,22l for 1911,
£19,512 for 1912, £22,770 for 1913, £28,317 for
1914, £33,687 for 1915, and £38,472 for 1916.
The “balance of undrawn cash bonus applied
in purchase of reversionary bonuses”—that
is, cash bonuses used to purchase paid-up
insurance—was until two or three years ago
included with the single-premium income.
Since the two have been distinguished the
bonus figures have been as follows: —•
Bonus applied
Total Bonus to Purchase
Declared. Insurance.
1914 .. .. £28,317 £26,979
1915 . . . . 33,687 31,995
1916 . . . . 38,472 37,036
The employment of so fine a proportion of
the bonus earnings to purchase more insur-
ance is to the credit of the office; for
the policy-holder says in so many words: "I
have had experience of these people, and.
instead of taking the cash they offer me, I
am content to increase my insurance with
them.” Let us deduct from the income the
£37,036 being the amount of cash-bonus, and
£216,927 remains as the true income to com-
pare with £76,874 of 10 years ago.' As the
office was established in 1579 the perform-
ance of the past 10 years is all the more
remarkable by comparison. There is no
doubt that the' big bonuses have done it.
They haven’t done anything extraordinary in
the way of capturing new business, but they
have had great influence in keeping the old
business. This supports The Bulletin’s
old idea, that the Maoriland Government
Life Office —the one general office owned and
run by the State in Australasia —might do
far better if, instead of going into expensive
competition with private offices, it settled
down as a non-canvassing office. This would
straightway double the bonuses; and if that
fact were made well known among policy-
holders, the result would be a very fair flow
of new business at practically no expense at
all. And every sixpence saved in getting
business is sixpence earned for bonuses.
Bonuses are drawn from three sources:
(1) from I’unning the business cheaper
than was provided for when the tables
of premiums were drawn up; (2) from
larger interest earnings than had been cal-
culated; (3) from such careful selection of
lives that the policy-holders go on paying
premiums for more years than had been
reckoned on. This co. has made exception-
ally fine bonus-profits from its investments.
Interest earnings (including £3OOO profit on
sales) during the year totalled £50,368,
which is equal to a shade over £5 15s. on
the mean of the insurance fund. The actu-
ary rarely assumes interest earnings of more
than 3:J p.c. when making his calculations;
and the extra 2 p.c. in this case means
nearly £17,500 of profit available for
bonuses. Very properly, .however, the £3OOO
derived from the sale of securities is not
divided among policy-holders, but is trans-
ferred to -a contingency fund, which now
amounts to £35,500. This has to protect a
more varied lot of investments than is pos-
sessed probably by any other life office in
Australia. The management has frankly
thrown overboard a lot of old ideas, and,
while keeping in mind that the society is a
trustee, has also proceeded on the principle
that profits are as welcome to insurance
policy-holders as to anybody else. Its size
has also helped. In a very large concern
some hard-and-fast rules have to be laid
down, because business involving risk may
have to be completed by a dozen or a hun-
dred men ; but in a concern of this size wide
discretion is allowable because practically
all matters of any importance can be
decided at headquarters. So the policy-
holder gets the benefit of many a sweet little
bit of business that others might turn down
because it was either too small or too un-
usual. _ Yet by far the greater part of the
funds is in the old familiar lines:—
Government stock .. £199,294 = 20.2 p.e.
Mortgages .. .. 437,970 = 44.4 „
Freeholds . . . . 148,878 = 16.1 „
Loans on Policies .. 67,654 = 6.8 ~
That leaves only 13.5 p.c. for the 'miscel-
laneous collection, including stocks and
shares in public cos., cash and outstanding
premiums.
There is one entry on the liabilities side of
the balance-sheet which would have shocked
the old insurance man who would do nothing
that his father hadn’t done first —“Deposits
bearing interest;” It is in this case a very
small amount, £5258. But why shouldn’t it
be there? And why shouldn’t it be three,
four, 20 times as big. Take this case to illus-
trate what The Bulletin said the other
day about the possibility of getting a large
sum of War Loan money through the insur-
ance offices —life-insurance offices in particu-
lar. Run the eye down the column showing
the amount added to the insurance fund year
after year—by thousands, 42, 67, 71, 81,* 80,
106. With just as much certainty as any-
thing can be promised, this co. will add an
average of at least £lOO,OOO a year to its
funds in the next three years. It will want
permanent investments for that money; and
if anybody wants temporary investments,
there can be no reason in the world why the
money should not be lent to this insurance
office for investment in the War Loan. The
co. can’t take up the loan because it hasn’t
the money, though it will have £300,000 to
invest in just such a security within "the
next three years; and the people who have
the money won’t invest it because they don’t
want permanent investments. The problem
is to bring this unused money into use in the
War Loan. It might be done, no doubt, by
direct short loans to the insurance offices;
but that always stirs up the banks, and it
may not be a good thing to antagonise them
in this national crisis. Rut if the banks
can’t suggest a plan by which their current
account money can be put to better use, and
also a plan by which people who know they
will accumulate certain sums in the next two
or three or five years can be financed to in-
vest those accumulations in advance, then the
matter will have to be taken out of their
hands.

This co. has put a lot of spirit into its
operations. The old firm covered only
Sydney and Newcastle. Then Brisbane was
included; after that. Melbourne; next a
branch was opened in London; and during
the past year the well-established ship-
chandlery business of James Clark. Sydney
and Newcastle, has been taken over.' The
most striking thing about this last purchase
is that nothing is included among the assets
for goodwill. Either Paul and Gray got
the business at the cost of the tangible assets
or the goodwill was paid for out of an
internal reserve. Meantime, partly no doubt
because of this purchase and partly because
the co. has been putting up new bulk stores
m Sydney, the liabilities have moved off in
a hurry, though in the natural order of
things they should be reduced as prices fall
and stocks of the lately-purchased business
are sorted out. Here are six years’ figures:

lhe dash which the management has put
into the business can be judged by the fact
that total assets are now valued at £249,718
as against £133,070 six years ago. Stock-
in-trade has more than doubled —partly
because of the tremendous increase in prices;
partly because of the opening of the new
branches. The tremendous increase of
prices and the tendency of shipping to pay
any old thing for services .just at present
have made profit-earning easy. There is,
however, a risk —not very much perhaps—
that after the war prices will fall quickly,
and that this co. may make losses on its
heavy merchandise stocks. But you never
know in a case of this kind what the stock-
taking has been like. Certainly, the direc-
tors have had every encouragement to do
this important job on conservative lines.
Ihey pay 35 per cent, and make nearly
enough to pay another 6 p.c.; and a co.
with comparatively small reserves which
shows more than 21 p.c. is inviting trouble.
Incidentally the co. has provided only £1789
for income taxes for the year. It looks as
though there may be - a much larger slice
than that to provide for; and the more pru-
dent thing might have been to have trans-
ferred a bit of the Profit and Loss balance
to contingent account. The sheet wouldn’t
have looked quite so glittering; and it is
just as well to make sure*that—in your own
case at least—all that glitters is gold.

Gordon’s run a side-line to the. house-furnish-
ing; and the co. is reputed to have made a
lot of money out of it. The idea started with
the discovery that bad debts in the time-
payment trade are astonishingly few. Seven
years ago this commentator was invited to
compare the losses in a well-known time-
payment and an almost equally well-known
ordinary retail business, doing a rather high-
class trade. He found the losses in the latter
50 p.c. higher than in the time-payment con-
cern. Making use of its inquiry system and
its machinery for collecting instalments, this
co. started selling cash-orders. In. other
words, instead of buying goods on time-pay-
ment from Gordon’s you can buy them else-
where and pay for them by instalments to
Gordon’s. Apparently most of the orders
are used to purchase drapery; and many of
them are bought by “the missus” without
the old man’s knowledge.
Theoretically, however, the losses ought to
be heavy; and the management has all along
done the correct thing in providing for the
contingency. In the table at the top of these *
remarks this contingency fund is included
with the reserves, which means that the
amounts added to the fund have been treated
as profit. The next table shows how the
directors separate the actual profit from what
may be profit if things go right

As usual, the profits would have been
better if something else had happened. Our
profits always would be better if everything
came our way. This time “the film business
is still seriously affected by the duty on
films” and “the dividends from Union
Theatres have been substantially less on
account of that co. having had to assist in
financing large film contracts, and in conse-
quence this co.’s full share of profits has not
been received.” The same story, with small
variations, is told every year; and it is
almost time the cos. in the Comliine realised
that the latter’s appetite for so-called profits
is a permanent thing. The co.’s worst in-
vestment appears to have been the Crystal
Palace. The net loss last year was £23 ; the
year before £1932. That improvement took
some of the sting out of the fall
in this co.’s share of the Combine’s
dividends —from £13,706 in 1915 to
£8287 in 1916. The co. is much over-capi-
talised, and there is the poorest sort of secur-
ity for the great bulk of the principal. But
there are going to be good profits in the
picture-business; though what might look a
good profit to one showman would be utterly
miserable to another, like this, with a heavy
goodwill loading. That’s how Greater J.D.W.
and several other of the old. concerns are
handicapped in competition with newcomers.
* * * *
The resurrected Mount Hope (N.S.W.),
which has been waiting for the extension of
the Condobolin railway to the Matakana
siding, ought to see traffic opened that far
some time this month. The country out that
way is very flat, and the unusually heavy
rains have flooded a lot of the permanent
way, which originally was too low ; hence a
long delay. Meantime the pot has been kept
boiling at the mine by the leaching of the
old tailings. New concentrating and leach-
ing plants have been erected. While adjust-
ments are being made 30 tons of 6 p.c. ore
are being handled per day at the mill and
worked up to about 22 p.c. This output will
be doubled when the mill is doing full duty,
and the leaching vats will produce about 30
tons copper per month. The total output
then should be 75 tons or more a month.
The mine has opened up very satisfactorily.
At the 270 ft. (bottom) level the payable ore
has been proved 300 ft. long; it averages,
apparently, 4ft., and is worth p.c. as an
average. The co. got to work on the under-
standing that a spur would be run out to the
main line; and, though the Government is
short of loan money, it admits that this line
is one that ought to be built, partly because
the Allies want copper badly and partly
because the Condobolin extension wants
freight badly.

The Carpathia, Ardletlian (N.S.W.),has
been feeding its mill at Barmedman with
about 70 tons of crudes per week; and the
stock of milling ore at the mine on Feb. 28
was 872 tons. Assays are not available, but
apparently about 32 tons of crudes are re-
quired to produce a ton of concentrates,
which means a recovery of about 2 p.c.
metallic tin per ton. At the close of the
half-year (Jan. 31) the credit balance was
£3396. Carpathia paid a sixpenny on June
30, 1916, following a similar distribution in
December, 1915; and there is a sporting
chance of another very soon. There are
60,000 paids and 44,070 contribs.

The old Confidence and Pearl leases, etc.,
were secured by Dyason’s party, and the
allotment is made to them. Circulars have
now been issued to shareholders in all the
cos. informing them of the proposed alloca-
tion and appealing to them to support the
scheme. Already most of the cos. have
agreed to necessary alterations of their con-
stitutions ; and if the vote on that question
counts for anything, the scheme will fly
through—the figures were 730,000, practi-
cally in favor of amalgamation, to 22,000 on
other side.
“Old ’Un” : The job that Dyason’s Bendi-
go assessment committee took in hand has
been done in a workmanlike manner. It
had to cut up 1,000,000 shares among 36
cos. having 1,052,991 live shares of their
own. Many of these latter shares had been
picked up by Dyason’s syndicate at practi-
cally liquidation prices—not to "best” any-
body, but mainly to help the syndicate in
getting its scheme through. The committee
first of all disregarded market prices, and
thus did the right thing. Each co.’s assess-
ment was based on the value of the visible
assets and the possibilities of the mines.
Otherwise it would not have been possible for
Carlisle to mop up 97,380 shares against its
present register of 35,801 shares, nor could
such a forlorn market-derelict as United
Redans have been booked for 18,000 shares
against its register of 34,790. Dyason is
offering 400,000 contribs. to the public at
Is. on application and Is. on allotment, but
to guard against possible coldness the issue
lias been underwritten, with a promoter’s
guarantee that the whole of the ss. per
share shall be paid. This arrangement is
a novelty. It means that in a no-liability
co., even if the original applicant fails to
pay more than application and allotment
money, the promoter has to toe the mark
for the other 3s. For doing that he gets
100,000 paids, or the same commission as the
underwriter. As 26,567 shares are allotted
to Dyason Proprietary Co. and the old
managers of the absorbed cos., 373,433
shares are left to go into reserve.
There has been a lot of discussion over
the water in the deal. First, promoters get
£25,000 of shares for guaranteeing £60,000.
Then the underwriters are handed £25,000
of shares for taking up £40,000 of scrip.
Next £5OOO cash goes to brokers. The Teddy
Dyason Co. gets back £l6lO cash paid to
compensate legal managers for loss of jobs,
and £6641 in shares goes to the same co. to
be cut up under agreements with the same
crowd. Abbott, of Bendigo, is already sool-
ing the local vigilants on to protest ; but if
Swinburne,- Higgins and McConochie have
consented to the prospectus, that settles the
matter. There is no denying that the
“water” is pretty extensive; but the job
lias been the most difficult as well as the
biggest of the kind ever tackled under the
Southern Cross, and it has been handled
admirably. “Water” in this case therefore
mostly represents work.
* * * *
Catheart Victory (Vic.) wants to fill the
gap left by Catheart and Catheart Central.
The difference .is that these, two dead ’uns
had their own gutter, in which gold had been
followed from the grass roots to the Langi
boundary, whereas the Victory has yet to
pick up the long-lost Hopkins run of gold.
Its first shot off No. 1 shaft was a pool-
tiling. Balipg was left to be done with tanks
and the gutter couldn’t be followed far down.
No. 2 shaft had been sunk to within 70ft. of
the bottom, when a tired lot decided to make
no more calls till the war was over. As that
is still miles off the idea in business man-
ager P. P. Cook’s head is to renew his hustle
after the western run. The richness of the
old shallow ground about Ararat makes the
quest tempting.
* * ■ , * *
Shareholders of Coheirs Long Tunnel
(Vic.) are giving new manager Miller a
chance to make good. His first job has been
to do some driving and rising at Goodwin’s
shaft, where he is on 2ft. of stuff said to be
worth from 12dwt. to 15dwt. These figures are
much more likely to be near the mark than
former estimates. While this prospecting is
going on, the Board is looking for plant to
unwater the old main shaft of the Long
Tunnel to see if it is worth while taking
over that part of the property from Hannan
and party, who got 2700 z. from 190 tons.
Considering what a fiasco were Cohen’s first
crushings, the co.’s financial position isn’t
bad. as debts are under £l2OO, with very
little outstanding in the way of unpaid calls.
Associated Northern (W.A.) last year de-
rived £27,337 from its Ivalgoorlie lease.
This was £1378 better than the pre-
vious term’s figures. The revenue from
the re-treatment of sands shrank from
£15,318 to £8481; while tributox-s’ earn-
ings went from £10,641 to £18,856.
The co.’s Ora Banda property supplied
£61,046 worth of bullion, as against £46,703
the previous year. The co.’s profit was
£13,295 ; but this was reduced to £5364 by
writing off £7931 to cover depreciation.
Edna May Consolidated’s new reef at the
21Uft. level is being represented by the bulls
as the second-best deposit so far located at
Westonia. They are not far .wrong, either ;
but there is a big gap between the best and
second-best in this instance. Consolidated’s
reef is a repetition of the boomerang-shaped
occurrences previously located in Edna May
and Central; but the length of ore body is
likely to be less than in those cases, and as
regards depth the reef is underlying strongly
into Centi-al, and eventually will prove more
valuable to that company than to Consoli-
dated. A big crushing from Consolidated
will soon be treated at the Greenfinch mill,
and as there is some good ore in the mine
the yield should put heart into supporters.
But it won’t make the mine worth the pre-
sent market price.
With the exception of the Consolidated
flutter, life for the present has gone out of
Westonia and its stocks. Deeps is still sink-
ing (in more senses than one), and if share-
holders agree to put up the proposed addi-
tional ss. per share on 90,000 shares there
will be a suspicion that all the heroes are not
at the Front.
Westralian Mines Department has decided
to publish its geological reports in the un-
learned language of the prospector ; and that
will give them some practical value for the
first time.
* # * *
By treating 15,900 tons last half-year,
Mararoa, Norseman (W.A.), gathered in
£24,054; and it shelled out two divs. of 6d.
They made 28s. a share paid by the co. since
Rutter Clarke got it oil the stocks. There
is still £12,000 in the cash-box to play with,
as well as a fair tonnage of ore in the
Mararoa mine. Bill Henderson’s ore reserves
ought theoretically to have given out a year
ago, but the flat make lying between Nos. 5
and 6 levels north has, with the aid of the
poorer stuff from the other levels, kept the
battery going on 30s. stone for the half-year.
Even now Henderson gives hope of another
six-months’ run, so that the co. must have
with cash and stone well on the way to 4s.
per share. Money is to be spent on the
Broncho, of which claim Henderson says
that 1800 tons treated by the tributors gave
345. per ton. His bedrock requirement is 30s.
per ton, and he is spending £l2OO in.cross-
cutting and driving to see if the big ore body
will sample up to that figure. If the Mara-
roa exercises the option it will hand over
£lOOO in cash and 10,000 paids in a new co.
to the old Broncho shareholders —that is, if
Swinburne and J. M. Higgins permit the
contemplated little float.
* * * *
The value of the minerals produced in
Bananaland during 1916 was £4,021,007, or
£696,146 more than in the previous 12 months.
The increase was due solely to enhanced
market values, copper, for instance, showing
an advance of £836,629 on the 1915 figures,
though production decreased from 19.704 tons
to 19,520 tons. During last year Cloncurry
produced red metal worth £1,149,328, an
advance of half a million. The yield of wol-
fram for the year was also smaller, although
the total value showed an increase. A heavy
drop in gold and a, slight reduction in the
coal output are also recorded, whilst gem
picking seems to have revived somewhat.
• * * *
The Vulcan, Irvinebank (Q.), has struck
something that looks good at the 1060 ft.
level. The machines have broken in for over
Bft. on an Bft. shoot; so there is a chance
that the long-lookcd-for patch has come at
last.
# * * *
Latest word from the Palmer Gold Mines
(Q.) is that water at the rate of 50,000
gallons an hour is being lifted from the
Louisa shaft. It is just the luck of the
Cooktown people to get the plant running
when the biggest wet season known is on,
and their money—much of it wasted by
Government mismanagement —is nearly-
done.
“Platman”: Treasurer Theodore told a
deputation at Cairns (Q.) that the Govern-
ment intended to open the Chillagoe smelters
even if it did not buy the line. Certainly
were these smelters blown in a lot of little
shows would be working in the Herberton.
Irvinebank and Chillag.oe districts —if prices
keep up, and the men come back from the
war. But at present, under Mr. Hughes’s
“settlement of the metal problem,” Northern
silver-lead ore costs in freight, returning
charges, etc., from £9 to £lO per ton. Any-
thing over that the miner gets. But there’s
very little ore knocking about that’s worth
more than £lO per ten.
* * * *
Dhlmayne colliery (Tas.) is being pushed
in Collins-street now that it is in Alec Pea-
cock’s office. Two things help it. First, old
warrior William Orr has had a look over the
mine and does not seem disappointed. Next,
there is a whisper that the Victorian Gov-
ernment is in the field tc buy- 100,000 tons
of this coal at 9s. 6d. per ton delivered on
the wharf at Piccaninny Point. Provided
the steamer ring does not put up prohibitive
prices, the purchase looks good; for Dal-
mayne holds that it costs 14s. to land coal
in Melbourne from Wonthaggi and 17s. from
Newcastle. There is some talk of Dalmayne
having issued 200 first-mortgage debentures
to assist in financing the show, interest at
the rate of 8 p.c., with a bonus of 50 p.c. in
paid-up shares. An additional attraction, if
a conversion be made, is that 20s. shares
will be handed over at the rate of 16s. each.
*** - *
Westralian Minister for Mines Robinson
is being inundated with suggestions for dis-
cussion at the forthcoming mining confer-
ence. If all of them were considered, the
proceedings would probably last 12 months.
An agenda committee, representative of the
mine-owners, mine workers, prospectors and
Mines Department, has been appointed to
sift those worth discussing. Among the
matters to receive consideration are : (1)
State aid to prospectors on lines similar to
that in N. S. Wales; (2) the establishment
of mining boards; (3) the price of mining
requisites to be fixed by the Government;
(4) the scale of royalties to be fixed by Act
of Parliament, and no tributes to be_let for
a shorter period than 12 months ; (5) pro-
spectors of any new gold discovery to be
granted 24 acres, all other applicants to be
restricted to 12 acres, and labor conditions
to be complied with within 14 days; and
(6) that Government assistance to prospec-
tors for the purchase of plant be on the same
terms as those given to farmers. It is sug-
gested that money advanced by the State to
needy mineowners or prospectors be written
off if the leases are afterwards abandoned.

The extraordinary success and popularity of the
Babcock and Wilcox Water-tube Boiler, so univer-
sally used where the economy in steam-raising is con-
sidered, is due, no doubt, to the splitting up of the
water into 4in. tubes, which permit of rapid circula-
tion and excellent facilities for cleaning, the heavy
tin. plates used in the Shell Boiler being done away
with.—Advt.
