A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
* Sydney, March 19, 1917.
My Dear Arini, —
There was a great rush to stay away from
a meeting at which a few citizens decided
to form themselves into a committee to pro-
mote the Shackleton lecture. But the stam-
pede to hear the man himself made the Town
Hall feel like an over flowing herring-
barrel. Shackleton, after almost incredible
privations and anxieties, looks as solid and
nuggetty as ever. He besran by asking any-
one at the back of the premises who couldn’t
hear him to mention it. This was a cosy-
family-party way of beginning the evening
much appreciated by the people on the hin-
terland. Hurley, a Sydney, man, whose
photographs gave us vivid pictures on the
screen, caught the doomed Endurance in
every phase of its desperate struggle in the
ice. At last a hulk and some crumpled
hoop-iron, inky-black against the dazzling
white floes, flashed across the calico.
Governor Strickland and the Cullens took .
the best seats. The Tom Knox family, who
have stalked tlie Polar heroes for the last
week or two, brought skipner Davis in their
wake and carefully distributed him amongst
four womenkind. A handsome cheque goes
to the widow of Captain Mclntosh, who
died in a blizzard and now sleeps under the
eternal snow.
Mrs. Harley, the sister of Lord French,
who has been killed by a shell in Servia, was
the matron of the Scottish Women’s Hospi-
tal established there.- Olive Kelso King, of
Sydney, is in charge of one of the ambu-
lances belonging to this hospital; and rela-
tives of this war-chauffeur are now very
anxious on her behalf.
Governor Strickland is to open the Easter
Show. It will be his last big function
before the family takes the long wet trail
to England.
The G.-G. Fairguson and his lady are
likely to put in six weeks in N. S. Wales,
with old Admiralty House as their head-
quarters. The Fairguson takes the Easter
Show in the mood of a genuine Outbacker.
He wants, officially, to be let alone, and last
year went off on a solitary jaunt amongst
the stock, chatting to the men in charge
and personally conducting his own wander-
ings. Sometimes he loses his way amongst
the prime bacon in the raw; but there’s
always a friendly bobby to put him on the
track.
It takes a genuine lover of art to give
away a dozen Hilders in these times; but
that is what Howard Hinton, a Sydney art
collector, has just done. Thanks to him,
Sydney National Gallery—formerly very
badly off for examples—row has a represen-
tative panel of the dead artist’s work. The
generous gift (the second of its kind) fol-
lowed on the reading of a persuasive article
by Bertram Stevens in the first number of
“Art in Australia.”
Local society’s cars nerfumed Pitt-street
on the first night of “Daddy Long Legs” at
the Criterion. A display of arms and bare
shoulders quite unusual in these war-times
tottered on 4in. heels up the d.c. steps.
Charles Waldron, beloved of the gallery girl,
is no broader than of yore. Perhaps one
notices his slender champagne-bottle shoul-
ders more in these days of square-built men
in khaki; but the Squaw Man’s smile is in
the old place. Like Kathlene MacDonell (a
pleasant red-haired girl), Charles was seen
rather than heard most of the evening. Ex-
cepting. of course, in the front stalls, where
Mrs. Charles could hear her leading gentle-
man’s soft asides to Judy. In the stalls
Mrs. Harry Levy achieved the dazzling glory
of one of the latest evening wraps. It was
raven black with bits of brilliant color
stitched on like a flight of butterflies. Dr.
McKay, in a box, was surrounded by misty
clouds of tulle. In one diaphanous Aurora
Australis sat Mrs. Henry Osborne, who used
to be Marjory Lord. Mrs. 11. Gordon, with
a garland of tiny buds holding her flimsy
loveliness together, was the second Venus.
Hugh Ward, in the manager’s box, had
Kathlene MacDonell’s mamma—a white-
haired elegance with a slender figure—under
his wing. The usual joyous clatter fell
down from the gallery when Grace Palotta,
with her back hair embedded in a long tulle
bow, arrived with a fsiie buxom escort, the
Misses Lamb.
There’s only one thing I’d like to ask about
the flat first act of “Daddy Long Legs.”
Where are all the orphans that ought to be
cuffing each other over the gloomy tables full
of mugs? About four orphans and a half
made a very thin crowd around desolate
islands of crockery in the John Grier home.
Kate Rooney is going back to home and
hubby in New York towards the end of
April. Just now she is joyously rusticating
with her parents while son Billy fishes hap-
pily in a pail. Kate, who is Mrs. Kirkham
on the domestic hearth, looks well and bonny,
and has a peggybag full of interesting
reminiscences.
“In three years or so I expect we'll go
back to our real home at St. Margaret’s on the
Thames,” she says. “N’York spells business
for my husband, but there’s no place so good
to get out of in summer. The poor souls
who have to stick it out sleep on the fire-
escapes. Probably they don’t sleep. They
just drag their bedding to the little plat-
form outside their windows in the great tene-
ment buildings and fight for air. As for
the babies —well, if they want a breath
they must be taken to the nearest park—and
parks are not in every other square as in
Australian cities. Australians don’t realise
What a boon their balconies and verandahs
are. Our home in England,” Kate adds,
“was one of the houses in which Dickens
lived and worked, before he settled at Gad’s
Hill, lie is said to have written ‘Nicholas
Nickleby’ in it, and not far away you can
see from our windows the famous old Ham
Common.”
Sumner Locke has gone to Boston on the
way to N’York. Lately she hopes to break
through, submarines permitting, to London.
The Tom Hughes lady has been trying
Melbourne for change of scene after the
heartbreak over son Roger’s death in France.
There’s a small grandson now to bring
smiles through the mist.
Mrs. J. 11. P. Murray, wife of the Papuan
Excellency, was in London in January. She
dared the submarine, and saw her captain-
son’s wedding with Philippa Kitchener, a
niece of K. of K.
Dr. Trinca, who used to pet the germs at
the Alfred Hospital, has taken unto himself
an English wife. In the early days of the
war “Trine,” as he was known to intimates,
left his microscopes and incubators to sail
with the Navy.
The sudden death of “Tot” Hall, Broken
Hill’s* witty lawyer, gives Australia another
lonely widow. She had two sons. One was
killed in action a few months ago; the other
is at the Front.
Nina Murdoch, poet, presswoman, pretty
girl and good sort, is going to make one
J. D. M. Brown the happiest man anywhere.
Her verses have often glowed in The Bul-
letin. The bridegroom-elect is part-owner
of Coogee Preparatory School, and in his
spare time is also a journalist and sonneteer.
But, anyway, the young pair won’t toll the
tradespeople to .start delivering goods till
the last joint of nineteen-seventeen’s tail is
out of Sight. Nina, by the way, exuded
knowledge to a boys’ school in Darlinghurst,
before she gripped the pen for keeps.
to Tlios. J. McMahon, just back
from Far Northern parts, Mrs. Warrington
Rogers is the indomitable spirit of Aus-
tralia, materialised. “She’s absolutely fear-
less, and manages her husband’s cattle-
station for month after month. It takes him
most of the time to get his mobs to market;
without a white man or woman within a
hundred miles or more, this plucky little
lady then runs the station. She and her
lubras will tackle practically any job with
stock that any man can do.” She has also
wrung some kind of respect from blacks in the
Roper River country, and they are not too
pleasant sometimes. Other stations may be
in a chronic state of guerilla warfare with
cattle-spearing natives; but Paddy’s Lagoon
has little trouble with them. Mrs. Rogers,
whose one son is at Melbourne Grammar
School, hasn’t herself been into a shop for
10 years, and only sees another white face
now and again. Yet she is keen and femi-
nine, serves up, with the help of “Princess
Polly,” dainty afternoon-tea ; and isn’t above
asking her one or two visitors in a twelve-
month “What’s the latest cut in skirts, and
are crinolines coming in?”
Gwen Nelson, a daughter of the Herbert
Nelsons, of Cremorne, is going to U.S.A. by
an early boat. It’s the movies that call.
But she won’t have to struggle for the usual
heroine’s crust till she plays the leading
lady for the film.
The Macleod girls’ “evening” at Dunve-
gan, Mosman, in aid of the 17th Battalion
Comforts Fund, is to be on the 28th. Last
week I asked you to make a note of it for
Saturday, the 24th. Scratch that out, and
write it for the Wednesday following.
Etham, Darling Point, has changed
hands. It used to be the home of the mon-
eyed Mitchells; you’ll remember one of the
Gore girls became Mrs. Willie Mitchell, and
then a widow. She has changed her name
twice, I fancy, since then. Since the Mit-
chells passed out. Etham has been the pro-
perty of Matthew Harris. A lonely man
with wife dead and both daughters married,
Sydney’s ex-Mayor recently sold the big
house at a price that gives the buyer a war-
time bargain. But it’s going to take some
weeks to pack up the bric-h-brac which the
late dame Harris gathered from the four
corners of the earth. The gems of the
art collection have been handed over, as a
gift, to the National Gallery. But there
will still be left an amazing collection of old
furniture and cabinets that date back to the
days when Queen Anne was in the school-
rooms.
IL M. Sorner, enthroned in odd moments
behind a high office desk, is about the most
interviewed man in the city. The Easter
Show secretary has probably soothed or
cheered or applied the brake to quite 100
battalion comforts ladies. They’re after
tents and space, and the rush would over-
turn the beef and pumpkin brigade if—but
that’s where the soothing syrup arrives.
Finally, about a dozen battalions have
fought their way to the Front. The light-
ing-up this year at night, a Toronto Show
scheme advocated by Mr. Somer for several
years, adds to the comforts dames 1001
questions. A frenzied dash for lamps and
desperate consultations as to price have not
produced any casualties as yet. Prodded
as to the Women’s Industries section, the
discreet secretary remarks that it should be
an improvement on past years’ exhibits.
That wax flowers and antimacassars are
not yet extinct is merely hinted.
Victoria’s dashing amateur sportswoman,
Alice McCulloch, will clear the timber on
the Sydney Show Ground this year. Mrs.
Stace and the indomitable Dolly Fogg are
booked for the same events. But Miss Fogg
had an accident lately'; and her movements
are at present painful and uncertain.
War-correspondent Martin Donohoe, miss-
ing in Roumania a while ago, now pops up
with a cheerful grin as a lieut. attached to
the British Intelligence Corps in Roumania.
The Daily Chronicle makes up the differ-
ence in his salary till the enemy is whacked.
By the way, Mrs. Donohoe’s sister, Mrs.
Clunies Ross, will kiss a third son good-bye
next month. The war calls, and there are no
daughters to sit at home with the widowed
mother.
Artist Dattilo-Rubbo is parent to a new
son, who should be very handy as a perma-
nent model in the house studio. The new
addition is labelled Mark Antony, so he’s got
something to live up to in the years ahead.
Died of wounds—the third helping—in
France, George C. S. Miller, who wore stars
in our Field Artillery. He was the only
son of the Millers who live on Mosman
heights, and his years were but 25.
After two years of caring for active-
service souls, Captain-Chaplain Tighe has
come back on the invalid list'. He lectured
to his old R.C. parish at North Sydney last
week, when he had to share honors with a
clerical brother, Padre Sydes, who got furi-
ous encores for his vocal share of the pro-
gramme. Father Sydes, before he forsook
the laity,- was a man of law in Bananaland,
and there’s always something doing when he
mounts the pulpit.
Sydney pressmen having spilt much saw-
dust at French’s Forest in building a
returned soldier’s home, the women scribblers
had to provide the pots and pans. By way
of raising £lOO .or so, they achieved a suc-
cessful show that made the Repertory
Theatre swarm like an antbed. But two
shillings was a bargain-counter price for a
programme that meandered on till the
outer suburbans had to bolt for home. To
Mrs. Fotheringhame and May Summerbelle;
Nina Murdoch and Zora Cross, for the
Muses’ rhymes ; to “Hop” for his talk and
chalk ; to Rhoda Kettlewell for dancing like
a fawn, with pretty Nmyphana Fox clutch-
ing her slim waist; to Andrew Black, Charles
Smythe, Nellie Leach, Mrs. Roy Evans (who
rushed to fill a gap in the Muses’ pageant),
a thousand encores! But not on one even-
ing!- To Colin Crane, Martinelli, George
Hawksley and Jim Burke, who trotted out
suitable ditties, the same compliments. Rena
Wallace and Blanche d’Alpuget were hon.
secs.; and Alfred Cooper (hon. treas.) is
still ready to welcome any offering, from a
new dishcloth to a plump cheque.
Green ribbons waved for St. Pat on Satur-
day, and that broth of a boy, Premier
Holman, had to face a fine hullabaloo at the
sports carnival. The concert the evening
before brought a party from Government
House, who found W. Asprey, St. Mary’s
choir-master, in charge. But the Town Hall
isn’t large enough for St. Pat’s evening-out,
and next year the committee may hire the
Domain.
Captain Piggot, one of the notable pilots
of the Torres Strait shipping route, returned
to Sydney on Friday, after being reported
drowned in one of the North Sea trawlers.
As a matter of fact he was one of the saved
on a boat that met the enemy, and he
comes back badly hurt to tell the story of
his rescue and the loss of many of his crew.
Dora Ohlfsen, back in her Rome studio,
lias just finished “In Eternal Remembrance,”
a medal commemorative of the Anzacs of
Gallipoli. On one side the figure of Aus-
tralia bends to kiss her fallen son. The
handsome Miss Alix Simpson, who posed for
Australia, is an English rose noted in Rome
for her heauty.
Eleanor Cameron, till lately a political
organiser here and in Vic., made her vows to
Andrew Glencross at the Phillip-street kirk
on Wednesday night. The fair Eleanor was
radiant in wreath and veil and a shell-
colored train; but a brace of bridesmaids
must have chosen their-colors in the early
daylight. Anyhow, they clashed in blue and
mauve. The wedding was partly political,
aud Sydney Smith, ex-Minister for Pump-
kins, was the bride’s guide and give-away at
the church ceremony. Eleanor’s father was
an old politician, too —Angus, of Wavqrley.
A well-known Sydney woman. Mrs. Alex-
ander, died last month in London, at the
over-ripe age of 90. She was a sister of the
late Saul Samuel, one-time Agent-General
for N. S. Wales, and an aunt of the present
Baronet Edward Samuel. Very many years
ago Mrs. Alexander was a notable figure
in Sydney society. That Lyons who built
Lyons Terrace was a near relative.
The F. 11. Snows, of Adelaide, are getting
some little consolation out of the war which
knocked their metal-export trade to pieces.
Son Wilfrid, who had been a clerk in the
Sydney branch of the business, went to Eng-
land to join the Flying Corps when there
was no Flying Corps to join in Australia;
and last week a cable told that he had been
awarded the Military Cross and promoted to
the rank of captain. Father got the glad
news while he was sampling Kosciusko.
North Sydney girls are- engineering a
bazaar in aid- of the Y.M.C.A. Field Fund.
Cafe Franeais, George-street (city) ; March
27. Engineer-in-chief, Addie Marr.
At the Women Painters’ show in the Edu-
cation Buildings E. E. Middleton’s most
ambitious effort, “The Coming Storm,” is
one of the best things in a rather weak lot.
Mrs. A. Iledley Parsons and her unrelated
namesake, Mrs. A. M. Parsons, are, with
Alice Norton (Mrs. A. Stephens, now resi-
dent in AVestralia), in the front row. Maude
Russell, forging ahead, is responsible for a
delicate group of water-colors. Mabel Bar-
ling’s tigers are fatter than of old. Perhaps
Bradley’s Head suits them better than Moore
Park. Lucie Peapes deserves lion, mention
for a charming tabloid in water-colors —old
St. John’s Church, Parramatta. Ruby
Winckler, strong and original, shows some
illustrations of the Odyssey—-not for sale.
If the hanging committee had thrown out
the too-crude amateurs the standard of the
show would have been higher. Why include
in a serious art show the experiments of the
eldest daughter at a young ladies’ seminary V
The musical Cherniavskys will shake their
amaranthine locks in this city in a couple
of months. Lately, they’ve been bowing to
loud applause in U.S.A., and shaking the
Four Hundred of N’York by the diamond-
circled fingers.
The Red Cross toy-makers at Ash-street,
City, can do with a lot of things, but they
specially would like the empty spools of
typewriter ribbons (they make splendid
wheels) and w r orn-out circular erasers that
mostly find their way to the w.p.b. Here’s
where the operators of type machines can
give a hand without effort.
The Vagabonds Club mentions in paren-
theses that it has already raised £lOOO for
war funds, and is going to double that if
the enemy doesn’t get on the run too quickly.
As a start for the second thou, it opens a
show in the Repertory on March 24. • Pro-
ceeds of “What Would a Gentleman Do?”
go to the N. S. Wales Fire Brigades’ Red
Cross Society.
In the early stages of the Big Noise,
“Kelly” Raymond Wise left off tending his
flocks at Gunnedah to help get the Turk off
Gallipoli. Then he went to France, became
a star-bearer in a border regiment, and has
now acquired a Military Cross. His proud
parents, the G. E. Wises, live at Croydon.
There’s to be a cafd chantant at the Ritz,
Cremorne, on March 29. Manager Ben
Fuller is lending some of his talent. Syd
Macdonald, Harry Thomas and Rita Raine
have also promised to be there. It’s to help
the Cause.
Swarms of Osbornes and other woolly
folk _ are camped at the Australia, and
they’re likely to stay without much pressing
for the Pumpkin and Horse carnival.
The poor war babies ! One blossomed in
the daily-paper birth notices this week as
Beatty Jutland. Still, it might have been
worse. If he had landed a little sooner he
might have been branded Protopopoff Bag-
dad. And some parents wonder why their
children take the wrong turning!
You can’t get ahead of the Army Service
Corps dames, unless you hop out of bed the
week before last. They’ve captured Lady
Helen to sip tea in their tent during the
Easter Show. You can almost hear the rush
of Mrs. Subbubs to taste the A.S.C. brew,
and pay up for the privilege on that par-
ticular afternoon.
Mrs. George Lee, wife of the new District
Commandant, is one of the genial, motherly
women who will put a pleasant top to the
military dames’ socks-and-chocolate parties.
Popular in Queensland, she effervesces with
the hearty kindliness that melts all sorts of
social glaciers.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, March 19, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Lady Helen gave an informal tea-party last
Tuesday at the big grey house, and the re-
turned nurses, guests-in-chief, arrived in
their dark grey uniforms with the dull
bronze badges arid buttons, crowns and stars.
Some of the girls are majors, captains and
lieutenants. The G.-G. piloted parties of
them all over Government House, and showed
them his collections of Australian gems and
pictures and woods. The hostess had tea set
in the big dining-room and music was laid on
in the drawing-room. Leslie Gage, Boffard,
Anne Williams, Betty Paterson and Ger-
trude Summerhayes managed the programme
between them.
The P’ran branch of the A.N.A. is at
present feeling not a bit like P'ran is sup-
posed to feel, having just acquired the Stan-
ley infant (Lyulph Henry Victor Owen) as
a life honorary member.
Lesley Madden starts her venture in the
underwear line about Easter, having taken
the rooms over Webb’s, where the Burberry
girls did such a roaring trade in hats.
, The Fred Douglass family, which is inter-
ested in the Geelong ’Tiser, has had a taste
of the horrors of war without an; of the
after-effects. The other day Lieut. Monte
Douglass, one of the two eligible sons on
service, was reported .wounded- —his back
was hurt and a leg was gone. After an
agonised interval another cable came to say
:hat the leg was in its proper place, and there
was nothing the matter with Monte’s back.
The misreported young man left Victoria
with the magnificent sth Battalion and went
through Gallipoli. Afterwards he took a
commission in the British army and has
been in France nearly ever since.
Mrs. Stewart Balmain, who was Alison
Fairbairn, has moved away from her Eng-
lish coast town, and is now living at Burn-
ham Beeches, one of England’s beauty spots,
while hubby pounds the Hun in France. He
lost a life-long friend in General Duncan
Glassfurd, who was killed in France some
time back.
Melbourne Grammar “Captain,” Ken
Clarke, has handed his school job over to
Herring, and goes into Maribyrnong camp
next week. Ken is the younger son of the
Rutter Clarkes. They lately drifted back
from India, whither their elder boy had been
invalided from one of the Fronts.
Roy Officer, a school champion bowler and
runner, has exchanged his cricketing flannels
for khaki. The 19-year-old recruit is of the
third generation of the squattocracy. Dad
is with the heads of Dalgety’s.
The Alliance Frangaise is to have a party
at the Independent Hall on March 27.
Coffee, music, French playlets and French
pastry.
Mrs. Clifton gave a Bananaland party at
her flat last week. Mrs. Parker and the
two pretty Piper girls were there. They
are all Northerners, though the Charters
Towers Pipers have been perched in Syd-
ney for some time between spells of travel.
Squatter John McArthur, who died so
suddenly at the Oriental Hotel last week,
was married twice. His widow was Etta
Ferguson, a first cousin, now left at Men-
ingoort station, in Camperdown. His first
wife was also a first cousin. She was
Bessie McLean, a daughter of the fine old
captain who brought the old Ozone out from
England. _ The McArthurs were clannish in
their marriages. Callum McArthur, the
brother of John, married a sister of Bessie
McLean. He is still living in the Western
District. The brothers at different times
contested the same seat in Parliament. John
was a serious politician and at his election
meetings talked politics to his people. When-
ever he won it was by a small margin. Cal-
lum’s method was to gather his folk together
and tell a jolly story or turn on a gramo-
phone. When he got home it was by a
majority of thousands.
There was a general meeting at the Aus-
tral Salon last week with the Madden dame
presiding. This was an event, for she has
been quite out of the run of social duties
since her illness. Though somewhat pale,
she managed quite in the old style, inspect-
ing the room through an eyeglass on a rod
while the versatile and immortal Claude
Hall managed a delectable programme of
music.
Toorak finds life hideously dull these
times, and if it were not for rest-home work
the girls whose job is killing time would be
quite desperate. A lot of them have stam-
peded into hospitals as trainees, the latest
recruit being Kath Austen, of the Western
District wool clan, a sporting type who
“shoved” her own car and went in for golf
before she started scrubbing wards.
The dignified head of. Melbourne Grammar
lately entertained a dozen small guests at
afternoon milk, and blue-capped scholars,
peeping through the fence, stared wildlv at
seeing “Lofty,” his eyes bandaged, sprinting
across the turf in pursuit of a flying, shriek-
ing band of youngsters, which included Lady
Helen's small niece, nephew Veronica and
Basil Blackwood.
Pauline Curran, the Hobart beauty who
came oyer with her mother for the Autumn
races, is still to be seen in the lounge at
Menzies’ at black-coffee time. In an English
tailor-made, with a big ivory collar, she was
one of the dreams at Flemington. Two
admirers attended her throughout the entire
season in Hobart. One was the attractive
khaki-clad Russian who takes observations
from the steps of the Athenseum Club. His
rival was a prominent Sydney man.
The shapely Philip Russell dame, who
turned a lively puce with excitement when
Harriet Graham won the Australian Cup,
was easily the best-turned-out matron on the
lawn in filmy black, with white furs and a
silver-laced toque of the Russian persuasion.
The frocking at the Scobie Mackinnon
matron’s garden-party was a trifle sombre,
on the whole. The weather was partly to
blame, and, besides, those who possessed
them wished to wear their new winter gowns.
A mere handful of heroines ventured out in
summery attire, two maidens only being
brave enough to appear in white. ' It was
distinctly a “coat and skirt” show.
The Foster district is mighty proud of its
recent Queen carnival. There were three
Queens —one each for Centre, South * and
East Gippsland. “South” headed the poll,
and -Mrs. Wilson, a doctor’s wife, was
crowned gloriously in the Foster Show
Grounds in a blaze of electric light. There
was tremendous excitement, as the affair
realised over £l5OO for the patriotic funds.
The High Chamberlain got so excited and
backed so furiously before the Queen that he
receded clear off the platform behind the
throne. The upset dignitary did not mind
the shin-and-elbow bruises so much; his chief
concern was about the injury done to the
lovely silk stockings which had been lent to
him by a woman. A prime mover in the
whole affair was Nicol, owner of Rael Loein,
one of the hot favorites that lost at Fleming-
ton because its nose was not long enough.
Its puzzling name —well, stand it on its
head and you will see what it means. Mrs.
Rael Nicol acted as Mistress of the Robes.
The Ancient Marinesses, who are to run
the kiosk outside the Town Hall on Sailors’
Day (23rd), are appealing for gifts for sale.
Address to the President of the A. M. Ladies
Committee at the Town Hail. Such things
as pipes, tobacco, books and socks are sug-
gested; but any old thing worth buying—
even soiled banknotes —will be received with
thanks.
Shackleton talked in the Auditorium on
Friday night. He is a fine, muscular young
man whose shoulders strain at the seams of
his “shore clothes” as he moves about with
a soft, padding walk. He spoke in a brusque
but easy and convincing manner. Half-way
through he said, “We’ll have a spell for five
minutes now —eh? —so that we may all
move about—see? What d’you think?”
Then lie slipped down into the audience and
men crowded round to shake hands with
him.
The Elenara variety show at Sandring-
ham last week netted 350 guineas for the
Red Cross, and young Jimmy Gatehouse and
his wife (she was one of the Geelong Aus-
tins) deserve a cordial vote of thanks for
lending their grounds. Lady Helen made
a semi-royal progress round the various
stalls, escorted by Arthur Robinson and a
genthelp.
Adelaide’s pickings:—
The publishers of her Ex.'s “Belgium Book,”
Hussey and Gillingham, have handed a cheque for
£375 to the Belgian Relief Fund, being the gross
proceeds of the sale of half the copies. The other
half are still available. Official statistics show that
South Aus. has contributed £647,695 to this and
other patriotic funds. The Soldiers’ Fund heads the
list with £330,000, the Belgian Fund being next
with £125,000.
Incidentally, her Ex., with her son and Miss
Macaulay, is still the guest of the H. H. Duttons at
1 ictor Harbor, where the Governor joined the party
for the last week-end. Lady Galway is taking an
“off” time from engagements till the end of the
summer brings sojourners back from the sea and the
hills, when Society will hang out the old sign: “Busi-
ness as usual.” .
Captain Blackburn, V.C., has been given his dis-
charge, and after Easter will become busy in the
pursuit of Costs as a partner in the firm of Fenn
and Hardy. There is another partnership to be
formed in connection with the house —but Captain
Blackburn and Miss Rose Kelly will attend to that.
Mrs. H. Bickford has given another armed aero-
plane to the British Government, With the request
that it shall be known as the “Mount Lofty.” It
is v only a few months since she made a similar gift
and also rolled along a motor-ambulance for the
use of Australian troops abroad. Good patriot!
Speaking of motor-ambuiances reminds me that C. T.
Bray has gone back to the Front. He got a spell
home not many weeks ago after putting in a lot of
strenuous work for the British and French Red Cross
at Verdun and elsewhere. He is convinced that to
cope adequately with the wounded every man who
can drive a car is needed over there. No doubt he’s
right, but it's very pleasant here, and there’s not
a bit of mud on the roads just now.
G. H. Wilkins, who spent three years with the
Arctic Expedition under Stefansson, is back in Adel-
aide. He started for the North as photographer to
the expedition, and returned as second in command.
Until April he is under the orders of the Canadian
Naval Service Department ; after that he hopes to
get on the trail of the Huns.
The Trench Comforts Shop, opened on Friday,
nominally at noon but really a .good deal earlier,
owing to the importunities of buyers who arrived
at 10 and threatened to crowd out the Arcade. “The
best home produce at market prices” is the motto;
expenses will be scraped to a minimum; and the
profits will be spent on gladdening Australians on
service. The local A.C.F. has also added to itself
ja men’s auxiliary, which has made the supply of
tobacco and cigarettes to S. Australians its special
business. The latter movement is the direct out-
■ come of Mr. Budden’s visit.
The Cheer-Up Society has added to its possessions
a motor-carrier, whose chief use will be for the
collector pf “cabbage patch” gifts. A cabbage patch,
by the way, is a garden wherein you grow vegetables
to feed soldiers at the Cheer-Up Hut. The idea has
spread wildly, and a children’s branch is in process
of formation. The same Cheer-Up Society ran a
cafe chantant in the Town Hall on Friday evening-
in aid of its funds.
Miss de Lissa, the kindergarten expert, has left
for Sydney on the way to her appointment in Eng-
land. Tucked amongst her luggage is an honorary
commission to inquire into educational matters in
Canada and the United States.
, The Tuesday Club is running a “Thrift Week,” and
we’ve all decided to go without furs and even race
frocks for as long as the week lasts.
Pianist Brewster Jones's sister was married last
week to Harry Armstrong. She is a pretty girl,
with misty brown hair, and has a helping of her
brother’s musical talent.
Women’s branches of the Agricultural Bureau are.
to be established. They will be governed by the
same rules as the men’s branches and will have the
same privileges. The new opportunity comes simul-
taneously with an offer of £5OO for a scheme to
wipe out the mice pest in wheat stacks, provided
the way is “economically practicable.” The Wheat
Harvest Board has made the offer.
Marie Tempest, looking like a Watteau
heroine gowned by Wirth, and leading-man
Graham Brown were boxed with Mrs. George
Tallis on the opening night of “Fair and
Warmer” at the Royal. Marie was more in-
terested in the audience than the play. Not
once was she seen to
smile at the silly
business on the
stage, but every
now and then a roar
of laughter from
the house would
cause her to look
round with quick
interest and a
slightly puzzled ex-
pression. O b v i-
ously she was won-
dering what on
earth there was to
laugh at.
A SILHOUETTE
IN THE BOX.
Marie Tempest
with an eye on
the audience.
Pianiste Nellie Billings (likewise cooings)
is to marry barrister Frank Maldon Robb.
The announcement has gone forth in the con-
ventional way.
Arthurine D. Tacey has murmured “Yes”
to the appeal of Reuben Le Lievre, of Adel-
aide. He is the son of the Le Lievres, of
near Dookie, and she is the daughter of the
Ernest Taceys, of the same town.
What sticklers for propriety some people
are! A Melbourne man of some note, who
was divorced in a blaze of publicity and a
shower of sentimental letters from the lady
co-re., is to lead her to the altar. The
tender news is broken to the press by means
of neatly-typed slips beginning, “An engage-
ment is announced.” I thought at first the
sweet tidings referred to two shy young-
unknowns who were facing the world for
the first time.
Another Anzac romance culminated in the
marriage of pretty Pattie Dobbin, daughter
of late Police-Magistrate Dobbin, of Wah-
gunyah, and dapper little solicitor-soldier
Reggie Hayes, of Mentone. They met at the
base hospital as nurse and patient.
The obliging stork has deposited a dew
boy on the English doorstep of the Clive
Fairbairns. (Mrs. Clive is Marjorie, voung-
est daughter of Edmund Jowett.) ‘ Clive
is doing his bit as lieutenant in the Grena-
dier Guards, and is said to have acquired a
beautiful mannah—the sort that drops from
Heaven on to a British Guardsman.
The G.-G. will open the memorial exhibi-
tion of pictures by the late -T. .J. Ililder on
Monday, March 2G, at the new Fine Art
Galleries, run by expert Gill. These premises
are the old German club in Alfred-place, at'
the back of Collins-street. Melba took a
great interest in the Gill project before she
left.
Mrs. Rothwell is making a good recovery
from the serious accident of a stove
bursting in her face, and there is no fear
now of disfigurement or blindness. (Hie
lady is coming out of the ordeal with a
new rose-leaf complexion, a skin like a
baby’s, and eyes as bright as ever. But the
doctor is shutting her up in semi-darkQess
for a further few weeks.
Betty Paterson is airing her nice young
voice in select circles just now. The other
day she poured a collection of old Scotch
songs into the ears of the G.-G. and his lady
at Government House. The dear old things
w'ere politely enraptured, and practically
asked the youngster to fill ’em up again.
Last Monday Betty took her soprani to the
Austral Salon and let it soar in “One Fine
Day” from “Butterfly” and a bracket of old
English and haggis airs. A fetching Maori-
land contralto, Peggy Sec-combe, melodiously
filled in the hiatus between Betty’s offerings.
You should see the Italians off-stage. No
frowns and scowls out of business hours.
The Best Fed have taken them in again and
are feeding and wining them lavishly. Mrs.
Polman gave a big initial reception party at
the Australia last Thursday. A great round
table strewn with purple asters was placed
in the supper-room and the food was whisked
about by waiters, the usual waitress-girls
being-all out on strike. The Italians showed
off by making speeches in English. Balboni
bit off some carefully rehearsed Australian
with his white teeth. The exquisite
Scanuiz/.i, wearing a green jade ring and an
envious air, murmured that he sang English
but did not spik it. Tenor Lois jumped up
excitedly and exclaimed, “Oompagnie—l
thank you !” and promptly collapsed. Kirby
sang a song from the balcony to Ldis’s
accompaniment. Mrs. Polman wore black
and gold and an ecstatic smile. Mrs. Donga],
the other hostess, wore, checks and a paddy-
green hat. Mrs. Springthorpe, Mrs. John-
stone, and Mrs. Monash were at the board ;
and n. string orchestra conducted by Ricardi
played the accompaniments to the soup and
spaghetti.
Dark stories are floating round to the
effect that Gregan McMahon is going ’ to
drown liis sorrows in the undertaking busi-
ness. Apparently the rumors have reached
Trafalgar, for the actor has received from
that obscure village a written offer to ex-
change a hearse for £2l. The cart, it is
stated, has “glass sides” with “a very good
under-carriage,” but the “body wants clean-
ing and repainting.” McMahon, it is said,
is favorably considering the offer, and will
probably go into partnership with his friend
Hugh McCrae. The tall poet reckons that
they will be able to put the hearse to lucra-
tive use at the forthcoming Federal elections.
Melba sends a glad coo-ee. It ripples
from California, where she is staying with
the millionaire Crockers, whose house is a
miniature Palace of Versailles crammed with
art treasures. She was giving a concert in
Los Angeles, with pianist Ruby Gray and
Julius Knight assisting, and had cheered
Marie Tempest on her way hither with a
farewell luncheon. The singer- was soon mov-
ing south to meet her friend Lady Susan
Fitzclarence.
The Italians have attached as official in-
terpreter Mr. Crosetto, a young man who
has been five years in Australia and is in-
tensely proud of his absorption of the lan-
guage. Crosetto is a severe trial to Walter
Kirby, who used to interpret the opera
singers’ drink, food and smoke orders at all
sorts of well-furnished sprees. Crosetto is
the one who’s being dined and wined this
time.
Vivacious little Irma Caron is being ten-
dered a farewell concert on Thursday, 29th,
at the Masonic Hall, prior to her departure
for “the East.” The Gaud Mayoral couple
have promised to be there, and the members
of the Stock Exchange are supporting the
send-off. Irma will appear in several oper-
atic character numbers.
Tours sincerely, JOHANNA.

“Silas Snell”: A competition of curious
funerals is it ? Well, here’s a faithful chronicle.
George Arthur was demonstrating at great
expense that the alleged rich leaders in the
hills beyond Oassilis (which is in the wild waste
between Bairnsdale and Omeo) are not worth a
bean, and one day, doing the last 10 miles of a woeful
jaunt per hoof, he was suddenly confronted by a
laden pack-mule trotting down the track entirely
on its own. The mule’s burden was a curious one,
a narrow, oblong box Oft. in length, 16in. deep
and 2 ft. across. George Arthur made a heroic,
middle-aged effort to intercept the lone hybrid ; but
the neddy shied off, climbed some piled butts, and
scuttled into the bush, whereupon George resumed
his own journey. About three-quarters of an. hour
further nor’-west, the weary wayfarer encountered
a blaspheming bushman with a gun and an expres-
sion of utter loathing. “Hi, you!” cried the
stranger. “Have you met a fune 1 anywhere atween
here and the stone road ?” The question called for
no thought. “No,” said George definitely. “You
quite sure ?” George was positive. . It occurred to
him that he would recognise a funeral at a glance,
and a funeral in that immense loneliness was not a
thing to escape attention. The man with the gun
explained: “Me name’s Owner. I’ve had a funeral
bolt on me. You see, my missus died yes’day
mornin’, ’n’ I was takin’ her down to Chapel Flat
ter bury, an’ leavin’ the mu-el by the crick while
I got a drink, the cow took a contrary sort of fit,
’n’ broke fer the scrub. I’ve bin after him ever
since. I’ve got to get him —he’s off with me
missus.” Then George understood—the laden pack-
mule was Mrs. Owner’s funeral.
WHISKERY BILL ADAPTS THE
“TANK” IDEA TO HIS OWN NEEDS.
“Tarn O’Shanter” : I cast, my vote against
“Lowan” (B. 22/2/’l7) for saying that “Spraggs’s”
70-horse team would pull the intestines out of any
waggon—on the bla_k-soil plains, at any rate—if
the ground is boggy. When the surface is in that
condition much of Prince’s and Bowler’s energy is
wasted in dragging themselves up by the roots and
keeping their equilibrium. Late in 1911 the
Hay-Ivanhoe mail coach stopped dead in the mud
near Booligal, and eight heavy draught horses were
sent out to induce her to come home. They had
several fierce arguments before she consented, but
they didn't even pull her frail quill out. Horses
won't pull worth a peanut in the mud. They are
too excitable.

“Bex” : “Tap’s” method of getting a run-
ning fire in stubble (B. 8/2/’l7) would be lovely if
it were practicable. I have been plugging along in
the Mallee for some years, and I have not yet seen
anyone adopt the plan of attaching a roller under
the comb for the purpose of getting a running burn;
but I was the first hereabouts to introduce the plan
of dragging a pole behind the stripper to insure a
better job with the fire rake. In case any new
hands may be tempted to try “Tap’s” method, I
would say that in an eight- or nine-bushel crop a
running fire is a practical impossibility unless you
strike a day when the ventilators of Sheol are open.
Further, any roller that is “from 6in. to a foot off.
the ground” will have no effect on the stubble at
all. The roller must hit the stubble at the ground-
line to be of any use.

“H.L.N.”: “Eryander” (B. 1/3/T7) seems
surprised to hear of a centipede 12in. in length. I
can go one even better. My brother sent me a speci-
men that he had killed on St. George’s Island, Ber-
muda, which measured 14iin. from head to tail, not
including its pincers, which went five-eighths of an
inch. After being stung by one of these horrors you
wish earnestly for 24 hours that your grandmother
had been eaten by cannibals in her infancy. The
best soother of the pain is tobacco juice.
* * * *
“E.S.S.” : Can’t we get some decent names
for our birds—names that the small boy and the
old man and the poet can catch on to and joy in?
No poet in creation could enthuse about the blue-
vented parrakeet, or the yellow-bellied fig-bird, or
the white-shouldered caterpillar-eater, or the grey
shrike thrush, though the three last are delightful
singers. The group comprising the coots, moorhens
and waterhens supplies a more striking example of
the awfulness of Australian bird nomenclature. I
allude to the popular or vernacu-
lar names; their classical titles
are impossible for general use out-
side a lunatic asylum. Moorhen
and waterhen (the males are pre-
sumably moorcocks and water-
cocks) are terms that apply to
dozens of waterfowl. Coot is all
right, only it’s borrowed; but
blue-bald coot, or azure-breasted
Phorphyrio, is an atrocity. Again,
as the coot is partly bald, having
a naked, lead-colored forehead,
bald coot isn't satisfactory as a
name for its near relative, which
is also known as redbill and black-
back Phorphyrio. Redbill would
be good enough, but it belongs to
quite another kind of bird (dozens
of birds, including the village
toper, have got red bills). Other
species are called black moorhen,
rufous-tailed moorhen, native hen,
black-tailed native hen, etc. Each
of these has several aboriginal
names, if they haven’t got good folk names, and
the most euphonious of them should be retained.
“Minkil”: “Koonah” (B. 22/2/T7) asks
the name of the common Mallee lizard about six
inches long that so easily loses portion of its tail.
This is no doubt the Kmoa spenceri, named after
Prof. IV. B. Spencer, of Melbourne University;
but I have never seen one change color when cap-
tured, as “Koonah” describes. Tiger snakes appre-
ciate them. I have several times found them partly
digested in the tiger’s cupboard. “Sarto” (B.
8/2/’ 17) fairly describes a gecko lizard which he
chopped out of a red-gum log. It was probably
Phyllurus millinsii, described by McCoy as a small,
disagreeable-looking lizard with broad, thick, heart-
shaped tail. The beautiful transverse dark and light
banding on the upper surface makes it one of the most
striking lizards of Victoria. This reptile can walk
up the wall of a room, but as all Australian geckos
are of nocturnal habit I would like to know where
“E.S.S.” (B. 8/2/’l7) saw them catching flies.
“Leo Lear”: Let me add to “E.S.S.’s”
three Australian birds (blue wren, bush-lark and
rccd-warbler) that sing at night. Most of the cuckoos,
especially the pallid and channel-bill, advertise their
vocal goods in the dark hours, though I reckon t-.e
latter’s is the most discordant clamor raised by
any Australian bird. Then how about the wagtail?
This little chap goes the wlmle night long and then
cheerfully tackles the day-shift. Lastly the white-
backed magpie often gives us a round of his un-
equalled warble at 2 g.m. By the way, does any-
one know a locality where white-backed and black-
backed maggies are equally common? In N.E.
Australia the black bloke seems to run the show,
and along the south coast of the continent the
slightly larger white cove rules the country.
* * * *
“I-1.V.E.”: “Eryander” says that the
yellow-bellied sea-snake is “rarely found so far south
as Sydney.” I found the corpse of one on a beach
between Bermagui and Tathra, over 200 miles far-
ther south. The extraordinarily attenuated head and
neck of this snake is about the thickness of an
under-sized little finger, a formation which enables
it to obtain its food from narrow crevices of sub-
merged rocks. The wriggler is, of course, highly
venomous, but no casualties are on record. The
compressed body flattens towards the tail and ends in
a blade-like section as in the case of eels. Such
extreme compression indicates that the creature lives
at a considerable depth, as this formation is best
suited for resistance of water-pressure. Another
variety of sea-snake, ringed with black and white,
has been captured in the vicinity of Port Jackson;
other species are confined to tropical seas. . By the
way, land snakes, even deadly ones such as the black
and tiger, are said to be edible, and possibly sea-
NO GLUTTON.
MISSUS: “Want to do some work for a meal, do yer? Well, there ain't much work to
he done here."
BEAT ( apologetically): “Well, Mum, I wouldn't do much."
snakes are also. Even the horrific estuary catfish
and the serpent-like green eel, common on rocky
foreshores, may serve as food. It is on record that
several bags (“Chapman's,” I think) full of dried
estuary catfish found a ready sale on a certain
V/estralian gold-field at a time when such luxuries
as bully beef and tinned bacon and eggs were out
of stock. The freshwater catfish (miscalled “jew-
fish”) is, of course, a recognised article of diet, and
in all probability its brother of the estuaries, if
skinned, would be good.
“Wandi”: A country paper says that
while working in Teven Creek (N.S.W.) three men
were stung by bullrouts “and suffered excruciating
agony for a couple of hours until the turn of the
tide arrested the pain.” It is an old belief on the
north-eastern rivers that the pain from the sting of
the bullrout persists until the tide turns, though
how the tide affects it has never been explained.
Granting that it has an allaying influence, I can’t
understand why a man who is stung a little while
after the tide has turned should suffer for several
hours, while one that is stung just before the turn
of the water is relieved in a few minutes. When I
was a boy a bullrout got me on the forefinger in
the Richmond. The tide did not reach within 12
miles of the spot, and a mate told me that the pain,
which was certainly excruciating, would continue
till' the sun went down. It did, after two long,
weary hours. Evidence points to two hours as
being the common duration of the pain.
“Pjck”: Has any Abo. heard lately of wool
catching fire through being pressed when wet? Dur-
ing the wet shearing of ,1910 the wool-hawks became
less particular about the risks of lumbago and
rheumatism through handling wet sheep. In differ-
ent sheds I saw the juice oozing out of the bales
when they were being pressed, and if that wool
hasn’t caught the I.W.W. disease, it’s about time to
drop the old theory that wet wool is liable to
spontaneous combustion.
* * * *
“Buzfuz” : Re Tommy Wilkinson’s feat of
drawing 22 tons with a string of 70 bullocks (15.
S/2/’l7). I saw this performance and it was some-
thing to write a poem about. A few miles from
Tarbrax (N.Q.), his starting-point, the load shifted
to one side and was on the point of capsizing every
moment. Half-way to its destination the waggon
sank to the axle-beds in soft ground, but Tommy
has a reputation in this district as one who does his
best work when up against trouble; so he set to and
dug a get-out for the wheels, hooked on a string of
83 steers, and after a good deal of battling delivered
his cargo.
* * * *
“Kuku Kuku”: The big Orokiva (from
the country where they eat men alive) was helping
to build a storeroom under my somewhat unskilled
direction: The ground was about as level as a
view of Sydney’s roofs from the top of the Metropole,
and a distant glimpse of the sea, apparently tilted
all to one side, upset calculations still worse. At
last I gave up trying to do the job with the eye,
and told the Northern cannibal to go and borrow a
level from my neighbor. He started off willingly—
he is always willing, and always literal; it you told
him to go and put his head in a bag he would imme-
diately pick up yesterday's rice sack and extinguish
himself till further orders, without presuming to
think what you meant by it. I called him back and
asked him to repeat the message; one never knows.
“What I tell you?” I asked. “1 savvy, Sinabada,”
he said. “You talk what I been tell you,” I in-
sisted, to make sure. “You tell along me I go along
Missi ’s house, I tell him he send boy here quick,
he bring Sinabada the devil!”

“Waudi”: A Glen limes squatter believes
in the domestic cat as a rabbit destroyer and has
distributed a few dozen of the species about his run.
His experience is that they remain in the bush
where liberated and go wild. They .cause a rapid
diminution in the rabbit population, but what will
bo done with the remedy when the disease has been
wiped out? A cat that will kill a rabbit will also
kill a young lamb; arid the damage it will do
among birds will be more serious in its ultimate
result than the rabbit plague. Bunny can be put
to good use, and to replace the herbivorous pest
with a prowling carnivorous one could be nothing
else but a calamity. Native cats (dasyures) were
once plentiful in the coastal regions and gave a
lot of trouble. They made their nests in hollow
logs and in the butts of hollow trees, and at night-
time raided the .hen-roosts. They were pretty- little
creatures, one species being thickly marked with
large brown spots and another with black spots.
Any of them are preferable to the tame cat that has
run wild.
JOHNSON.
“What sort of a chap is Johnson?”
“Well, if ever you see two men in a corner, and one looks bored to death, the other
is Johnson.”
A Ilawkesbury (N.S.W.) spider lately left
home hurriedly and went out for a drink, when
something like a Zeppelin shot into the net he had
spread for a fly. It was a “silver-eye,” and though
it shook the trap thoroughly it didn’t get out till
two chaps from the Ilawkesbury Agricultural College
freed it. Most of the big sticky web was swathed
round the prisoner’s feathers and it was hanging
helpless by the tail and one wing on two main
strands.
“Vailima”: “E.M.W.” (B. 15/2/T7) is
quite right about the variety' of carnivorous plants
in IV.A., but astray in some points. For instance,
there are between 40 and 50 sundews instead of only
two. The four-petalled flycatcher is not carnivorous.
It is a well-known trigger plant —alias “Jack-in-
the-box,” “spring flower” and “donkey orchid”—
of which IV.A. has over 70 species. The object of
the hammer (w’hich does not imprison the insect,
but merely slaps it on the back and lets it go) is
the pollination of the flower. Neither is the spider
orchid carnivorous. Its mechanism has just the
same purpose as the hammer in the trigger plants.
In addition to its sundews, IV.A. has two other
insectivorous plants, one called Bi/blis (no common
name), and the unique and beautiful little pitcher-
plant of Albany, which is found nowhere else in
the world than in that restricted area. Both these
plants are remarkable for entertaining guests, or, as
some biologists call them, messmates. A brilliantly-
colored bug lives among the sticky hairs of Bi/blis,
and when a new victim arrives lie saunters up non-
chalantly, and inserting his sucking beak fares
sumptuously', thus robbing the poor plant of its
hard-earned dinner. The same insect also frequents
the forked sundew, common in the wet sandstone
country around Sydney. The pitchers of the Albany
plant always contain the maggots of a species of
March fly, which lays its eggs in the mass, of cap-
tured insects in the pitchers, the larva waxing fat
on this predigested food. It is rather remarkable
that the larva is not itself digested, but as in the
case of intestinal worms there is probably an enzyme
produced by the maggot which prevents the action
of the digestive fluid contained in the pitchers.
“Toby C.”: In B. S/2/’l7 “Slybacon”
cracks a stockwhip, but it is evidently his first, since
he remarks that “the lash is usually lighter and
thicker than the tail.” Any stockman worthy of
the name can tell him that the 1 h, though lighter,
must be thinner than the fall, or tail, otherwise
correct balance is lacking j and balance is every-
thing in a stockwhip. This is not an • rgument
against the vacuum-creating principles of the crack,
but merely a hint for “Slybacon” to-learn something
about the persuaders.
“F.N.T.” : Binghi is getting right up to
date. The other day' in Melbourne I saw one of him,
a full-blooded abo., dressed like any other casual
toiler, whirl in on a motor-bike among the traffic
at St. Paul’s corner and whirl out again with the
insouciant ease of an old hand at the game. He
rode with his head down and his eyes up, rolling
them whitely- upon the motor-ears, jinkers and
women with babes-in-arms that threatened him on
every side. Then he was off and out along St.
Kilda-road towards the far wilds of the Prahran
bush, most of which, where it isn’t dingy bricks-and-
mortar, is nowadays imported willows and elms and
cypresses, with about two gum-trees to the 100 acres.

“Soupdriuker”: These naturalists take a
long time to And out things that every buslnnan
knows. Here is the great Fabre, for instance, telling
how he was tempted to sample wood-grubs by a
quotation from the late Pliny:—
Luxury had reached such a pitch amongst the
Homans that they looked upon the large worms of
the oak as a delicacy. They called them Cossi.
labre got his grubs out of the decayed stumps of
some French neighbor's firtrees, and, with a couple
of trustful neighbors as guests, roasted the big white
things on skewers and ate them with a pinch of salt.
They tasted like burnt almonds flavored with vanilla.
Later lie made the experiment of keeping and fatten-
ing them in jars filled with flour —another of the
tips lie picked up from Pliny, who records that
the Homans got their grubs in good -condition for
Ihe banqueting table by gorging them on flour and
wine - T he tip might be useful to England in these
dajs if it wasn t for the shortage of flour and wine.

“Jerry M." : A friend of mine has a pair
of white cockatoos which he has had for 20 years.
They are kept in a roomy cage, in the centre of
which was a hollow log. Three seasons ago the birds
dug a -round hole about a foot in diameter in one
corner of the cage and laid two eggs. The following
season the same thing 'occurred, except that the hole
was dug in another corner; but the result from both
lots of eggs was nil. A fresh hollow log was then
placed in the cage, and last season two more eggs
were laid, and this time two young Pollies were
hatched. Up to date they are doing well. Is there
any other known instance of cockatoos breeding in
captivity ? Incidentally, how do you tell the gent
from his better-half?
“P.1I.”; A pleasing feature, of the times
is the quick increase of the native Australian bird
and beast population. The murderous attitude of
the pioneer cockies and their children towards these
serviceable and beautiful creatures has given place
to solicitude. Around Orbost (Vic.) fur and
feathers are multiplying splendidly. The . day is
enlivened by the notes of the magpie, jackass,
butcher-bird, whipbird and a score of smaller species,
while at night one is reminded of early days by-
scrambling squirrels and ’possums and the strong
voice of the mopoke. In the Snowy River and its
tributaries the platypus can now be seen at any
time. Even on St. Kikla-road, Melbourne, wild mag-
pies are nesting among the chimney-pots of St.
Margaret’s Nurses’ Home.
“Main Shaft”: Another word about the
broad-leaved box as food for sheep. During the 1902
drought, when the country from Gilgandra to Coona-
barabran (N.S.W.) was as bare as a road, the only
feed available was kurrajong, she-oak, trefoil burr
ai d the box. On the rough ridges 40 miles out from
Gilgandra the •woollies not only kept alive on box-
leaves, but were active and vigorous and in very fair
condition. One owner told me that his box-fed flock,
was far superior to a lot that was considered fortu-
nate in having dry g-rass. The trefoil burr also
proved its value on the flat country along parts of
the Castlercagli. Edible scrub was gone, yet the
jumbucks, with no other food than burrs, put their
thumbs to their noses at the drought.

SISTER MINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Night winds straying o’er your pillow,
Faintly rustling in the willow,
Whispering to the creaking stable,
Crooning out the old-time fable ;
Baron munching by his feed-bin
(How I’d love to pat his old skin!),
Mother in the big loved bedroom
Where we both were given life-bloom;
Ivy clinging round the grey posts. . . .
I wake to find I’m with the brown hosts,
Standing in our rain-soaked breeches, .
’Nodding twixt the shrapnel screeches.
Little black-haired, quiet sleeper^
Can’t you hear me calling deeper,
Clearer than the gurgling river,
Stronger than the curlew’s quiver,
Floating out among the pine trees,
Carried homewards b.v the south breeze?
Fragrant head, sink sdft an'd deep,
I should grieve to break your sleep,
Or to see y-our eves sad wond’ring
Of your brother 'midst the thund’ring
And the fitful star shells' flashing
Where the front supports are massing!
Girlie, I do hope you’re dreaming—
That the moonbeams are still gleaming
In between the waving curtain....
Strange to-night I feel quite certain
Somehow this will soon be over,
And I’ll sneak across to Dover ;
Golly-, girlie, I’m near weeping,
■Just because I see you sleeping.
SOMEWHERE.
B. N. J.

“M.P.A.”: A few years ago I saw the
Swartz Brothers play a broken-mirror act
in London; and now I see the J-C.\\.
comedians play it in the Melbourne panto
this year. In a nightly round of theatres
and halls I placed the Swartz act at the
Coliseum as the best of all comedy stuff in
the Big Smoke. Years of practice had given
such a finish and completeness of detail to
their work that the act almost amounted to
genius in mimicry. The house rocked with
laughter from the moment the play in the
mirror frame began. But that wasn t the
original mirror business. Back in the
’GO’S Madame Julia Lehman and Madame
Marzetti gave an excellent performance at
the old Haymarket Theatre, Bourke-street,
Melbourne; and not so many years ago the
late George Musgrove introduced a mirror
dance on similar lines into one of his many
attractions. The Swartz Bros.’ sketch is no
doubt protected because it contains a deal
of clever dialogue, but the actual broken-
mirror idea was worked—Heaven knows how
long ago!
“Stargazer”: I have discussed “Turn to
the Right” with several actors and one or
two actor-managers, and they all love it. In
each case the sugar-coated mother was just
the most lovable of her sex, an angelic
sample that even grown men who respect
sweetness and light must cry over. Io
me the play, apart from its low-comedy
elements, is trite and tripey, and the good
mother is a bore. She is striking evidence
of the impossibility of making a living
woman wholly out of molasses, which you
can no more do than make a successful meal
on the same nauseating extract. Maw
in “Turn to the Right” is cloying; you leel
sticky after contact with her. But actors
from long contact with exaggerated senti-
ment cannot appreciate pleasing human
qualities that have not the superlative charm
of a seraph pickled in glucose, nor be stirred
by evil attributes that are not persistently
and aggressively diabolical. To be a good
judge of plays in the commercial theatre you
must have deplorable misconceptions of
human nature.
“Billy T.”: A Melbourne doctor claims
that woman does not faint nowadays, and
holds it to be proof of great physical and
psychological progress. In the middle of
last century a woman confronted with any
difficulty of which her mind offered no imme-
diate solution got out of it by tumbling
in a swoon ; to-day she faces her difficulties
like a man. That doctor does not go to
our theatres or picture-shows, where the
dramatists hold their little mirrors up to
nature, and where woman, confronted with
a problem, faints away just as freely as she
did in 1850. But I have to admit that in the
drama woman faints, not to dodge her own
difficulties, but to enable the playwright to
dodge his. In a play I sampled the other
night the villain, in the hands of the
police for robbery and a trifle of murder, is
about to be incriminated by the word of the
heroine, when she flops. “Saved! Saved.
cries the villain, throwing off the claws of
the law. “She has fainted!” So two vil-
lains were saved. The other was the play-
writer.
“ Jwosp”: The Yankee mummers mean
business and are working up a federation of
all actors, stage, vaudeville and screen, on
a trades-union basis. So far only the
movie crowd are standing out, but if they
come in out of the wet the umbrella will
cover 20,000 all told.
“Viator”: One of the greatest sights on
' earth is the annual gratuitous night at Paris
Grand Opera. The municipality supplies
poor citizens with tickets to any part of the
house. First come, first served—stage boxes,
stalls, dress-circle or galleries; even the
boxes behind the curtain, for which this
Opera is unique, and which are mightily ex-
pensive, are flung open. Exquisite politeness
is met on every hand—it’s all “Monsieur,
“Madame” and “Mademoiselle.” Immense
orchestra, all immaculate in evening-dress.
What opera? “La Juive.” With this the
new Grand had opened soon after the
Franco-German War, as a sort of defiance
to Germany. What splendid acting and
singing! Calls and recalls. “Merci!” cry
the laughing performers. Ho! The conduc-
tor ! Must have him up! Here he is at
last, all smiles and perspiration, led on by
the principals stringing hand in hand.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“H.M.”: Farmers have been scowling at
a mob of prosperous-looking individuals who
have no official connection with the Wheat
Pool, yet seem to have a fine pull somewhere.
They are fluent, good-natured people, who,
if a man has any difficulty with the pool, are
willing to help him through—for a considera-
tion, of course. It is whispered that these
wizards have worked wonders and that in
some cases inferior wheat has been accepted,
in some magical way, as f.a.q. At Wagga
(N.S.W.) it is alleged that a share cocky’s
wheat was declared to be not f.a.q., and a
heavy deduction was made, while the land-
lord’s whack of the same crop was passed
as f.a.q. Of course the question immedi-
ately asked is “Why employ agents at all?”
But the fact is that there has been so much
red-tape about the payment of money out
of the pool (especially if the farmer has
owed the Government a trifle for seed-wheat,
etc.) that many a farmer has been driven
to desperation. It is the red-tape and cir-
cumlocution and delay that has given the
agent his chance ; and for that the Agricul-
tural Departments are resnonsible.

“Haka”: They open their heads pretty
wide in the Argentine when tliere’d shorthorn
bull on the catalogue. At the recent Palermo
cattle sales 44 of them changed hands in an
afternoon at an average of £760 per head.
In the four-figure class two brought £4785
and £4370 apiece, while others cost from
£lOOO- to £4OOO per bull. What would have
been a record, however, was spoiled by the
refusal of a paltry £5240 for.the champion
on the list. Many of the animals were
importations from John Bull’s stud.
One of the drawbacks attached to the
importing industry is the pillaging of cargo
-—for which, in the long run, the consumer
pays. But, though the Australian lumper
gets blamed for a lot of this, he isn’t always
the culprit. The other day a case of tools
from N’Yark was dumped carelessly on
the Melbourne wharf, whereupon some
inquisitive stranger inspected it and asked
the consignee if it paid to import bricks.
The case of “tools” was full of baked clay
of a Yankee brand !
“Edo”: The evil odor tliat is not usual
around Westralian Government departments
has latterly been specially strong in the office
of the Curator of Intestate Estates. A Com-
mission of Inquiry has been staggered to
learn that the affairs of people dead several
years had not yet been wound up, and that
in many instances articles of jewellery were
missing. It seems, also, to have been quite
a common practice for (say) a good gold
watch to be replaced by a poor imitation.
Officers complain of overwork and poor pay ;
but it looks as if someone had found time to
arrange for extra remuneration.

“C. 0.”: In these hard-up times when
the public is urged by our calamitous poli-
ticians to exercise the most rigid economy,
it is interesting to note the example set by
the Government in even the smallest details.
At the N. S. Wales railway refreshment
rooms it was customary for Private Enter-
prise to use the glass water-jugs provided
gratis by advertising whisky-distillers. But
since Sosh took the rooms over the humble
glass pitchers have been replaced by gor-
geous silver-plated flagons costing £3 each.
The only advantage these offer to the. travel-
ler is that he cannot see the flies that have
suicided in them until he pours them into
his liquor or tea. The delay in extracting
the dead and dying may save the department
a portion of a meal when the “All aboard”
bell rings, but more than that is required
to make up the £l5OO a year the private
lessee used to pay.

“E.O’S.” : There is a Jap shop in Sydney
which has been giving me a queer feeling
lately. In the window, attached to some
goods or other, is a bold sign: “Made in
Australia.” That Jap evidently thinks
the fact that something is made in Australia
will naturally appeal to Australians, just as
he knows that in Japan a sign “Made in
Japan” would appeal to Japanese. Doesn’t
that Jap teach us a lesson?
* * * *
“Quidam” : Does anyone know what drug
the “century plant” contains? It grows
plentifully in N. S. Wales, and is highly
esteemed by bush folk as a cure for dysen-
tery ; also it is said to be a good tonic. It
is about as bitter as quinine, but its bush
champions swear that it does no harm to
anyone. Whatever the drug is, it is prob-
ably. of value if properly prepared. Possibly
Australians, as is customary, import it from
somewhere at a tall price. We are a highly-
developed people when it comes to botanical
chemistry—nearly as highly developed as our
own gohannas.
* * * *
The Wairarapa (M.L.) men on the land
are at it again on behalf of the boys in
brown. To raise funds for a soldiers’ club
they put up a pig at auction, and in a little
over five minutes he had been sold and resold
for £lOOO. A sheep fair was on at the time,
and Captain Cook’s legacy was again put
up. The auctioneer took off hid coat and
appealed to the Lincoln-fanciers to wipe the
floor with the Romney-adherents, which they
promptly did by bidding £1067. Add the
two, and the total ought to be in the vicinity
of a record price for a grunt.

“Mish”: Against “Murkah’s” parade of
prize telegraphic bulls I’ll enter mine. A
Melbourne merchant cabled Jones’s, of
Hobart, an order for 50 tins of jam. A
fortnight later the astounded man received
bills of lading for 50 .tons. A frequent cause
of errors is the weak section of a bad line
dropping dots. That explained a London
cablegram addressed “Christ Melbourne.”
It was two days before Mr. Christie got his
corrected message. One of the boys at the
Front cabled his father, “In hospital with
child.” It took the perplexed old man only
one guess to make it “chill.”
* * * *
“May D.”: Australia should shortly be
reading some practical remarks on vanilla-
growing by Beatrice Grimshaw, for that
writing lady is now supervising her own
plantation in Papua. Vanilla is such un-
certain stuff to cultivate, though, that her
whole year’s work may prove to have been
wasted. You can put your acres under the
plant, watch the blooms and then the pods
develop, and start to order new frocks and
silken luxuries, only to find that you can’t
squeeze a drop of essence from the crop.
Yet a planter a few hundred yards distant
will probably succeed year after year, just
because he has. hit upon soil which to the
eye is the same as yours, but is somehow
different. It is the most beautiful gamble
for vegetable stakes you could possibly
imagine.
* * * *
Two Maorilanders have been experiment-
ing with the juices of home-grown weeds, and
have produced 250 shades of dyes. The
scene of their researches is at Kaitoke,
where the ground produces fine crops and
the only industry is dairying, except for one
individual, who is boring for coal which he
says he found with a willow stick. The
dye discovery seems to be a sound one,
though, so far, no brilliant blues or reds
have been produced.

“Wages don’t trouble me,” said the
candle manufacturer. “Every time my
coves come at me for a bit more, I nip
another quarter-inch off me candles.” In
other words, he passes it on by cutting it off.
# * * *
“Two Seas”: In most of the London
magazine stories I have read lately, when-
ever a motor-car has been mentioned it has
always been the same brand of stinkpot. The
villain always abducts the persecuted
heroine in a Whatsthis car of incredible
speed, and as the hero pursues him with a
car of the same brand the villain is never
overtaken until he has scooted well into the
last chapter. Even then he has to be
stonned only by an obstacle that nothing
on wheels could get through. It is an old
idea in business, robbery, etc., revived.
When I was pursuing literature in the Big
Smoke I was under contract never to men-
tion but one brand of cigar. If my hero
desired to soothe his ruffled feelings with
my lady Nicotine he was always condemned
to choose “a choice Parfum de Chou from
the box Parker had placed silently at his
master’s elbow.” In part return for this
devotion of Parker’s his creator was kept
supplied with the high-flavored brand. I
found it very useful. Whenever the daily
bore blew into my den with suggestions for
plots I introduced him to a Parfum de Chou
and begged him to smoke until I was ready
to hear his views. I seldom heard anything
but a hiccough and an indecent rush for the
door.
POSTAL ECONOMY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Through his decision to sell waste-paper instead 01
paying to have it burned, Mr. Webster, Postmaster-
General, has made an annual saving- of £5OO a year.
We saved the scraps of paper with a will,
We gathered up the odds and ends of string ;
In sealing-wax alone our present bill
Is much below the.one we paid last Spring.
We do not boast, but by our thrifty way
AVe hope to make the postal system pay.
What’s that? The large fool-public still complains
That letters wander round for weeks astray,
That telegraphic muddle still remains
AVhile wrath chokes action and reforms delay.
Is the Post Office merely here to meet
The wishes of the people in the street?
No, no ! The reason postage stamps are sold
And mailbags hustled fiercely to and fro.
Is but to keep from want and shame and cold
The weary actors in a costly show.
All other views are false. At vast expense
We offer shelter for incompetence.
Grasp this and all things else are clear and plain.
But watch us as we save the public string!
To this tremendous task our soul and brain
With zealous patriotic force we bring.
Paper and string! The gross result is great.
The public? Let it waste its time and wait.
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.
THE RHYME OF YOUNG CARLO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Young Carlo, da hawker, on beeg sheep ees gone,
No more mash weeth Flora, no more Leetle Lon;
When firsta dey call heem it maka heem seeck,
But back for Napoli he go pretty queeck ;
No more in da Market
We seeta for yarn,
Or seenga da warcry, “Peanut-ta—Banan’!”
Young Carlo, da hawker, he taka hees gun,
An’ join da Alpini for fighta da Hun ;
He write in da letter dey sinkin’ da boot
So soon as dey can in da side of da brute;
So moocha da knifin’
He never have seen —-
But Viva da redda, da whita, da green!
Young Flora she sit in da wineshop an’ cry ;
Da fatta ol’ woman, she say, “Whaffor? Why?
He stay in Napoli, you stay in da shop,
Da besta in Bourke-strit for cateha da cop.!’
But Flora no lees’en,
She never take on,
She steeck for Young Carlo an’ turn down da John.
Young Carlo he senda me letter one day,
For geeva young Flora hees money an’ pay;
So bad he ees wounded he never get right,
When bursta da bomb on Gorizia height;
I no tella Flora,
I maka pretend
How sure ees Young Carlo come back in da end.
Young. Carlo da hawker ees come back agin,
Weeth hook for da handa, an’ seeck in da.brain;
But Flora she walka so prouda, so sweet,
She know how young Carlo been doin’ his beet—
You teenka heem done for,
I tella you “Garni”
You lees’en heem eeenga, “Peanutta —Banan’l”
VICTORIA.
RANN DALY.
HOLDING HER AUDIENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’ve made a hit. A pleasant thing
It is to make a perfect hit.
Once people said I could not sing,
Nor even dance a little bit;
And still I cannot sing at all;
I dance, ’tis said, a trifle less ;
But all about the foyer wall
Are set my photos large and small,
And I can wear a splendid dress—
Emphatic is my new success.
My name is large upon the bills;
I chirp a mere melodic spasm.
It’s true my brief performance kills
All popular enthusiasm ;
Yet I am starred at great expense,
And all the pretty things may buy.
My triumph it is just immense.
I easily hold my audience,
Nor do I feel the need to sigh
If no one guesses it but I.
No matter what the critics write,
The manager is pleased with me.
My rivals in their petty spite
May throw it in my teeth, but, see,
I score them off with perfect ease!
It comes to this when all is done:
Their energies they have to tease
Admirers by the score to please,
Whereas fatigue and fears I shun,
Expected to delight but one.
VICTORIA.
STARGAZER.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Prime Minister, talking of the pro-
posed importation of luxuries, says “it is
suicidal to send money out of the country
when every penny and every atom of wealth
is essential.” This is the policy of trying
to win the war by doing for a few weeks
what ought to have been done for years. Cer-
tainly it ought to be done now even if it
wasn’t done before; but those who have
made desperate remedies necessary, and re-
duced our chances of winning through even
if we adopt them, have no right to
regard themselves as statesmen. On their
past record they are the worst sort of dan-
gerous, short-sighted blunderers.
DOING HIS BIT.
CITIZEN : “Your mate seems to be pretty
full!”
JACK : “Oh, ’e belongs to a submarine, and
’e’s filling ’is tanks before ’e sub-
merges!”
In the House oif Reps, last week ex-
Treasurer Higgs gave away the interesting
information that several attempts had been
made to get the Labor Government to redeem
its Protectionist pledge; but “owing to the
presence in the Cabinet of several influential
Freetraders, notably Mr. Hughes, Senator
Pearce and Mr. Poynton, the attempts had
failed.’- However, now that these are.rein-
forced by other Freetraders like Cook and
Glynn, there is no telling what may happen.
Rut the Australian who thinks that his
country needs Protection, and gives such
men the job of supplying it, will also buy tar
when they want to whitewash the fowlhouse.
In the Senate they call it the S.O.S.
Ministry.
When the quaint Victorian “Economist”
Bowser called his remnant together the other
day it first decided to support the Fusion
holus-bolus and to deprecate any inquiry
into the Senate scandal; then it enthusiasti-
cally supported A. A. Billson’s motion that
the life of the State Assembly, which ends
next October, should be extended to March,
’lB, in order that it might deal with such
urgent matters as control of tramways,
charities, drift of population, etc. The war
has, of course, nothing to do with these
things ; still, the idea is entrancing. Its only
fault is its absurd modesty. Why not extend
the present' House’s life sine die, apply the
law of entail to the sitting members, and
have done with it? These recurrent elec-
tions are a great expense and very trying to
real statesmen.
This looks as though the N. S. Wales
Government lias serious thoughts of running
its business on business-like lines. It has
just decided to establish a co-operative
drug depot, through which, as from July 1,
the hospitals all over the State will be able
to purchase their supplies. The hospitals
will practically be the co-operators ; through
the State they will import or manufacture
their own drugs and cut out the middleman.
But the Government’s far bigger job will be
to promote co-operation among the produ-
cers. It is of spine importance to cut down
the cost of drugs to the consumer, no doubt;
but until the depot manufactures' in Aus-
tralia what before was imported, tlie Com-
monwealth will not really be the richer.
On the other hand, when tbe Government en-
courages primary production by helping to
cut out the parasites that now so .often make
primary production a poor business, it will
do something that may easily be worth
millions a year to the country.
From the S. M. Herald ( 1 4/8/M 71 :
Australia is the land of paradox; and it would be
quite in keeping with our history if, while the
general election campaigns in State and Common-
wealth were being carried on, the recruiting returns
should steadily improve. Already there are signs of
improvement. The latest returns in this State are
on the up grade, and —etc., etc.
The paradox is that Australia goes on allow-
ing itself to be misrepresented by men who
don't know Australia. The real Australian
never believed that a general election would
prejudice recruiting. It tvas the bogus Aus-
tralians who at least professed to believe it;
and that fact constitutes another reason
why they should not be trusted to speak or
legislate for people whom they don’t under-
stand.
At Prahran (Vic.), where for many months
the Labor League has been restlessly con-
sidering the matter of choosing a candidate
to oppose Donald Mackinnon, M.L.A., at the
next State elections, matters have taken a
turn for the worse. Private Alec Parker
was originally chosenbut
after his pre-election success
lie' was disqualified for hav-
ing allegedly failed to com-
ply with the spirit of the
P.L.C. rules. Another ballot
was called for, and on the
close of nominations Parker’s
paper was found to be ab-
sent, although he declared he
had posted it. Since then
Parker has gone to the Front
and the branch is bitterly
arguing the matter with his
friends. A reasonable way
out would be to place the
blame on the Postal Depart-
ment, which has no reputa-
tion to lose, and allow
Parker’s pals to put in a
new nomination on his be-
half. It might not be in
accordance with the letter of
the P.L.C. constitution; but
when the constitution
bumps the Fair Thing, the
Fair Thing ought to have the
road.
There has been too much talk in the N. S.
Wales election campaign about the alleged
connection of the Labor party with the
I.W.W. All the harm the I.WAV. would
have done would not be worth worrying
about had it not been for the cursed borrow-
ing orgy of the Labor Government. It
was this that created the artificial condi-
tions in which the I.W.W. theories have
flourished. The Government was splashing
around eight or ten millions a year, mostly
to provide, jobs for supporters; and with
bosses afraid to sack workmen no matter
how dishonestly they behaved, the I.W.W.
got the chance it wanted. Holman therefore
is the last man in the world who ought to
blame the Labor party for being influenced
by the I.WAV. It was he who got it all
ready to be influenced. He seems now to be
sorry he did it; on tbe other hand the Labor
party wants to continue tbe borrowing jag,
and while that lasts the I.W.W. theories
will prevail whether the Labor party counte-
nances them or not.

have a cut in; but after a day or tAvo Public-
