Last week the Federal Public Works Com-
mittee handed Parliament a 48-page report
on the proposed Postal Stores building in
Sydney. Without so much as glancing at
it, Parliament ordered it to be printed and
distributed galore. The Committee, which
is, of course, paid fat fees over and above
its £GOO a year, could have said all it had to
say in a line and a half, viz., that the Stores
building wasn’t required. The Government
sets out to spend £70,000 on a building that
isn’t needed, the Committee mops up a thou-
sand in finding it out, Parliament spends
more money in publishing a verbose report
that nobody will read —and so we go on prac-
tising economy and winning the war.
The Federal Government, though it is “not
going to interfere with the usual channels
through which cornsacks are imported to
Australia,” is also going to “import itself,
or through the Wheat Board, sufficient sacks
to cover a full harvest.” Heaven only knows
what the intention really is; but at present
there seem to be the makings of a horrible
muddle.

Sydney Daily Telegraph spoke the truth
that day (16/3/T7).
Although the Victorian Venereal Diseases
Act was passed last year, the regulations
have not yet been approved by the Execu-
tive Council. Cases emphasising the need
for haste are of almost daily occurrence, but
the languid Peacock administration shows
no sign of waking up. At Richmond the other
morning a 20-year-old damsel, arrested for
assault while in company with an alleged
deserter from Langwarrin camp, was found
to be in a condition that made her a public-
menace. A certificate to this effect from
the Government Medical Officer was handed
to the Bench that dealt with the case, but,
owing to tbe absence of regulations, the Law
decided that it had no power in the matter.
Somebody or other suggested that if some-
one had a word with somebody in authority
something might be done in regard to the
regulations or something of the sort, so the
girl was remanded for seven days on the off-
chance.
Instead of Avasting time disposing of freak
motions, the F.L.L. Conference this year
might do all unionists a good turn by devot-
ing a feAv hours to the subject of union book-
keeping and audits. A union secretary was
convicted of embezzlement at last Quarter
Sessions in Sydney, and Avhen the jurymen
came to examine the chaotic books they
saw lioav easy it had been for the unfortu-
nate man in the dock to ruin himself and
beggar bis mates. A qualified accountant
gave evidence for the prosecution and helped
the defaulting secretary towards his cell —
cold comfort for the men- whose money he
had taken. The same expert’s advice and
supervision a few months earlier might have
saved one poor fellow’s reputation and the
money of many others.

THE BILLJIM : “ Well, I got the tip that the
loss teas going to sack me, so I went
straight up to the barracks. Then he
had to promise to keep my job open for
me.”
THE GENTLE CANDIDATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(Written after hearing a two-hour speech of pro-
found pathos delivered by a sentimental political
aspirant, who, it appeared, desired nothing better
than to die for his country in comfortable circum-
stances.)
Behold me where I stand and wait,
As ’twere the Peri at the gate—
I am the gentle candidate,
So soft of speech, so mild of creed,
So terrible against the greed
And wickedness of wretches who
Would blight the happiness of you. ,
Again I state
I am the gentle candidate.
I have but little heart for mirth;
My purpose is of serious worth.
It is the grandest thing on earth
To save and raise all fallen men,
To serve my country dear, and then,
When Wrong is down, and Right rides high,
To turn me to the wall and die.
The soul of me
So aches to lift humanity!
My people, grind the flesh of me
In mills of hard necessity!
All that I am i offer ye—.
If not for good that may be done,
i To spare ye then the other one,
The fiend who all fair things disowns,
And seeks to pick his country’s bones.
With hope divine
I’d have my country picking mine!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.
AS IN A GLASS DARKLY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rail not at ramparts reared with human hands
To break the bounds of petty human pride,
Nor curse red war, nor pray for Peace, nor chide
Some puny princeling! Lo, in hidden hands
Slow turns the glass from whence the pallid sands
Of cosmic balance, trickling slow, decide
The judgment of the gods! Poor masks, ye hide
Under your pageants their supreme commands!
Beneath their rods the man-tides ebb and flow
Like seas beneath the moon. So Asia smote ;
So, ’neath her mask of pride, her presence brave,
Where sank the West beneath the Orient wave,
Africa’s fingers fumble at the throat
Of that old Asia which laid Europe low.
VICTORIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Payment of first forfeit leaves 57 in the
Doncaster and 52 in the Sydney Oup, including a
dozen or two which on no reasonable grounds can
be given a chance of doing anything except get in
the way of something else. Harriet Graham and
Court Jester seem to be settling down to favoritism
for the Cup, and if all goes well with them may
stay there. Most of the betting has been in doubles,
so far, with punters holding themselves in reserve
for further inspection of the Doncaster. Whitefield
has been fairly well backed. There has been money
for Bonny Plume, and King’s Bounty seems to have
friends on tire Antarctic side of the Murray. King’s
Bounty was a runner at Caulfield last week in the
South Yarra Handicap, six furlongs. Carrying 7st.
181 b. he ran second to Durveney (7st. lib.), who
carved out the distance in He had a bad
passage, hut was doing best at the end. A smart
colt and in the Doncaster handily weighted at 7st.
51b.
* ♦ * •
One expects that the coming Sydney Cup
will be run on truer lines than last year’s event.
The good time recorded last year (3min. 24-jsee.)
suggests a fair test race; but the weather and track
conditions happened to very favorable anyhow, and
the battling circumstances gave the heavy weights a
poor show in the last mile. Also, they were mostly
off-color at the time. Not one of the first five
horses at the finish carried more than 7st. 71b., nor
were any ot the higher weights ever near the front
at any point of the race. Prince Bardolph got a
break on the field after leaving the straight, and
was j never caught. Green Cap (7st. 71b.) made up a
lot ;of ground, and finished two lengths behind the
winner; but the English horse was tiring, whilst
Bardolph (7st. 31b.) romped in. TJiey will each
have another 151 b., or more, to carry if they face
the barrier this time, and on recent form Rushford
should beat the pair. All three of them ran in the
recent Australian Cup, where Green Clap, after
making a bright effort, again tired conspicuously
in the last 50 yards. If the rich prize is going to
Victoria once more it will probably be Harriet
Graham or Court Jester who will snatch it. Nothing
was near them when -they fought out the finish of
the Australian Cup in record time. And both were
thought to be a trifle on the green side. Harriet'
is up 161 b. and Court Jester has another 131 b. to
hump. The new burdens are quite as much as these
natty little gees can easily manage, but all things
considered they are not • excessive.
In the. last 12 years nine winners of the
Sydney Cup, also nine of the runners-up, have
carried less than Bst. Eight years the going has
been more or less heavy, and the time accordingly
slow. So there is no encouragement to back top
Weights at a shortish price before the day. In fact,
the history of the race these last 20 years is a dis-
couragement of any early investment. The Doncaster
Handicap is a different proposition, for three of the
winners in the last six years—Broadsword, Jolly
Beggar and Eurobin —had run prominently in the
Newmarket, and could be legitimately fancied for
the mile race at Randwick.
Tt can easily be argued that Balarang, with 9st.
lib., is kindly treated in the Doncaster Handicap.
He receives 151 b. from Woorak, whereas there was
141 b. between them when he beat the little steam-
engine over seven furlongs at Caulfield last month.
The extra furlong should be ail in favor of the long-
striding browM fellow, who meets, also, the handsome
Whitefield on better terms than before.
Anyone looking for a fancy bet could hardly
expect more than even money against the Sydney Cup
being won by an imported nag. There are about a
dozen imported possibles among the entrants, and
several of them are highly probables.
* «■ * *
Patrobas arrived in Sydney last week
with a badly filled fore-leg, and after a course of hot
fomentations came to the conclusion that it would
be better to leave w.f.a. events at Randwick to the
,others. No serious objection has been made by the
others. It was hard luck, though, for both owner and
horse, for at least a couple of the races bore the
appearance of certainties.
* * *- *
The Autumn Meeting at Randwick had a
i preliminary phase at Rosehill on Saturday. At any
rate, some of the horses that will be at the other
place were there, though what some of them went
for this page was unable to detect. It .was a day
of disastrous happenings, five horses falling in the
first race, others coming to grief later on, and the
pulblic tumbling in all through the proceedings.
After many weeks of suffering Ike came into spine
of his own again, every favorite going down with a
heavy thud, and the two principal event’s being
appropriated by rank outsiders. Winners’ prices’:
,4 to 1, 5 to 1, 20 or 30 to 1, 6 to 1, 6 to 1, 25 to 1,
4 to li If the same thing} happens at Randwick the
increase in the cost of going a-racing will be a
•serious question.
* * * *
Coat-o’-mail is only a handful but lm
.seems very well just now. On Saturday the little
fellow ran* away with the Rosehill Handicap from
;end to end, the mile and a quarter occupying -2min.
7sec. As he galloped as if prepared to stay as long
:as a poor, relation, the fact that he is burdened with
only 6st. 101 b. in the Sydney Clip has more than a
little, significance. Coat-o’-mail is by Wallace j.
j—Daisy Chain, and there are more improbable
'things than that at some stage of the Cup he may be
taking' a hand in the argument. Among the Cup
horses behind him on Saturday were Chantemerle
(9st. 21b.), the Fortune Hunter (Bst. 131 b.) and
Canzone (Bst. 21b.). None of them were in their
best trim, though perhaps this didn't matter much.
Chantemerle, though big, was bright enough to sug-
gest that another three weeks would just about catch
him.
» * » *
The luck of Agar Wynne and W. Leonard,
owners of Tatyoon, was out a mile on Saturday/ The
colt was a starter in the first division of the Rosehill
Nursery Handicap, and when-looking like a certain -
winner faltered on three legs and a swinger.' One
of his fetlocks had broken. A bullet did the rest.
Tatyoon was in Payten’s stable, and since coming to
N. S. Wales for the New Year meetings at Randwick
had shown much promise, being one of the best of
his year. It would have required a lot of money to
buy him on Saturday morning.
The tote was given another spin in N. S.
Wales last week—at Kembla Grange. Tickets 10s.
There was nothing about it to alter this page’s
view that the system of running the machine
adopted in the Ma State is entirely destructive of
the objects sought by the advocates of the tote.
Ikey was there in great numbers, of course, and
as long as that happens the whole business is
upside down; and it is going to continue happening
while the State appropriates 7 per cent. In one
race at Kembla Grange the Moes were taking 2 to 1
on Knight Middleton. The good thing arrived and
the machine handed back 9s. on each winning ticket.
* * *
“Mujik”: I’m not expecting much from
the tote myself while Ike has the inside running, but
the people who deal with place-merchants ought to
feel a bit harmier when the machine arrives. Under
the present dispensation a 1-2-3 machine would be
a popular innovation, for the place-layer is as hungry
ah all Germany. About a fifth or a sixth of the
price for a win is about the best they do. In a
race where the betting is fairly well spread out this
is just picking up money, In a race at Rosehill on
Saturday the prices for a place were—s to 4 on in
the case of two horses, even money three, 5 to 4
against two others, 6 to 4 several more, and 2 to 1
and 3to 1. And, so far as I could see, they were all
being backed at these figures. There is only one in
a deal like this ; and if he can stay in on the same
basis against a machine the public ought to be
handed over, body and bones, to the Public Trustee.
“Trot”: At Rosehill on Saturday the
stewards had something to say to a jockey about
riding, the substance of their remarks being that if
he did no better next time his license would be can-
celled. He was not a “leading jockey” and probably
deserved to hear a few remarks. The pity is that
they are not more frequent; for not a meeting goes
by without examples of utterly brainless riding—
also of the other sort. Nothing will convince me
that jockeys should not be in the direct employment
of the racing clubs, put on hoard the horses by club
officials and ordered to report upon the condition of
each horse after every race. There is nothing im-
possible about this. There is no valid argument
against it. The allegation that a nag might win
when the owner didn’t want it to is easily met by this
—that no horse could win if it was kept in its
stable; and if it isn’t wanted to win the stable is
the proper place for it. Does anyone'know how the
public interest or the breeding of thoroughbreds
would be prejudiced if this were nrhde' a working
rule of the game?
» » * *
A capita] field of gees turned out for the
St. Kilda Cup at Caulfield last Saturday, when opti-
mistic victims of the recent V.R.C. meeting sank
deeper into the mire. Never a favorite got home, but
the widely-fancied Lavendo nearly did the trick in the
chief event. In the first mile of the journey he was
where the donkey’s tail is ; but for that he would have
added St. Kilda Cup to his winning account. As it
was, the somewhat erratic English horse caught every-
thing except his compatriot, Half-a-Glass, who led
from end to end. Judged on his best form the
winner deserved a heavier burden than 7st. 51b., and
Lavendo did well to give him 321 b. and run him to
a neck. First Trim, heavily backed, seemed to fail
for want of stamina in the last of the 11 furlongs.
* * * *
The imported nags again. Two of them
had the St. Kilda Cup all to themselves on Saturday
at Caulfield—Half-a-Glass (7st. 51b.) and Lavendo,
and it was Lavendo (9st. 91b.) that omitted to
arrive in time. Half-a-Glass, by Isinglass—Scotch
Gift, is a stable mate of Highfield’s, and on Saturday
was ridden by Dempsey. He was weighted at 7st. 51b.
in the Sydney Cup, but declined the invitation.
* » * *
“Spinner”; Am quite satisfied that there
is nothing in getting the “office.” On the Thursday
before the Newmarket in the Market Square at
Bendigo I encountered a large piece of agricultural
machinery, branded “Highfield,” drawn by a team
of eight bullocks. Turned up the paper and found
that a nag called Highfield was in the Bst. division,
was to be ridden by F. Bullock, and was eighth on
the list. Also, the time was precisely 8 a.m. by the
city clock. Plainly this was the “ciinkum oil” ; so
we took the best price on offer, which was 8 to 1—
and did in our dough.
A brand-new jockey made his appearance
in the list of winning riders at the recent Flemington
meeting. This was a youth named Valentine who
steered Bridges home in the Northcote Handicap on
the last day. He had ridden only once previously in
public ; in fact, had only six months before begun his
apprenticeship to W. Gerrard, who owns Bridges.
According to this form, Valentine’s day is not far
distant.
* * * *
“Code”: When Coronet won the New-
maiket in 1885, the owners, D. M. Robertson and
another, were said to have won £40,000. That
night they “turned it on” for the crowd at the
Newmarket hotel —bought the stock for £7OO, and
said “Let ’em all come.” And, ’strutli, how- did thev

* » * *
“Tote Abstainer”: No more odds-on
favorites for me, thank you. I’ll let ’em run in
future and enjoy seeing them get whacked. For it’s
fair odds against them winning. Take the last
V.R.C. meeting:—
First Day,—V olaroi, 5 to 4 on, beaten bv the out-
sider Colbert, 10 to 1 against, in a field of five
(consternation). Patrobas, 5 to 2 on; won (frantic
cheering).
Third Day.—Woorak, 10 to 9 on, beaten by
Wolaroi, 6 to 1 against (hoots). Patrobas, 2 to 1
on, beaten by Aides, 10 to 1 against (groans and
gnashing of teeth).
The human form may be divine. Equine form is
quite as likely to be devilish.
* * * *
Nothing like a good cross-country race—
sometimes. In Hie Sowden Steeplechase at the Port
Adelaide meeting last week there were 12 starters.
Four of the animals fell. Two others stopped. The
winner arrived 15 lengths or half an hour or some-
thing before the judge sighted the remainder. South
Aus. used to be fairly well-off for cross-country
horses; but it is almost as bare now as the rest.
The racing clubs offer hardly any encouragement;
and “hunting” is nearly as dead in Australia as the
grandfather of last season’s grasshopper. The ground
is so infernally hard. But the sport has made a
desperate struggle to live in Adelaide, and the Hunt
Club still manages to run Hie second-biggest picnic
race-meeting of the year. For a dozen years or so
Paddy Glynn, now one of Hughes’s colleagues (and
how he must chuckle when he thinks of it!), did a
great thing to keep the weekly “hunt” alive; he
wrote for Monday’s Register a charming little half-
column literary essay about the previous week’s
‘.‘meet” ; and the farmers whose land was run over
could read their names between a quotation from
Horace and a reference to Maecenas.
“Collar-Stud”: Rumored that the Ship-
ley stud is in the market, Sol Green having decided
to give up breeding. Sol has had a fair measure of
success with his thoroughbreds, but I don’t suppose
there’s a fortune in the chancey business these times,
though there may be more kudos in it than in
making a book. Sol prefers to take the cash and let
the kudos go.
* * *
“Musketoon”: I’ll nominate Jack Clif-
ford’s pony The Boar for the long-distance-in-the-
shortest-time championship. Some time ago John
wanted to proceed from Forbes (N.S.W.) to Con-
dobolin, and, harnessing The Boar in a light buggy,
started off at 9.30 a.m. He was back at Forbes
at 7.30 the same evening. Distance 130 miles. The
Boar was 20 years old at the time. (Anyway,
that’s the driver’s story. Personally 1 have a sus-
picion the watch stopped somewhere on the trip.)
***■*.
“D.F.”: About the finest fishing ground
on the Australian coast —for line fishing, anyway—is
the submarine platform off the south coast of the
Speck. As this is about the stormiest bit of ocean to
be found anywhere, it is only occasionally that it' can
be fished ; but the fishermen reckon (or used to, for
lately the fish seem to be a bit uncertain) that they
are sure of a good boatload if they can get to work
on the bottom round the rock which Tasman’s Dutch-
man in 1642 called Piedra Blanca, but
which is now known in the vernacular as Peter’s
Bank. It’s the best real trumpeter bank on the coast,
and a great place for
perch, rock-cod and
about 25 other kinds
of fish. According to
Captain J. K. Davis,
of the Aurora, there
are shallow soundings
for about 200 miles
due south from (he
Tasmanian coast,, and
then the bottom * falls
away rather steeply.
Scientists say that
this is a sunken land,
a bit of the old land
bridge that used to
join Tasmania to Ant-
artica ’wav ,back be-
fore the Flood. Bits
of the lantt*still stick
up 20 or 30 niiles off
the coast'. There's the
Mewstone, a bare
rock 440 ft. high, and
the Eddystone, over
200 ft. high, which,
looks for all the world'
‘like a square church-
tower.
VERNON
RANSFORD
(Vic.).
The little ’uns were given a night out at
Sydney Stadium on Saturday, the fly-weight and
bantam championships being danced for. In both
cases the conflict went to the bitter end. A young-
ster named Jack Green got the verdict for flies
against A 1 White; the bantams Williams and
Holmes perpetrated _ a draw. Young Green is only
15, but has already developed an assortment of good
hard punches, the one with the right being particu-
larly emphatic. There is fighting blood in the
family, too, uncle Teddy Green being an old-time
champion.
* «• ■ » *
Herb McCoy’s idea that he could put up
a stronger fight in the welterweight division is
proving to be wrong. Either that or it happens
that the other fellow finds himself stronger still.
Whichever it is Herbert is certainly not doing so
well as a welter as he did as a lightweight. On
Saturday he got another hiding, this time at Mel-
bourne from Harry Stone. The winner had all the
best of it, boxing more skilfully and hitting more
harmfully. As early as the third round Bert was
in serious trouble. The punch that counts came over
in the twelfth clash, and once more McCoy was
carried back to the place from whence he came.
How about taking six months spell in the bush ?
* * * *
Wave Geike and Matty Smith (a country-
man of Woodrow Wilson) contributed the chief item
on the stoush menu at Brisbane on Saturday. Smith
suffered badly from ennui for the first nine rounds,
even Geilce’s continued application of straight lefts
failing to rouse him. At the tenth box-on America
suddenly began to take an interest in Geike, and in
the next round settled that industrious youth for the
evening, using a left to the chin. Smith meets Llew
Edwards next, and will need to take notice a bit
earlier in the proceedings.
* * * *
After a year’s interval for prayer and fast-
ing McGoorty is coming back to the game, and has
an appointment to interview Tommy Uren at Sydney
during Easter. At the time McGoorty was hookin’-
’em, one after another, this meeting would have
looked a bit over the odds; but as Thomas has
grown stronger in the meantime, and Edward has
not been getting either thinner or younger, there
may be an interesting exchange of smite. But the
main point will be in the revelation of how much
McGoorty has benefited by living at peace. Another
match .pending is between Dave Smith and the
winner of a conflict in which Colin Bell and Albert
Lloyd will supply the wallop. This will be for the •
heavyweight championship, though it all sounds like
the rending of the tomb.
Sam Austin, of the N’Yark Police
Gazette, denouncing the suggested scrap between
Darcy and A 1 McCoy as daylight robbery: “Time
and the Australian’s dilatory actions have taken the
edge off the public’s interest in him, and unless he
does something to encourage the idea that he is a
fighter, and came here to fight instead of grafting
from the public in the guise of an actor—God, save
the’mark! nobody will care a hoot in Hades what he
does.” Things evidently got warm in a hurry when
somebody mentioned that Darcy was to get 30,000
dollars for a scrap with a fifth-rater.
Veteran Tommy Warne, who built up a
second century this season (107 not out) for Carlton
against Northcote, has tasted much of the glory
which belongs to Victorian cricket. Many years ago
Tommy lifted a bat in representative matches; nowa-
days he keeps in nick by keeping in order the blades
of grass on Carlton ground. The Warne family, by
the way, is sufficiently numerous to form an Aus-
tralian XI.
Batting for Melbourne against P’ran the
other day, H. Ironmonger lifted the ball clean
into the M.C.C. public stand. This was a big hit,
but not the biggest. Bill Howell, best remem-
bered as an Australian XI bowler, once sent a ball
right out of the M.C.C. ground into Richmond
Park. For years it was the ambition of hefty
smiters to smash the face of the M.C.C. grandstand
clock. Time after time George Bonnor attempted
to win a standing bet from an enthusiast who had
defied him to do the deed. The man who came
nearest to doing it was George Alexander, who landed
the ball in a gutter a few inches below the clock.
A. R. Jenkins banged up liis first century
in N.S.W. premiership cricket on Saturday—los not
out for Sydney against Western Suburbs. Last sea-
son he headed the Sydney sClub's averages with 30.44
per innings, although his highest smite was only 90.
Years before that he was occupying the limelight in
S. Aus. A. .couple of other fine efforts on Saturday
were E. P. Hall’s 81 out of 170 for four wickets on
behalf of Gordon against North Sydney; and consis-
tent W. .T. Folkard’s capture for Balmain of seven
Middle Harbor wickets for 27.
* * * *
“Cleido” : Don Fleming, who went out in
tragic circumstances recently, was a sprinter of class.
But for a slow beginning he would have probably
added the Echuca Gift to his Maryborough anil
Sta Well victories. Bob Drew defeated him at Echuca.
Drew, who had turned 40 at the time, got a bit of
a break on in the first half of the journey, and
managed to reach the worsted with an inch or two
to spare. Competing in that final were J. Godfrey,
also 40, and C. Kjng, very little younger. Some
|Teds. retain their form in a surprising manner. G.
Grenell, 50, recently won a £lOO handicap at
Flemington (Vic.). A. B. Postle’s is also a case in
point. Do sprinters, as a general thing, last longer
at their game than most other devotees of violent
athletics?
* * * »
J. F. Bradley (Olympic) made two fine
sprints from scratch on Saturday in the Cardinal’s
Cup—the biggest open handicap in Sydney amateur
athletics nowadays. He won the first heat in 10sec.,
but in the final just failed to give the flying P.
Tranquille (Western Suburbs) syds. in similar time.
Two years ago Bradley was the Ma State’s best
snrinter; but a breakdown last season put him on
the shelf for repairs. There doesn’t seem to be
much wrong with him now,
* * # *
The old warrior Kismet has worried into
first place among the Sydney sailing boats, and heads
the season’s winners. Thq old champion lias won
comparatively few events, but by consistently appear-
ing among the place-getters both in heats and finals
steadily climbed to the top in the matter of prize-
money. !
* #• * *
C. Crackanthorp, the new junior (under
20) State swimming champion, who churned 100yds.
behind in 60 4-ssec. at Sydney on Saturday, looks
like climbing high. Last season Crackanthorp made
a rare splash in High School championships when he
won I.ooyds. (64 4-ssee.), 440yds., 880yds. and other
trifles, and defeated Keith Kirkland, who finished
second in all but the 50yds. Most of the starters in
Saturday’s junior championship were secretly “tried”
to cover 1 OO.yds. in less than 65sec. —which gives color
to the theory that N. S. Wales is rich in probable
swimming top-notchers.
* * * *
“F.H.E.”: Re “A.J.W.” and the s^lb.
bream. Was in the company of V. B. Simeon, of
Adelaide, when he landed a 51b. 14oz. bream at
Port Noarlunga. He caught it with a small schnap-
per hook attached to copper wire after several un-
successful attempts with bream hook on gut. Used
crayfish for bait.
* * * »
“Phfisht”: Go to, “G.R.K.,” with your
three-pound sprats (B. 8/3/’l7). My friend the
secretary of the Linton (Vic.) Anglers, after an
hour’s thrill, with cobweb tackle, grassed a rainbow
trout weighing 641 b. ; also others up to 4Alb. At
the new Moorabool Reservoir, some miles out of
Ballarat, the trout are biting phenomenally. These
fish were liberated about two years ago as yearlings,
and many up to 41b. and 51b. are being caught.
No fish under 21b. and none but the rainbow species
are amongst the hundreds that have been taken;
and any old bait scores.
* * * #
“Barney”; “Lantana” (B. 8/3/T7), by
nominating a 451 b. jewey as his heaviest, admits
catching undersized fish. That animal of tys would
only be a fair sort of bait for some of the real fish
residing in Tuggerah Lakes. For example, a 96-
pounder was caught there some time back by Cap-
tain Pullen, of North Sydney; and before that:
arrived Walter Dennis landed a member of the same
family which turned the scale at three over the'
century. Anything under Aewt. is left for the cham-
pion catchers from Sydney to annex—and tell tales
about.

. “Foo-lar” : When will misguided gun enthu-
siasts desist from informing the newchum that to
hit a moving object witli a shot-gun you must aim
ahead of the object? For a long time I kept on
missing all my flying shots by aiming ahead of the
game, but I was at last made wise by an old hand
and since then can bag my share.
* • Hr * *
“Foo-lar” ; Has any sport ever blasphemed
because a broken-off portion of the small end of a
Winchester shell stuck in the breech after firing?
Next time it happens keep cool and insert a strong
empty shell and jam jt in as tightly as the lever
will permit. Then withdraw and the vagrant por-
tion of brass will come out adhering to the shell.
Never fails.

PEACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Great racket broke out in the night.
With stirring hair we sat in bed
And listened in increasing fright
To cries that might have waked the dead.
One rang a bell, one beat a tin —
Shrill whistles added to the din.
A baritone Gargantuan
Sang in a fit of frenzy born.
And, somewhere near, a frantic man
Was blowing on a bullock horn,
While many howled, and did not cease.
She whispered: “John, it must be Peace!”
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.
The claim recently made by the New York
Mining and Engineering Journal on
behalf of Bovyser’s Reserve Co., Johannes-
burg, for record shaft-sinking is disputed by
Johnson, of the Ora Banda (W.A.). Bovy-
ser’s sank, with 30 natives working three
shifts, 252 ft. in 31 days; but with white
Australian labor Johnson reckons he went
down 331 ft. in the same time. And he can
produce scores of witnesses to prove it.
“Saint”: There is one kinema man in
Wellington (M.L.) who isn't quite asleep.
When the old Mararoa went ashore at the
entrance to the harbor he was first on the
scene in a launch, and from that unstable
refuge “took” a general view of the stranded
vessel. Then he boarded her from a dinghy
and exposed more film from the deck. A
hurried return to town and some hours’
strenuous work resulted in the film being
shown in the theatres that night.
“Bonnie Mary of Argyle” must be one of
the oldest of popular tenor ballads, for
Carry Nelson, who recently died in Eng-
land at the age of 79, was a daughter of
the composer of the eminently sentimental
ditty. . Carry was the last of the Sisters
Nelson, burlesque actresses who toured the
world in days of yore, and are fondly remem-
bered by a few old identities in Melbourne
and Sydney.
. “F.N.T.” : This is an appeal to Austra-
lians to go and see a picture-play which was
hatched in tire U.S.:—
Hey, folks! Pilly Winks in The Dynasty of Dol-
lars. You’ll sure enjoy it! It’s one of Pill’s best!
Do we rush these American drummers be-
cause of or in spite of such advertising?
Isn’t the Australian vernacular good enough
for Australians?
“Wat” from a new rendezvous,:—
More Australians are seeing Sierra Leone than
ever expected to. The harbor of Free Town,
the capital, is very large and very picturesque, but
the entrance abounds with shoals. Lately an Aus-
tralian ship found one of the mudbanks and lost
her propeller, with the result that her complement
had to be distributed among other vessels in port.
From our ship a few cases of meningitis were put
ashore. I feel sorry for the poor wretches, as it is
terribly hot here, being only eight degrees above
the Equator. If you look at the map you will find
Sierra Leone on the bulge in the Gulf of Guinea,
above the Gold Coast. These Equatorial waters teem
with sharks, and since we have been in the harbor
they have been swimming around the boat all day-
big, wild fellows, anything from 15ft. to 20ft. long.
The berthing facilities are poor, and most of the
landing has to be done in juriks. The town, purelv
native, begins with a bad smell and ends with a
worse. It is the kind of place an Australian might
read about but would never believe existed. The shops
are built of any old rubbish, mostly boxes. The
worse the shop the more extortionate the demands ;
and if the man can’t sell he begs. The police
buildings appear to be the only ones intended to
endure: they are of concrete. The weirdest thing
after the aroma is the music: we had a choice oppor-
tunity of hearing a little of it. because a local chief
was making some sort of procession, surrounded by
a guard of magnificently-proportioned negroes.
“Quidam”: In these days when every jam-
tin has its net weight printed on it. why
shouldn’t tobacco manufacturers be obliged
to show the weight of plugs on the article?
Ihe mystic figures “si” or are cer-
tainly displayed on the end of the boxes, but
any experienced storekeeper invariably keeps
that side away from the public, and when
questioned on the subject, usuallv thinks
there are “about” four plugs to the 'lb. Any-
way. the brand of tobacco that the ordinary
storekeeper still “thinks” averages “about”
four plugs to the lb., as it reallv did when
he used to sell it at Is. sd. per plug, is
packed in cases bearing the symbol “44” on
the end, although the retail price is now Is. 6d.
per plug. Only an expert mathematician can
work out the present price per lb. of some
brands, assuming that, ho can got a private
view of the box, which he generally can't.
It seems anomalous that the purchaser of a
mere luxury like jam should be put wise to
the weight, while he who indulges in a
necessity like tobacco should be kept in
blackest ignorance.
It is emphasised at everv meeting of its
members that the “National” Federation is
“a purely non-party organisation, devoted to
winning the war.” The matter was put much
more honestly by Plain, M.L.A. for Geelong,
when he told the new Middle Park branch
that the aim of the Federation was to get “a
majority behind a Government prepared to”
do something or other. Shorn of all flum-
mery, the purpose of the Federation is to
help Billy Hughes keep his job.
There are signs that the agreement of
amusement managers in Adelaide not to
advertise in the Tlser because it supported
the Amusement Tax Act is wearing thin.
Bonython is an awkward opponent to bump
against, and he hands out slabs of praise to
such shows as make use of his paper. Also
there appear in the ’Tiser sundry ads. for
and notices of shows that are meantime pro-
claiming in Register and Herald that
those papers alone have any authorised
announcements. Last week things got as
far as the start of legal proceedings against
one of the “honorable understanding” lot by
some of the others, witn an appeal to
Injunctions and Mandamuses, and other
fearsome beasts that live in the Supreme
Court. The effect may be to cement the
bonds again, or it may not. At any rate,
as late as the middle of March the “Impor-
tant!” manifesto to beware of all unauthor-
ised ads. was still being put forth by
Royal, Tivoli. Majestic, King’s, West’s,
Wondergraph, Empire and Pav.

Miss Gladys Cooper Explains
Some Beauty Secrets.
The Popular London Star Favors Simple Methods.
I have been asked to give a few simple recipes that I know, either through personal
use or by observation, to be valuable to the toilet, and which are within the reach of the
average woman. In these days of £lOO facial treatments and elaborate and expensive
eautitying processes my suggestions may read like lessons in economy, but they are
. ,< e £ pe ?. y s °„ feuded. They are merely practical suggestions, in which the keynote
* s , , eaectiveness. All the materials or ingredients which I mention are either already
J? an< ? m home or may be readily procured from the chemist. Fortunately Ido not
sutrei from the ailments or troubles enumerated below, but some people who are not so
mtunate have told me their experiences, and with your permission I will set forth some
remedies which they have found to be efficacious.
Home-made Hair Tonic.
My acquaintances say that a good stimulant
to the natural growth of the hair is boranium.
A very simple, effective and safe stimulating
lotion made up by mixing one ounce of bora-
nmm with i pint of bay rum. This may be
applied to the hair roots occasionally with the
finger-tips with good results.
Complexion Renewals.
Complexion experts advise me that a normal,
healthy complexion is constantly renewing it-
self by dropping off tiny flakes of worn-out
tissue, thus revealing the fresh young skin
underneath. They sa.v that when this process
is checked by age, expo-
sure or some of many
causes, the complexion
becomes dull and ugly.
The rational treatment
recommended is to help
the skin perform the
natural functions of
“shedding” worn-out' tis-
sue. For this purpose, I
am informed, there is
nothing so good as pure
mercolized wax, used for
a few nights, just as you
would use a face cream.
It is claimed that it pos-
sesses a special affinity
for the effete scarf-skin,
which it quickly removes
by absorbing it. The face.
I am assured, will soon
look much younger and
prettier under this treat-
ment.
The Curling Iron.
Don’t use a hot iron to
cur] your hair. Some of
my friends make the cun-
ningest sort of curls
wherever they want
them simply by damp-
ing the hair with liquid
silmerine before retiring
at night. When the hair is dry in the morn-
ing it will be softly curly just where you
want it to he. This method is perfectly harm-
less, even beneficial to the hair, and the curls
last a long time. The liquid is quite pleasant
and neither sticky nor greasy.
How to Shampoo.
Most women, I am informed, do not know
how to use stallax properly when shampooing
with it. Unless the hair is naturally very
oily, a stallax shampoo may sometimes leave
it rather dry. But I am told if you will apply
olive oil freely to your hair and scalp just
before shampooing with stallax, the result is
most delightful. The hair will be left clean,
soft, bright and wavy, the olive oil having"
properly balanced the action of this wonderful
hair cleanser.
Is Powder Necessary?
A great many women object to using pow-
der, for various reasons. The following
formula is a good one: Dissolve an ounce of
MISS GLADYS
Photo Wrather & Buy.
COOPER.
) cleminite in four tablespoonfuls of water, or
witch-hazel, and use it as a face lotion,
smoothing the skin with the lingers until it
is dry. This method is perfectly harmless.
I am told that a really beautiful, natural, vel-
vety bloom results and remains for many hours
quite unaffected by the most trying conditions
out of doors or in the ballroom or theatre,
and that it gives a much more natural appear-
ance to some skins than does ordinary powder.
Grey Hair.
I have observed many attempts of many
people to conceal grey hair. Some of these
experiments were amusing, some disastrous,
and some were success-
ful. Personally I believe
I shall let my hair turn
when the appointed time
comes, but if I were
foing to try to evade it,
would give a trial to a
real old “grandmother”
formula that would prob-
ably do the work. This
formula. I am informed,
has been used with de-'
grees of success for many
generations, and consists
merely of two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite
mixed with* three ounces
of bay rum. It is ap-
plied to the grey hair
a few times with a small
sponge, and ladies tell
me it appears to darken
the hair to a natural
shade, not like a dye. hut
gradually and naturally.
Miscellaneous Hints.
A few useful hints from
my friends on aids to
beauty, are as follows:
The eyebrows and lashes
may be made to grow
stronger and darker by
applying mennaline, a
specially delicate pomade, with the finger-tips
at night.
Rouge is often obvious, and many women
therefore prefer to use powdered colliandum,
which gives a most natural tint.
The lips may be prevented from drying and
chapping by touching them with a stick
of prolactum, which also gives them a good
color.
Freckles are most effectively combated with
ammoniated mercury creams, of which jetta-
line is the best.
Note.—This interesting article on beauty
culture in general was written by Miss Gladys
Cooper at the request of -the manufacturers of
Pilenta Soap—the best complexion soap in the
world. On sale by all chemists. [4

"Brig” : I sent a wireless message from a
hospital ship at the rate of lid. per word.
1 paid 10 elevenpences for a reply, and when
it flew hack from the shore I was refunded
4s. 2d. and told that the rate from shore to
ship was (id. The handling either way is
identical, so the difference in the charges is
a glaring inconsistency. A mate also pre-
paid a reply ; but although he handed up lid
a word, nothing was refunded. The ordi-
nary charge otf the Australian coast is 6d.
per word. Surely the Navy, which controls
the land stations, can arrauge that the
returning Australian soldier at least pays no
more than the stay-at-home civilian.
Though Australia cannot get the one
choice flower she wants and has been pro-
mised —Protection—quite a few bouquets, as
Page calls them, are being handed out by
the Win-the-War (you bet!) Government.
The Bananaland sugar-growers have been
handed a lily; the hop-growers are to re-
ceive a-er-daisy; the wheat-growers are—
excused the duty on bags; the Tasmanian
millers are to have half the cost of trans-
porting wheat from the mainland shouldered
by the community-in-general. Of course
there is no bribery in this; it is merely
"assisting the producer in a time- of stress”
—said time of stress occurring this year be-
tween March 3 and Mav 5.

IMPORTANT.
Look to Your Tooth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes,. crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free. 1/3. Free sample posted.
ROCKE, TOMPSITT & CO., Melbourne.

BANANA LAND
Even Black Brother has got into the habit
of blaming it on to the war. Here's a letter
from a Torres Strait Islander to a Thursday
Island pearler:—
Dear Sir Tom, —Just a few line to you I am very
hungery. This war no good. You all white people
you got plenty food to eat we all black people we
hungery all the time. War he make all thing too
dear, please my friend can you give me one bag of
flour please. 1 am very older; I live on small
island. Please sent me some tobocco and match.
When I go my Island you send me one bag flour.
Dear Friend I think I ani going to close my letter
now. Me hungery like hell. When war finish I pay
you for bag flour. —Yours truly friend, Mamba.
‘‘Dry Shave”: Labor does make a damfool
of itself at times! A coastal steamer put in
at Gladstone wharf the other morning at
7.55. A rope was heaved over with the
request that one of the assembled water-
siders make it fast. Not one would stir a
hand, “for any —— steamship com-
pany” till 8 a.m. As a dangerous sea was
running the skipper couldn’t wait alongside
for five minutes, so he had to turn about
and put out to sea and safety. If ever an
invader calls round, let’s hope he doesn’t
do it when Bill Lumper is at smoke-oh.
A resident in one of Brisbane’s newest
suburbs, having written to the local co.uncil
17 times about the state of the alleged street
in front of his house, decided lately to
try another line. Next morning there was
another notice for the small boys of the
neighborhood to throw mud at. Stuck right
in the middle of the read it read: “Persons
found shooting in this swamp will be pros-
ecuted. By order.”
When Bill the Butcher forgot himself, one
of two Queensland brothers hurried off to
England and joined the navy. He was
aboard a destroyer sunk in the North Sea and
was saved by a Norwegian boat. Escaping, he
pushed over to the U.S.A., and from there
worked his passage to Sydney. Meanwhile
his old folks at home hadn’t heard of him
for two years. The night he landed he
went to a show. The same day his brother
arrived at Sydney and went to a show also,
getting in just as the curtain rose. When
the lights llared in the interval the two
brothers were sitting side by side!
“l’ot Jostler” : In Queensland all recruits
are medically examined as soon as they
reach camp. There was quite a big day
recently ; something like 76 came in willing
to strafe Bill. All was bustle, and perspir-
ing sergeants were pushing the men through
the medical hut. A man was patiently wait-
ing near the door of the hut when a s, r-
geant rushed him inside and ordered him to
take off: his shirt. The man tried to get a
few words in, but there was no time for
explanations, and he was hustled before the
M.O. vainly expostulating. The Medical
Officer had him stripped and was examining
him for varicose veins and other things.
Then he stopped and asked him his age.
“Sixty-one,” said the old chap. “Then what
in God’s name are you doing here?” angrily-
cried the M.O. “Isn’t my fault,” said the
sixty-oner. “I been trying to tell somebody
the last two hours that I’m only a visitor
looking for my son who joined yesterday.”
Exit John Memovitcli, the cheerful claim-
ant for a modest £8,000,000 from the Queens-
land Government. Memovitch fob years past
has sent in his bill every year. He claimed to
have discovered the Clermont goldfield some
50 years ago, and reckoned that he was
entitled to the reward paid in those days
for such finds. He brought the amount up to
eight millions by adding compound interest
for a term of about half a century. His
claim, however, had not a great deal to sup-
port it, and when he passed over the line the
other day at the age of 70, he was still
hoping against hope. An Austrian by
birth, he had been a great many years in
Queensland. When Parliament was in ses-
sion Memovitch was a regular attendant, and
Ministers were hard put to it to escape the
interviews which he continually sought.
Years ago Joe Lesina presented a petition
to Parliament on the old chap’s behalf, and
the claim was subsequently investigated. The
petition is in itself a bit of a curio, and
many of the names attached to it prove that
the signatories did not take the old fellow
very seriously. In fact, the mention of some
of the names in a public place would pro-
bably have been followed by arrest for in-
decent language.
Queensland Government pays the manager
of a State pub £450 salary and provides
board and residence for him and his family.
Then, in order to encourage young men to
turn to science, it seeks a Government
Botanist and proposes to load him with
£3OO a year and feed himself.
Queensland’s Under-Secretary for Public
Instruction spoke proudly the other day of a
“linking-up between the University and the
meatworks” which had been accomplished.
This sounds like good news. It probably
means that the Uni. Professor who gets dis-
contented with his pay is to be allowed to
take a turn at butchering, and so improve his
income.
The Northern climate told again last
week, when Edward Wilson, one of Queens-
land's best-known Congregational ministers,
died at Rockhampton aged 78. Born in Ire-
land. Wilson drifted into the Army, and
took part in the suppression of the Indian
Mutiny. In hospital with cholera he suc-
cessfully skirmished with Death; and the
experience decided him to enter the Church.
Bronchial trouble brought him to Australia,
lie served in various parts of Queensland,
and had labored in the vineyard altogether
for 55 years.
WHY HURRY?
MR. BROWN : “I had a queer dream last night, my dear. I thought I saw another
man running off with you."
MRS. BROWN : “And what did you say to him?"
MR. BROWN : “I asked him what he teas running for."
“D.G.S.” : How- is this for record bidding?
At Rockhampton Land Court the other day
a perpetual lease of 39 acres was auctioned.
The upset price was £3B 15s. ; only two men
wanted the block, but in £1 bids the pair
soon sent the offer up to hundreds. The
crowd got impatient and wanted them to
leap in fivers. When £9OO was reached one
threw in the towel, casually remarking to
the other fool “that if he had had the sense
to toss for it he might have had it for half
the price.”
“Van Eck”: When a theatrical show puts
up at Toowoomba Town Hall the manage-
ment always has to take no account of cer-
tain seats. Painted on three of the best in the
hall are the words, “Reserved for the
Alayor.” And it’s Heaven help the indivi-
dual who encroaches on his preserves!
The sight of Alat Reid, one of Labor’s
pioneers in the ’Bo’s, going round Southern
Bananaland organising for the National
Federation, must be gall to the extreme sec-
tion of the Brisbane Trades Hall. - Alat is
one of dozens who have been driven out of
the ranks of Queensland orthodox Labor in
the last 20 years. Expelled AI’s.L.A. alone
in Bananaland number nearly 30.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, Alarch 17, 1917.
Aly Dear Thargomindah,—
A distinguished Russian, Alexander Pop-
oil:, who is entitled to be addressed as “your
Excellency” when he is in the home-land
(Excellencies run about seven for six-
pence in Russia, I believe —or did up till a
few days ago), is the Bear’s new Trade
Commissioner in Australia. His head-
quarters will be Brisbane, and to christen
the venture he lunched a party of focal pop-
guns at the Belle Vue Hotel the other day.
Amongst the guests were Premier and Airs.
Ryan.
Another visitor to Brisbane is Leonard AI.
Sandston, ALA., B.Sc., who is getting round
Australia studying street and road construc-
tion ! That’s what he says, anyhow. He is
a Alaorilander, who, after leaving the Shaky
Isles, spent 10 years in Europe and America
practising engineering.
The State Fisheries business has been set
going under the management of A. A. Gil-
mour, for many years with the Q.AI.E. Co.
A receiving depot, with freezing plant, has
been established on Wynnum Creek, and
from a central market in Brisbane delivery-
carts will serve the suburbs with wholesome
sea-food. The scheme ought to receive a
great welcome.
Mrs. Irving, wife of our new State Com-
mandant, has been,the guest of honor at
morning teas given by the National Council
of Women and the Women’s Mutual Service
Club.
Died ill France, Lieut. J. J. Trickett, son
of the famous oarsman. Lieut. Trickett
saw service in the Boer War, and emerged
wearing a D.C.M. and a S’African medal
with five clasps. Immediately after that
scuffle he became famous in Queensland with
the stranding of the steamer Aramac. He
was in charge of one of the boats, and re-
mained at the tiller for 42 hours without a
spell. Ilis wife was a Miss Cross, of Rock-
hampton. Prior to going after the Huns
Lieut. Trickett was manager of Brisbane
branch of the Imperial Oil Co.
Bruce Mclntosh, well known in and near
Burketown, has risen to captain's rank. He
left here in 1914 as a sergeant, won a com-
mission at Gallipoli, got his second star
after the evacuation, was recently wounded
in France, and is now back in the firing-
line.
Another Bananalander who has been dis-
tinguishing himself is barrister Tom Macleod,
flight-commander. He has just completed
six months’ service in France. Two Long-
reach boys,. Lieuts. Shaw and Donaldson,
also members of the R.F.C., are doing good
work in the same sphere.
, Capt. A. C. Morgan, son of the late
Arthur Morgan, is on his way home on leave
from Egypt. Ilis pretty little wife is wait-
ing for him in Sydney.
Miss Fuller, the artist, of whom Vandorian
gave us a portrait lately, is at present in
Brisbane. An exhibition of her work is on
view at the Turner galleries.
Miss Helen Ilislop, daughter of the Robert
Hislops of this city, is wearing the golden
promise of Sergt. F. J. Dawson, of the
Maoriland Expeditionary Forces. He is a
Wellington man.
T. 11. Slatyer, manager of the Common-
wealth Bank at Rabaul, will arrive in
Queensland at the end of this month. Ilis
marriage to Miss Jean Mackenzie, daughter
of K. Murchison Mackenzie, of the Q. N.
Bank. Maryborough, is to take place soon,
after his arrival.
By the death of Airs. George Harris last
Friday, Brisbane lost one who was a leader
of Society' in the merry bad old days when
John Stevenson, Henry Donkin and Joshua
Peter Bell drove rival four-in-hands, and
Newstead (the Harris mansion) was the
rendezvous of the smartest set. Airs. Harris
reigned amidst maids and servants galore, in
an atmosphere which resulted in her becom-
ing a very wilful and spoiled young woman.
One of the handsome Thorn sisters (the
other two became Airs. Donald Wallace and
Airs. Tom Rome —-the latter’s son will be
remembered as one of the Denman aides),
she married Brisbane’s rich merchant when
she was just IS. Her lovely golden hair
remained almost unfaded to the last. In-
deed, she did what is given to few of her sex.
and lived to be a very pretty old woman.
Of her two daughters the eldest married Tay-
lor, of Bellevue station, and on his death
Lumley Hill ; the younger is .Airs. George
Casey, of Aielbouriie, and a grand-daughter
child of her second son is a young Airs.
Bullivant. of Victoria.
Air. and Airs. Parry Okeden have gone to
Sydney to await the arrival of their daughter
and her husband, Roger Cope, who have
been in France doing splendid and little-
advertised work in a military hospital where
many of the tragic Belgian cases were
treated.
I was telling you last week about the
Telegraph’s attitude to the committee of
the Repatriation - scheme, and between you
and me we got the trouble mixed. The Tele-
graph’s representative is not to be excluded
from all meetings of the Repatriation Com-
mittee ; what happened was that the Tele-
graph’s representative on the publicity
sub-committee was requested to withdraw
from the Presence. Now, let’s get on with
the war again.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

Don’t Have Grey Hair.
A simple, old-fashioned, home-made recipe will
make the greyness disappear.
Grey hair is often a serious handicap to
both men and women while still in the prime
of life. Hair dyes are not advisable because
they are always obvious, inconvenient and
often downright injurious. Few people know
that a very simple formula, which is easily
made up at home, will turn the hair back to
a. natural color in a perfectly harmless
manner. You have only to get two ounces
of tammalite concentrate from your chemist
and mix it with three'ounces of bay rum to
prove this. Apply this simple and harmless
lotion for a few nights to the hair with a
small sponge and the greyness will gradually
disappear. The lotion is neither sticky nor
greasy, and has been proved over and over
again for generations past bv those in posses-
sion of the formula.- —Advt.

Razor.
News comes from London of the death of
Frank Desprez, who in his way has caused
as much suffering as W. Holienzollern, for
he was the author of “Lasca,” which has been
hissed, sobbed and shouted by elocutionary
fiends wherever the English language is
mangled. In his early days Desprez was a
cowboy in Texas, and on passing to London
was mixed up in theatrical affairs. He
wrote many curtain-raisers and a farce,
“Happy Hampstead,” which was set to music
by D’Oyly Carte, who composed under the
nom-de-t une of “Mark Lynn.” Afterwards
Desprez was Carte’s secretary, and later
became editor of The Era, London’s old-
established grease-paint publication.
“The Moth”: What darkness would lie
over the. land but for our glorious cable ser-
vice! The papers that feature Northcliffe
news the other day solemnly printed this
message: “The Times has discontinued the
issue of its contents bill.” How should we,
as loyal subjects of George IV., refceive
news of this kind? Should we moan and
go into sackcloth and cinders? Should we
be joyful and slay the fatted calf, or should
we offer up prayers that someone will slay
the fatted ass at the other end of the cable?
If the Times should e\ er change the- com-
plexion of its front door I look forward to
a happy couple of columns.
“Lou”: “Bill” Smith, organiser for the
Painters’ Union, has been selected to contest
Henty, the Vie. constituency that Boyd has
represented almost since it was put on the
Federal map. Labor’s hope last time was
A. J. Andrews, secretary of East Melbourne
P.L.C.; but Boyd outed him by about 900.
Still, that was a light poll, and Smith, a
young and “scientific” laborite and one of
the best debaters in Melbourne Trades Hall,
will be much closer up to the sitting mem-
ber. than that —if the sitting member isn’t
entirely overcome on the journey by the
fumes arising from his party.
“Rio”: “Returned Billjim” is wrong in
attributing the restrictions on army nurses
in uniform -to the petty tyranny of snobbish
staff-officers (B. 1/3/T7). The responsible
parties are the Director-General of Medical
Services (in ordinary times a suburban doc-
tor) and the Principal Matron, who is her-
self a nurse. _ Like the restrictions in civil
life, the war-time rules for nurses have been
deemed necessary because of the very few
who have been indiscreet, and the number of
Billjims, returned and otherwise, who cannot
see. a nursing sister in the company of a
patient on leave without letting filthy tongues
run loose.
The Institution of Civil Engineers,
London, numbered the Kaiser among its
members; but by a special vote since the out-
break of war he was removed from the list.
Yet no man, by reason of railways, bridges,
cities and ships destroyed, has done more to
provide members of the profession with
opportunities for work in the future. Some
people are ungrateful beggars. Lord Kit-
chener was another .member whom the war
has been responsible for removing.
Capt. de Beauchamp and Lieut. Daneourt,
who recently bombed Essen, rode from
France to the scene of the explosion on the
latest type of So.pwitli biplane, which the
Australian Harry Hawker helped to design.
Hawker is now an important member of the
firm. _ Jn fact, Hawker, T. O. M.
Sopwitli (head of the business) and one
Sigrist, the works manager, are responsible
for all the new developments in the Sopwith
flier. The details of the famous “tabloid”
machine, which created a sensation on its
first appearance in 1913, were worked out
by the trio with a lump of chalk on the
factory floor.
“L.0.W.”: The N. S. Wales Education
Department may be a bit over-canny in
some ways, as “Alfonso” suggests (B.
22/2/’l7). But it is entitled to praise for
endeavoring to make Australian youngsters
take an interest in beautifying their schools
and their country by tree-planting and
similar improvements. In the opinion of
ancient long-bearded cockies such things are
fads, as “Alfonso” indicates. But, then,
anything at all, public or private, which
institutes any departure from antediluvian
ideas and methods is a fad. Whatever
faults exist, the fact remains that the
State schools of to-day make youngsters far
more interested and enlightened in regard
to Australia than did the educational sys-
tems of 30 or 40 years ago, when a large
percentage of the sneering pedagogues had
only recently arrived from the Cold Country,
bringing their imported ideas and ideals
with them. In my own youth it was
instilled into me that everything Australian,
soil, climate, plants, animals and people,
were inferior, and it took many years before
the hard logic of facts and experience
slaughtered that humiliating belief. We
learnt then all about the plants and animals
and immense wealth-producing resources of
every country on the globe, always barring
our own. The animals and birds of our
own land were merely monstrosities; our
fish were inferior to the fish of other seas.
Ilence the rabbit and the fox in place of
the marsupial; the wretched carp instead
of the Murray cod and the blackfish. Inci-
dentally, school in those days was a Sahara
that every well-conditioned -boy hated.
Nowadays my kids go off to school quite will-
ingly, have already learned more about their
native land than I did in all my years of
school and take an intelligent interest in the
birds and plants and insects of Australia.
In fact, they are being educated as Austra-
lians by an Australian teacher, and the
whole atmosphere, tends to make them regard
Australia as their country. In my day,
we were taught by alien teachers to regard
ourselves as foreign born Englishmen, con-
-demned by harsh circumstances to reside in
a country totally unworthy of consideration.
When facetious old cockies hang their heads
over the school fence and sneer at “fads” they
never knew in their schooldays, it would be
as well, then, to ask them whether, fads
and, all chucked in, they notice that kids
don t now appear to be as sorry about going"
to school as they used to be in the good old
fadless days.

The Inky Way.
“F.N.T.”: Dr. Thomas Patlirick Mcln-
erney, a mellow old lawyer who acts as
warden to the Melbourne 'Varsity Senate in
his spare time, is one of the leading lights of
the Australian Literature Society. The
other night, over a discussion of Furphy’s
poems, the little company side-tracked the
poems for a time in order to discuss The
Bulletin ; whereupon Mclnerney arose,
shook out his flowing back hair and declared
that The Bulletin, “caustic and hyper-
critical of everything that was noble
and inspiring”—loafing, sweating, political
trickery, cow slavery, bookmaking, and
religious cant and hypocrisy, for example
—“was injuring the literature of Aus-
tralia.” Newspaper-man Sergeant Alfred
Morris, back on furlough from hot times in
the melting-pot of the new war-literature,
hacked up by poet-critic Bernard O’Dowd,
suggested that so far The Bulletin hadn’t
been caustic or hypercritical of the Austra-
lian soldier’s noble and inspiring war-eftorts.
either in literature or oh the stricken field.
But Thomas Patrick simply refused to aha'n-
don Ids gloomy view of this country’s
literary future.
“Peter”: Archie Whyte has given up his
perch in the iuter-State press gallery at
Federal Parliament House for a post as
organiser of the National Federation. For
over 10 years Whyte had poured the per-
siflage of Parliament into the columns of the
S. M. lleralu, and struggled nobly to make
a success, of the tiresome job. He is a
bright young man who in his new job will
be something like a grasshopper in a
cemetery.
* * * *
Listen to the Melbourne Age on the sub-
ject of the rats that have been eating the
stacked wheat at Williamstown :
These creatures, whose organising genius would
surely put the boomed ant to the blush —
And then Tiie Bulletin knocked off work
and had a vision of a boomed ant blushing.
* * * *
“Melb” introduces the.family:—
George Collins Levev is alive and well in England,
at the age of 86. He was on the front Opposition
bench of the Victorian Assembly half a century ago,
alongside Nat Levi, and promised to become a Min-
ister. Then he missed an election for Normanby
and went out. Levey had good writing power, but
could not get along with the Argus, so Davy Symc
took him over—as he did also Marcus Clarke,
“Vagabond” Julian Thomas and James Smith in
later years—-and found him a highly useful man.
Afterwards he was a promoter of exhibitions. Of
his three brothers, William, Oliver and J.A., the
first-named edited Melbourne Bell's Life and wrote
therein as “Voltigcur,” with Chapman, afterwards
of the Weekly Times and Australasian, as “Orange
Blossom.” Oliver is still in business in Melbourne
as printers’ broker.
* * * *
“Melb”: Cole, of the Book Arcade, once
let me into his method of producing a book.
In his studio adjoining the store in Mel-
bourne he had little boxes all round the
walls, on the plan of the private-box depart-
ment in the post office. Each receptacle
was labelled, and' whenever Cole got an idea
he wrote it down and popped it away. Thus
all his books growed, like Topsy, each com-
ing to light as the deposits of copy war-
ranted. There must be the makings of a
hundred books in the room yet. It is, by
the way, exactly the system on which Sardou
built Up his plays.
* * * *
Walter Cummins, who has been represent-
ing the Hobart Mercury in the A.J.A. Arbi-
tration Court case, and is C, E. Davies’s
right-hand man in running that organ, hoed
a pretty hard row in working up to his job.
He started as a boy in the office, then took
on the round of canvasser and collector in
the country, and came back into the shop
later. He plugged away at exams., and
made himself a qualified accountant, and
when a chance came he wasted no time.
“Q.C.”: The staff of the Hobart Daily
Post, which contains the speediest quick-
change artists on the Inky Way in Aus-
tralia, has had another shake-up. Ex-editor
Dwyer Gray is now sub-editor after a tour
round the reporting staff. Roger Kearney
has found the baton in his knapsack, and is
taking his first turn as chief of staff vice
J. D. Donolioe, the star mover, who is a
staffsman again. It isn’t a new job for
Donohoe; there aren’t any new jobs left on
the paper as far as he is concerned.
* ¥r ■X’ *
“Hamer”: Regarding that apt heading
competition, where stands the S. M.
Herald’s caption regarding the lightning
change by which Hughes landed Earle in the
Senate? The heading was simply “Senator
Ready.”
“ J.F.11.”: A lot of people suppose that
Captain Bairnsfather is the most striking
and original pictorial humorist evolved by
the war. But London Times, in reviewing
a collection of his studies of soldier life,
slates them unmercifully. “He standardises,
almost idolises, a degraded type of face. We
protest against so cruel a caricature.”
Nothing so quickly lowers the morale of an
army as slovenliness ; yet here unquestionably
is an army officei’ who invariably depicts, his
men as the very type which the army is
anxious to suppress. Incidentally the Times
gives a wipe to Rudyard Kipling as the liter-
ary inspirer of the Bairnsfather pictures, the
man who has led the public to believe that
“no soldier is a real soldier unless he uses
plenty of the worst language.”
A GOOD PLACE FOR BUSINESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Don held the paper, and he read
Of pillage, wreck and fire,
What Mr. Woodrow Wilson said,
And troubles yet more dire,
Of Krupps’ infernal new machines
And doings of the submarines.
And then he read of men who froze
At an Antarctic goal.
“By gum !” said Dick. “One more of those
Excursions to the Pole!
They’re goin’ all the, bloomin’ year.
I wisht 1 ran the pub down there.”
VICTORIA.

The Ballad of a Queen’s
Laughter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FAINT grew the sound of weeping down
the night;
The spent fires flamed and fell;
The Persian tents in order glimmered white ;
The dead were sleeping well.
A spearman moaned upon his couch of grass ;
The fitful lantern swung.
We heard the sentry’s clanging armor pass ;
The moon was frail and young.
But yesterday the thronging city prest
Its burden on my heart,
And with a sword of custom held the west
And glowing east apart.
I, of the west, a slave to do their will;
You, of the east, a queen—
loved and trembled at the gulf that still
Yawned out hot souls between.
The foeman hammered at the northern gate.
His javelins of fire
Smoked through the skies with trailing
sparks of hate
To light his checked desire.
The walls were strong. The princes, in their
pride,
Laughed in their towers too well,
Till in their sight the sudden breach grew
wide
And the first rampart fell.
The flash of steel, the murder-reddened
street,
The roar of wind-borne flame:
The city crumbled at the Persian’s feet —
A wailing without name.
In the red ruin I arose a man.
You, proud and undismayed,
Red splashes on your jewelled sandals, ran
To shelter of my blade.
I tore a Persian helmet from the dead.
I grasped a Persian spear.
I was as they. Their shouting cohorts fled
Down lanes of death and fear.
The fiery sun dropt red behind the smoke.
I bore you from the place.
A clear bird-laughter from your warm lips
broke;
I saw your shining face.
White tents that glimmered in a ghostly light
Under a frail, young moon,
We left three leagues behind that happy
night.
The yellow dawn came soon.
They broke your jewel caskets open wide.
Your pots of rose and nard
Make sweetness somewhere for a Medean
bride
Whose lord is battle-scarred.
Your beds, with gold and silver lustres hung,
The fierce flames burnt at will;
Yet are your brown arms clinging, round
and young,
And you are laughing still.
A barley-cake upon a glowing coal
Is all your banquet spread ;
You share your chamber with the camel’s
foal,
With stars for. lamps o’erhead.
More than the spicy wine Darius sips
In desert wells you find,
With my hot kisses on your happy lips,
And all your wise heart blind.
A queen....a slave! But I was born so
free
I chose you from a throne,
And lifted you to desert poverty—
My queen alone!
n.s.w.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
Fares, Please
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was a very wonderful thing for
Madame Martinetta —the yreat Mad-
ame Martinetta —to be on a common,
dusty tram at all. Usually one of her
fleet of motors whirled her w-herever
she wanted to go. But the best car had
broken down hopelessly to-day, and the
weather being wet, no cabs or taxis handy
and a tram approaching, she had said to
her faithful secretary, “Let’s go on this
tram, Mark—it will be such fun!” So
there they were on the funny old tram,
Madame in her fine clothes laughing and
looking about her like a happy child on a
new adventure, and Mark with the two
pennies all ready.
They had travelled a couple of blocks
when the conductor appeared. Swinging
himself along the narrow footboard he threw
back the glass doors of their compartment
with a crash. “Fares, please!” he intoned
in a full, rich voice.
Madame jumped on the dusty seat and
impulsively shot out one hand,' arresting
Mark’s fare-arm.
“Fares, please!” boomed the impatient
conductor to the roaring accompaniment of
the wheels; and Mark delivered the money
and got the two tickets.
Madame Martinetta, ablaze with excite-
ment. took h Id of Mark’s sleeve and shook
it. “Did you notice that man’s voice?”
“Yes, I did. It’s above the average.”
The faint praise nettled the great singer.
“Above the average, Mark! Why, I should
think it was! Mark, that man has a per-
fectly wonderful voice. Did you notice
the exquisite pathos he put into those two
commonplace words? Why, I believe he
could make me cry by repeating them over
and over again !” She turned off her rhap-
sodies smartly and became the brisk busi-
ness woman again.- “Mark, go and tell
that man I want to see him at once.”
Mark had been well trained. In two
minutes he had the mystified conductor on
the. footboard outside the compartment lis-
tening with open mouth to the illustrious
lady passenger.
“I am Madame Martinetta —you know of
me, don’t you ? I thought so! Well, I just
noticed how beautifully you called out
‘Fares, please!’ Yopr voice charmed me.
Do you sing?”
“Not as a rule, Mum,” stammered the
honest conductor. “Used to sing a bit before
I was married, but I gave it up.”
“What a pity! Would you mind singing
‘Fares, please!’—just as you usually do it,
you know?”
But the conductor blushed. “Ah, I
couldn’t do that. Missus.”
“ ‘Madame,’ if you please, conductor.”
“Sorry, Madame. No, I’m ’fraid I
couldn’t do it right off like that —not when
you ask me, y’ know.”
She nodded “I under-
stand—you feel nervous. Well, so should
I. But I want you to come and have your
voice tried at my house, if you will. This
is my secretary, Mr. Mark Cleff. He will
give you my address. What time could you
come ?”
The delighted and flustered man thought
for a minute. “Well, Missus —Madame, I
mean ! —how about Wednesday morning?”
Madame turned to her guide. “Have I
anything on Wednesday?”
‘ The Tasmanian girl who wauts to g-'t
your opinion.”
“That won’t take long. Yes”—to the
conductor—“say Wednesday at 10. You’ll
be there?”
“Yes, Madame—and thank you !”
“Good-bye for the present,” said Madame,
extending a gloved hand. As he took it she
said quizzically, “And what is your name?”
“Beg pardon, Madame, Fendlen’s my
name —Richard Fendlen.”
She thanked him with a nod and said
over her shoulder, “Take a note of Mr.
Fendlen’s address, Mark, and tell him where
the house is.”
“Very good,” said the obedient Mark, and
promptly fixed it.
When the tram reached Pitt-street a well-
known violinist noticed Madame and her
secretary alight and nod good-bye to the
conductor, who raised his cap in return.
At the city terminus the motorman said
casually to his work-mate: “That was
Madame Martinetta, wasn’t it, Dick?”
“Yes,” said Dick —almost as casually.
“What did she want?”
Dick was nearly bursting with the good
news. But he was quite calm outwardly
as he answered: “Oh, just an address or
two. Bill. Seems a nice friendly sort.”
Then they started on the return trip.
* * * #
Conductor Fendlen in his ordinary clothes
thought the big music-room at Madame Mar-
tinetta’s a bit bare and empty as he waited
on the appointed morning. Presently the
ever-smiling Cleff came bustling in. “Good
morning, Mr. Fendlen, I’m so glad you’re
on time. When you know Madame as well
as I do you’ll realise how much punctuality
pleases her. There, I think I hear her com-
ing now.” And Madame herself entered
briskly, looking very trim and fresh in the
sort of frock that most ladies only wear on
company afternoons.
She nodded brightly and took a step
towards the piano. “Feel like singing this
morning?”
“Oh, yes, I think so.” Fendlen ner-
vously produced a roll of old songs which
she accepted aud laid on the piano. “Very
well, then, we’ll try something over now.”
The great lady seated herself daintily on
the piano-stool and struck a few rich chords
that made the music-room ring. Then, half
turning round, she shook a warning finger
with three flashing rings on it at the con-
ductor. “Now, I want you to stand over
there and sing ‘Fares, please!’ just as you
do when you’re on the car. Now, try it
with me!” (More chords.) “There’s your
note, see!” (Note struck with decision.)
“Now —ready!”
Fendlen braced his shoulders, struck a
smoke-concert attitude and sang through his
nose with a miserable whining intonation,
“Fares, please!”
Madame lifted her white hands off the
ivory keyboard as though it were hot and
waved them distractedly in the air, at the
same time making a grimace and shaking
her head. It was an impressive performance
and thoroughly startled the conductor. “Oh,
no, no, no! That isn’t a hit like it! You
didn’t say it like that on the tram, you
know! You said it like this!” The plump
singer hopped off the piano-stool, placed her
left hand lightly on her chest, flung out the
right with all its diamond rings, and, with
the air of a gipsy chief demanding ransom,
shrilled in a peerless soprano, “Fares,
please!” It was such a delicious grand-
opera burlesque of real tram life that
Fendlen .had to throw’ back his head and
.roar with laughter. But neither Madame
nor Cleff seemed to be aware that a joke
had been made.
“I want you to do it like that!” said
Madame hurriedly, and resumed her seat on
the stool. “JSioio, please, Mr. Fendlen!”
Fendlen came to attention rather shame-
facedly. Madame struck the chord and he
tried again, “Fares, please !”• —a dreadful
monotonous drawl this time.
Madame whirled round and faced him.
She looked really severe this time and the
faithful Cleff fidgeted unhappily in the back-
ground. “You’re not paying attention!”
shrilled Madame: “that was hopelessly
wrong! You must give your wdiole mind to
it this time. Imagine you are on the tram
and* that the wheels are making the noise
they do. The passengers are talking, so
you have to sing out to draw their attention.
You throw open the doors wdth a crash so,
and cry out so! (Crashing bass chord—
‘Fares, please!') Now 7 , I’m sure you can
get it right if you try; Forget that you’re
in this room ! Try it again!” They' tried
again and Fendlen failed just as brilliantly.
When he saw her signals of distress lie
sought to excuse himself. “I’m very sorry,
Madame, but I can’t do it here —really. I'm
not wdiat you might call a regular singer”—
“Oh, but you will be if you try ! Why,
man, you’ve got a hea-utiful voice, only you
don’t know how to use it. Now we’ll let
the ‘Fares, please!’ go for the present and
try you with one of the songs. Which do
you consider your best?”
“Well. tK’ wife says I’m pretty good in
‘Queen of the Earth,’ Madame.”

“AA r eU, if you asked him, Madame” .

Fendlen stood by, not knowing quite what

AA T hen he had gathered up his music and
