had never heard anything better than
Fcndlen’s declamation in any of the grand-
opera houses of the world, and Mark agreed
with her that it was superb—simply superb:
* * * *
The next act happened one working morn-
ing on a crowded tram. Fendlen opened a
compartment door, . intoned “Fares,
please!”—and the next instant found
Aladame applauding frantically with gloved
hands. Opposite her was the smiling Mark
nodding his approval, and next door a stout
man with an enormous bass voice sat bel-
lowing his admiration. Madame patted the
amazed conductor on the shoulder. “Now,
that’s the way you should sing! You see
you can do it easily when you’re not try-
ing !”
“I congratulate you!” bellowed the
strange stout gentleman. “Y'ou have the
magnificent voice!”
“Now, Boleri —-will you teach him?”
“Teach, Madame! —for nothing I will
teach that great singer—for nothing at all!
Ah-h, my God, I haf heard Foluzzi at Milan
and Pizarro at Paris, but never anything
like that!” The distinguished basso rose to
his feet, flung out his arms and thundered
“Fwires, pleas a!” The non-artistic passen-
gers shrieked with joy and the conductor
escaped to the other car to hide his blushes.
But the basso wasn’t abashed. On the
contrary, he flung out his arms and made
a scale of it—“Faires. pleasa! Faires,
pleasa! Faires, pleasa!”
“Bravo!” cried Madame and Mark
together—and at that instant the ticket
inspector opened the door, and, holding the
trip slip before him, uttered bis warning
cry—“ Show tickets, please !”
“ Faires , pleasa! — faires, pleasa!” thun-
dered Boleri, unconscious of the Inspector’s
presence.
“Show tickets, please!” repeated the
official, frowning savagely.
Boleri heard him this time. “It is a
rival!” he_remarked, and sat down. “I have
no ticket." lie added with a shrug. Then as
be noted the inspector’s stern official air:
“AVe have no silly tickets here at all. You
see, we have just been discovering a great
singer, my friend —he is the young man who
sells the tickets so beautifully. Will you
ask him to come back and sing for us again?
I am afraid we have driven him away. But
he will get over his bashfulness in time—we
singers always do, eh?”
The inspector was inclined to make a fuss
about finding a compartment in the middle
of a tram without tickets; but Madame’s
card and a smile or two fixed that. And
Fendlen’s career had started !
The singing conductor is still on the Syd-
ney trams, singing endless grand opera for
the heedless public. But Boleri has him in
hand, and presently Ivennerley Rumford and
a few more of them will find a new star
blazing and roaring before them.'
Madame takes at least one trip per week
on Fendlen’s tram just to hear him sing, and
when in a thoughtful mood her worshippers
are puzzled by hearing her hum a curious
yet familiar cry. Some of the more earnest
students have searched for it in the grand-
opera scores. But nobody lias found it yet.
KODAK.
The Crystal Gazer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MISS BREAKPEACE was breathless
with excitement, as she stood, the
centre of attraction, in the group
of women on the landing.
“It just seemed Fate. There I was stand-
ing looking at the meat in the new butcher’s
window when .she came along—a decent-
looking woman in black, a bit awkward on
her feet through bein’ so stout. I looked at
her, and she looked at me, and then we both
looked at the meat. And just as I made up
my mind to give him the order for Sunday’s
dinner, she spoke. ‘I think,’ she says, ‘that’s
a real juicy bit of steak, don’t you, dear?’
AVell, bein’ appealed to, I looked at the bit
of steak she pointed out, and then went to
the side window to get it closer. ‘What
character! AA r hat thoughtfulness!’ she says
in a deep voice, lookin’ at me hard. ‘My
dear, it’s women like you makes the British
Hempire what it is.’ I looked up quick, and
knew from the far-away gaze in her eyes she
was psychic.”
“What?” interjected Aster the housemaid.
“Psychic ! Clairvoyant,” Miss Breakpeace
stooped to explain,
“Oh!” said Aster, and sank into impressed
silence.
“So then,” Miss Breakpeace went on, “we
got talking, an’ she showed me her crystal
all wrapped up in a black-velvet cloth —silk
velvet it was, must ha’ cost seven-eleven a
yard, sale price. It come from the United
States, and it cost her £SO. She told me she
never has had a lonely or down-hearted
moment since she got it. One night a year
ago she looked into it and saw her husband
being laid out, and, sure enough, three
months later he was dead.”
Respiratory organs could stand the helter-
skelter strain no longer. Miss Breakpeace
gasped, choked, and Miss Sommer leaped at
the pause to ask the • burning question:
“Where does she live?”
Miss Breakpeace whispered mysteriously :
“She’s cornin’ here this evening to see me on
her way to one of the swellest houses at
Darling Point, and if any of you want your
fortunes told, now’s your chance! She’s real
good. She told me wonderful things she
seen in the crystal. The time of the Emden
she got an urgent wire: ‘Come and bring
your crystal. Five-guinea fee.’ And that
telegram was signed by someone in a high
place.”
Miss Breakpeace let her gaze sweep Syd-
ney Harbor till it paused significantly in the
direction of Admiralty House.
“An’ when she got there she found three
men with maps spread out, and two Govern-
ment reporters to take down every word that
fell from her lips. Well, of course, bein’ in
a trance, she doesn’t know what she told
them, but as she come out’of the trance she
heard a short, dark man in a naval uniform
say, sharp: ‘Three wireless messages in
quick succession to the ’ Then he seen
her looking and dropped his voice. But,”
Miss Breakpeace became shrill with triumph,
“the next day the Sydney caught the
Emden.”
A gasp and a shiver of excitement ran
through her listeners.
“Five bob and ’alf a crown!” reflected
Aster, a rosy-cheeked, pincushiony-figured
young person. “I’ll have the crystal,” she
decided. “I’m ’avin’ me weddin’-dress made
to marry ’Erb, but it’s better to spend five
bob than p’raps marry the wrong man.”
"I'd better have the crystal, too,” mused
Miss Sommer, the soulful-eyed little person
who hasn’t heard from her soldier-boy for
three months-—“because if Chari has been
killed, there’s no points in missing my chance
with Jack Green.”
The rest of us, having only unimportant
matters such as “rises” and tickets in
Tatt.’s to occupy our minds, were for the
two-and-sixpenny spasm.
After a hasty tea —“on the wing,” as
Aster expressed it—we waited, in a state
of _ almost boiling-over excitement, the
arrival of the. seer. Miss Breakpeace was
the first to consult the oracle. She came
out looking tremendously important. “AVon-
derful! AVonderful!” she whispered hoarsely,
and then gazed into the fire with a look of
beatific reflection. The young-old girl with
the long upper lip, who has been for months
determinedly keeping company with a young
gentleman in “the bank,” went next.' She
swept out with a triumphant air, and I saw
her measuring the third finger of her left
hand with the stem of a forget-me-not pulled
from the posy in her hat.
Miss Sommer emerged with red eyes.
“Poor old Chari,” she whimpered. “She saw
him lying dead with my photo pressed to his
lips. Heavens, is that the right time! I’ll
have to run—Jack Green has been waiting
twenty minutes already!”
Aster waylaid me as I went in. She
looked perplexed. “I don’ know what to do.
She says if I don’t marry ’Erb this time
I’ll never marry ’im. She’s good, though—
she told me that ’Erb gets sudden rises, an'
that’s true, ’im bein’ a lift-man. But she
didn’t even mention the young gentleman
who brings the coals, although lie’s been
most pressing in his attentions.” (So he
bad. Aster. Many a time have I seen finger-
prints on the waist of your print frocks!)
AA T ith a fluttering heart I entered, to find
the Sibyl disguised as an excessively fat,
greasy-looking female. “Shuffle an’ cut with
the left ’and,” she whispered, thrusting a
pack of microbe-teeming playing-cards to-
wards me. One of them fell to the floor. I
picked it up. “Thank you, me dear —I’ve
got me corsets on, an’ that cooks me for
stoopin’.”
She moistened her thumb and forefinger,
spread the cards in a mystic maze and read
for me the following fortune :
“AYho’s the man goes in an’ out a big
buildin’? You work surrounded by men.
A pen—some man you know earns ’is living
with a pen! Men’s your frien’s, not women,
me dear. A fair woman cuts up to you
with jealousy. Think her a frien’ you do.
but she. cuts up with knavery. A dark
woman across the water faces a weddin’
ring....ls she any relation? You’ll get a
letter concerning the death of an old man—
leaves you money, he does. One, two, three,
four, five, six, seven”—poking at the dirty
cards with an equally dirty finger—“yes.
the seventh card’s the ace of spades”—in a
hollow voice—“reversed. No gettin’ away
from that —a death. Leaves you money, ’e
does. A fair man ’ere ’olds your fate. AA T ho’s
Bill? His thoughts are on you. That fair
woman, almost red she is, a real diamond,
she comes up again with jealousy. Makes
mischief, she does. One, two. three, four,
five, six, seven —spades all round: a bad
hinfluence, she is. Don’t worry tryin’ to
think who she is now. Probably in the
middle o’ the night you’ll wake up, and it’ll
come to you in a flash. A’marriage settle-
ment for you. an’ five children—the youngest
will be a genius, a musician. I see ’im
playin’ before crowned ,’eads —A Royal
Remand, as they say. You’ve got a good
hand, my dear—perfectly formed. Puts me
in mind o’ Clara Butt’s. You’ve a wonder-
ful life before you. Two and sixpence, dear,
thank you.”
That was six months ago. Yesterday
when I was reading the paper I saw
a returned soldier named Charles Smith
pleaded guilty to a charge of brutal assault
on a civilian named Jack Green. Asked if
he had anything to say in defence, the
accused (a heavily-built man with a scowl)
howled with biting scorn: “’E wouldn’t
fight. ’E stayed at ’ome and stole me girl—
the cow !”
NORA McAULIFEE.
